Explicit Divorce is One Revocable Pronouncement
Proof and ruling that explicit divorce is one revocable pronouncement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for explicit (sarih) divorce and explain the Quranic evidence that it is one revocable pronouncement, regardless of intention.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce (sarih) always counts as one revocable divorce; the husband's intention to increase it is ineffective.

In Hanafi law, the wording of divorce matters. When a husband uses explicit language like 'you are divorced', the divorce takes effect as one revocable pronouncement — even if he intended to give three. This lesson examines the Quranic basis for this rule and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pronouncing sarih divorce. The ruling: it falls as one revocable divorce.
2
Locate the dalil
The proofs are Quran 2:228 and 2:229.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'husbands' (bu'ulah) in 2:228 refers to those who have pronounced explicit divorce, showing they are still husbands during iddah, so it is revocable. 2:229 'divorce is twice' indicates each pronouncement is one and the husband has the right to take back.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) might consider intention to affect the number. The Hanafi position is based on the apparent meaning of the texts.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The clear Quranic text is taken at face value; the husband's intention does not override the explicit wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وتقع واحدة رجعية وإن نوى الأكثر أو الإيانة أو لم ينو شيئاً) بيان لأحكام الصريح وهي ثلاثة : الأول وقوع الرجعي به ولا تصح نية الإبانة لقوله تعالى (وبعولتهن أحق بردهن) [البقرة: 228] بعد صريح طلاقه المفاد بقوله تعالى (والمطلقات يتربصن) [البقرة: 228] فعلم أن الصريح يستعقبها للإجماع على أن المراد بالبعولة في الآية المطلقون صريحاً، حقيقة كان أو مجازاً، غير متوقف على إثبات كون المطلق طلاقاً رجعياً بعلاً حقيقة، ويدل عليه أيضاً قوله تعالى (الطلاق مرتان فإمساك بمعروف أو تسريح بإحسان) [البقرة: 229]
His saying: (and one revocable divorce occurs, whether he intended more or irrevocability or intended nothing) is an explanation of the rulings of explicit (sarih) divorce, which are three: the first is that revocable divorce occurs by it, and the intention of irrevocability is not valid due to His saying (and their husbands have more right to take them back) [2:228] after the explicit divorce indicated by His saying (and divorced women shall wait) [2:228]. So it is known that explicit divorce entails revocability because of the consensus that what is meant by 'husbands' in the verse are those who have pronounced explicit divorce, whether literally or metaphorically, not dependent on proving that the divorcer being a husband literally for revocable divorce. And it is also indicated by His saying (Divorce is twice; then retention with honor or release with kindness) [2:229].
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of explicit (sarih) divorce: it always results in one revocable (raj'i) divorce, regardless of the husband's intention. The proof is from Surah al-Baqarah: After mentioning divorced women ('mutallaqat') in verse 228, Allah says 'their husbands have more right to take them back'. The word 'husbands' (bu'ulah) refers to men who have pronounced explicit divorce, because they are still considered husbands during the waiting period. This shows that explicit divorce is revocable. Additionally, verse 229 ('Divorce is twice') implies that the first two divorces are revocable; the third is final. The husband's intention to make it three or irrevocable (ba'in) is ineffective; the sarih wording itself only gives one revocable divorce.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-sarih): Explicit divorce wording like 'anti taliq' which is not ambiguous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'you are divorced' intending to give three divorces at once. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) Three divorces occur.
B) One revocable divorce occurs.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs.
D) No divorce occurs until he repeats it.

Key Takeaway
Explicit divorce (sarih) in the Hanafi school is always one revocable pronouncement; the husband's intention to increase is ineffective.

When a Wife Says 'I Am Divorced': The Effect of the Husband's Response
Explains when a wife's statement triggers divorce and the effect of husband's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of a husband's response to his wife's statement about divorce, and apply the Hanafi rule that custom equates 'bala' and 'naam'.

Core Idea
A husband's 'yes' to 'I am divorced' effects divorce whether he means future or past; custom makes 'bala' and 'naam' equivalent affirmations.

Divorce can be initiated by the husband, but sometimes a wife's statement triggers it if the husband confirms. In this lesson, we examine when a wife says 'I am divorced' and the husband responds. The Hanafi scholars analyse his intention and the custom of language to determine the ruling. The key is that the husband's affirmative words—whether 'naam' or 'bala'—have legal effect based on his intention and the common understanding of people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Read the text from al-Muhit: a wife says 'I am divorced' and the husband says 'naam'. Identify the two possible intentions of the husband (future divorce or confirmation of past). Understand that both lead to divorce, but the legal nature differs.
The wife's statement is kinayah; the husband's response is interpreted based on his intention.
2
Distinguish the legal consequences of each intention
If he intends a future divorce, it counts as a new, revocable divorce. If he intends to inform about a past divorce, it confirms that the divorce already occurred. The thamarah al-khilaf is whether it is a new divorce or a confirmation.
In both cases, she is divorced; the difference matters for counting divorces and iddah.
3
Extend to indirect scenarios
Consider the case where someone asks the husband 'Is your wife other than divorced?' and he says 'no' vs. 'yes'. Trace the linguistic logic: 'no' implies she is not other than divorced (ambiguous), but the Hanafi ruling is that 'no' does not effect divorce, while 'yes' does. Also consider the question 'Have you not divorced her?' with 'bala' vs. 'naam'.
These scenarios test the understanding of negative questions and affirmatives.
4
Analyse the preferred view on 'bala' vs 'naam'
The text from Fath al-Qadir states that there should be no difference between 'bala' and 'naam' because custom ('urf) treats them both as simple affirmations of the negated question. Apply this principle.
This overrides any linguistic nuance that might differentiate them.
5
Synthesize the legal principle
Derive: A husband's affirmative response to a wife's divorce statement or to a question about divorce is legally effective. The ruling relies on the husband's intention and customary usage of language, not on the specific wording of the affirmation.
This principle is embedded in the Hanafi reliance on 'urf as a source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الطلاق واعتمده في فتح القدير وروى فيه أثراً عن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه . وفي المحيط : لو قالت المرأة أنا طالق فقال الزوج نعم كانت طالقاً إن نوى به طلاقاً مستقبلاً، وإن نوى به الخبر عما مضى وقع. وفي البزازية قالت له أنا طالق فقال نعم طلقت ولو قالت طلقني فقال وإن نوى ا هـ . ولو قال لآخر هل امرأتك إلا طالق فقال الزوج لا تطلق ولو قال تطلق لأن في الأول صار قائلاً ليس امرأتي إلا طالق وفي الثاني صار قائلاً نعم امرأتي غير طالق ا هـ. وكذا في الخانية. ولو قيل له ألست طلقتها فقال بلى طلقت، ولو قال نعم تطلق، والذي ينبغي عدم الفرق فإن أهل العرف لا يفرقون بل يفهمون منهما إيجاب المنفي كذا في فتح القدير .
The Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. And it was relied upon in Fath al-Qadir, and he narrated in it an effect from Umar, may Allah be pleased with him. And in al-Muhit: If a woman says 'I am divorced' and the husband says 'yes', she is divorced if he intended a future divorce, and if he intended informing about the past, it occurs. And in al-Bazzaziyya: She said to him 'I am divorced' and he said 'yes', she is divorced. And if she said 'Divorce me' and he said 'and'[?] and if he intended... end. And if he said to another 'Is your wife other than divorced?' and the husband said 'no', she is not divorced, and if he said 'yes' she is divorced, because in the first he becomes a sayer 'My wife is not other than divorced' and in the second he becomes a sayer 'Yes, my wife is other than divorced' end. And likewise in al-Khaniyya. And if it was said to him 'Have you not divorced her?' and he said 'bala', she is divorced, and if he said 'naam', she is divorced. And what is appropriate is no difference, for the people of custom do not differentiate, rather they understand from both affirmation of the negated. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement with proof, explaining the effect of a wife's statement about divorce and the husband's response. The author begins by citing an effect (athar) from Umar (RA) indicating that such responses are taken seriously. The main ruling from al-Muhit: if a woman says 'I am divorced' and the husband says 'yes' (n'am), then if he intends by it a future divorce, it takes effect as a new divorce; if he intends to report a past divorce (i.e., confirming that she was already divorced), it also takes effect as a confirmation of that past divorce. Thus in both cases, divorce occurs. Similarly, in al-Bazzaziyya, if she says 'I am divorced' and he says 'yes', she is divorced; and if she says 'Divorce me' and he responds 'and' (wa) apparently meaning he says something like 'and I do', then if he intended divorce it occurs. The text then discusses indirect scenarios: if someone asks the husband 'Is your wife anything but divorced?' and the husband says 'no', she is not divorced because he means 'my wife is not something other than divorced' (i.e., she is not divorced? Actually the logic: 'no' negates the exception, so he is saying 'my wife is not other than divorced' meaning she is not divorced? Wait careful: The phrase 'هل امرأتك إلا طالق' means 'Is your wife other than divorced?' i.e., is she anything but divorced? If he says 'no' (la), he is affirming that she is not other than divorced, meaning she is divorced. But the text says 'لا تطلق' meaning she is not divorced. That seems contradictory. Actually the author explains: because if he says 'la' he becomes a sayer 'ليس امرأتي إلا طالق' meaning 'my wife is not except divorced' which is ambiguous. The Hanafi ruling here is that the response depends on intention and custom. The correct understanding is that if he says 'la' (no), he is denying that she is other than divorced, i.e., affirming she is divorced? The text says 'لا تطلق' meaning she is not divorced. So the ruling is that if he says 'la' she is not divorced, and if he says 'naam' she is divorced. This is because in the first, he is saying 'my wife is not other than divorced' which could mean she is not divorced but rather something else? The reasoning is based on the grammatical structure of exception. The author then mentions that if someone says to the husband 'Have you not divorced her?' and he replies 'bala' (yes, I have), she is divorced; if he replies 'naam' (yes), she is also divorced according to the correct view. The preferred position is not to differentiate between 'bala' and 'naam' because customarily both are understood as affirmations of the negated question. This is from Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit wording of divorce, such as 'anti taliq'.
كناية (kinayah): Implicit wording that may imply divorce, such as 'I am divorced' used as a statement.
عرف (urf): Customary understanding of language used by people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife says 'I am divorced' and the husband says 'naam' intending to confirm a past divorce, what is the ruling?
A) She is not divorced because he confirmed the past.
B) She is divorced because the confirmation itself is a new divorce.
C) She is divorced if he intended a new divorce, but if he intended confirmation, it is not a new divorce.
D) She is divorced even if he intended confirmation, because his word counts as a new divorce according to the preferred view.

Key Takeaway
The husband's affirmative response to the wife's divorce statement is legally effective; the ruling depends on his intention, and custom equates 'bala' and 'naam' as equivalent affirmatives.

Classification of Sarih Talaq into Raj'i and Ba'in
Defines and classifies sarih talaq as raj'i or ba'in with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sarih talaq statement as raj'i or ba'in by applying the Hanafi conditions of consummation, number, and accompanying words.

Core Idea
Sarih talaq after consummation is raj'i by default; only specific conditions make it ba'in.

In Hanafi law, explicit divorce (sarih talaq) is not always revocable. The school distinguishes between raj'i (revocable) and ba'in (irrevocable) based on clear criteria: consummation of marriage, the number of divorces, and the specific words used. This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the authoritative classification from al-Bada'i', helping us determine the legal effect of a given divorce pronouncement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing of the divorce
Determine whether the sarih talaq was pronounced after consummation (dakhul haqiqi) or before. This is the first critical distinction.
Consummation here means actual physical union, not just valid retirement (khalwa sahiha).
2
Check for ba'in conditions
If after consummation, examine whether the talaq is accompanied by compensation (iwad), the number three (explicitly or implicitly), words of ibanah (e.g., 'anti ba'in'), or descriptive words indicating severity or irreversibility.
Also consider if the talaq is described with a quality that implies ba'in, or resembles a number.
3
Apply the classification rule
If after consummation and none of the ba'in conditions are present, the talaq is raj'i. If before consummation or any ba'in condition is present, it is ba'in.
Intention does not change the classification: a sarih raj'i cannot become ba'in by mere intention.
4
Understand the legal effect
Raj'i divorce allows the husband to revoke during the 'iddah without a new contract. Ba'in divorce ends the marriage immediately; revocation requires a new marriage contract.
This has practical implications for spousal interaction during 'iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق وقوع الرجعي لأن الطلاق عند تسمية مال أو في مقابلة إبراء أو عند وصفه بما ينبئ عن الشدة أو عند تقدم طلاق بائن ليس منه فلا حاجة إلى الاحتراز عنه بشيء، وإن كان من الصريح فالمراد عند عدم العارض وفي هذه المواضع البينونة للعارض واختار الأول في فتح القدير، واختار الثاني في البدائع مقتصراً عليه فقال : الصريح نوعان : صريح رجعي وصريح بائن. فالصريح الرجعي أن يكون الطلاق بعد الدخول حقيقة ليس مقروناً بعوض ولا بعدد الثلاث لا نصاً ولا إشارة ولا موصوفاً بصفة تنبئ عن البينونة أو تدل عليها من غير حرف العطف ولا مشبه بعدد أو صفة تدل عليها. وأما الصريح البائن فبخلافه وهو أن يكون بحروف الإبانة أو بحروف الطلاق لكن قبل الدخول حقيقة أو بعده لكن مقروناً بعدد الثلاث نصاً أو إشارة أو موصوفاً بصفة تنبئ عن البينونة أو تدل عليها من غير حرف العطف أو مشبهاً بعدد أو صفة تدل عليها ا هـ . وهو الظاهر لأن حد الصريح يشمل الكل، وأما عدم صحة نية الإبانة فلأنه نوى تغيير الشرع لأن الشرع أثبت البينونة بهذا اللفظ مؤجلاً إلى ما بعد انقضاء العدة، فإذا نوى إثباتها للحال معجلاً فقد نوى تغيير الشرع وليس له هذه الولاية فبطلت نيته .
He said: The occurrence of raj'i is absolute because divorce with mention of a dowry or in exchange for a release or when described with something indicating severity or after a prior ba'in divorce is not from it, so there is no need to guard against it with anything. And if it is from sarih, then the intent is when there is no impediment, and in these places, ba'in occurs due to the impediment. The first opinion was chosen in Fath al-Qadir, and the second was chosen in al-Bada'i', limiting himself to it, saying: Sarih is of two types: sarih raj'i and sarih ba'in. Sarih raj'i is that the divorce is after consummation actually, not accompanied by compensation nor by the number three explicitly or implicitly, nor described with a quality that indicates or implies ba'in without a conjunction, nor resembling a number or quality that indicates it. As for sarih ba'in, it is the opposite: it is with letters of ibanah or letters of talaq but before consummation actually, or after it but accompanied by the number three explicitly or implicitly, or described with a quality that indicates or implies ba'in without a conjunction, or resembling a number or quality that indicates it. End quote. And the apparent view is that the definition of sarih includes all. As for the invalidity of intending ibanah, it is because he intended to change the Shari'ah, for the Shari'ah established ba'in with this word deferred to after the 'iddah. So if he intended to establish it immediately, he intended to change the Shari'ah and has no such authority, so his intention is invalid.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a ruling/definition from the commentary al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the classification of sarih talaq into raj'i and ba'in. The author begins by stating that raj'i divorce applies broadly; it is not prevented by conditions like mentioning a dowry, except that ba'in occurs in those cases due to the impediment. He then presents two views: one from Fath al-Qadir (preferring the first) and one from al-Bada'i' (preferring the second). The second view, from al-Bada'i', is quoted: sarih talaq has two types—sarih raj'i and sarih ba'in. Sarih raj'i is after consummation, without any condition (like compensation, number three, or descriptive words) that indicates ba'in. Sarih ba'in is either before consummation, or after consummation but with an explicit condition (like number three or words of ibanah). The author then adopts the apparent view that the definition of sarih includes all forms, and then explains why intending ba'in in sarih raj'i fails: because the Shari'ah has made such a word carry ba'in only after the 'iddah, so intending immediate ba'in is an invalid attempt to change the Shari'ah.

Key Terms
صريح رجعي (sarih raj'i): Clear (explicit) divorce that is revocable, occurring after consummation without any condition indicating ba'in.
صريح بائن (sarih ba'in): Clear divorce that is irrevocable immediately, either because it is before consummation or accompanied by conditions like three divorces or words of ibanah.
حروف الإبانة (huruf al-ibanah): Words that explicitly indicate irrevocable separation, such as 'anti ba'in' or 'anti khaliyah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of sarih ba'in talaq?
A) Saying 'anti taliq' after consummation without any conditions.
B) Saying 'anti taliq' before consummation.
C) Saying 'anti taliq' after consummation but intending it to be one revocable divorce.
D) Saying 'anti taliq' and not specifying number or condition.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default for sarih talaq after consummation is raj'i; ba'in occurs only if it is before consummation or accompanied by specific conditions like number three or words of ibanah.

Intention for Number in Sarih Talaq (Hanafi vs. Three Imams)
Rulings on intention for number in sarih talaq and the inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intention for number in sarih talaq, explain the Hanafi proof, and distinguish it from the opposing view of the three Imams.

Core Idea
In sarih talaq, only one divorce falls; intention for more is legally invalid.

This lesson addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi school and the majority of other madhahib regarding the effect of intention when a husband utters a sarih (explicit) divorce formula like 'anti taliq' but intends two or three divorces. The Hanafi position, based on the nature of sarih talaq as an insha (creative utterance) of one divorce, holds that the intention for more is irrelevant. The three Imams argue that the word can carry multiple meanings and the number specifies the intended one. We will analyze the proofs and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
What is the exact point of disagreement? The dispute is about whether a husband's intention for more than one divorce has any legal effect when he uses a sarih expression.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school holds that only one divorce occurs, and intention for two or three is invalid. Note the authority: this is zahir riwayah, mufta bihi.
3
Understand the Hanafi Dalil
The Shari'ah has transferred the word from its original linguistic meaning (khabar/reporting) to insha of one divorce. The verbal noun 'intilaq' is a single description that cannot be multiplied. Intending more would require using the word in its original linguistic sense, which is no longer operative.
4
Contrast with the Opposing View
The three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) argue that the word 'talaq' as a verbal noun can carry multiple meanings, and the number can specify which meaning is intended, just as in kinayah. They see sarih as stronger, so if intention works in kinayah, it should work even more in sarih.
5
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response: the transfer to insha abandons the linguistic meaning; the verbal noun indicates a single attribute (intilaq) which cannot be numbered. The analogy with kinayah fails because kinayah has not been transferred to insha of one.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if husband intends three, Hanafi: one divorce only; three Imams: three divorces, irrevocable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني وقوع الواحدة به ولا تصح نية الأكثر ثنتين أو ثلاثاً وقال الأئمة الثلاثة : يقع ما نوى وهو قول الإمام الأول لأنه نوى محتمل لفظه لأن ذكر الطلاق ذكر للطلاق المصدر لأن الوصف كالفعل جزء مفهومه المصدر وهو يحتمله اتفاقاً، ولذا صح قران العدد به تفسيراً حتى ينصب على التمييز، وحاصل التمييز ليس إلا تعيين أحد محتملات اللفظ ولذا صحت نية الثلاث في قوله «أنت بائن» وهو كناية ففي الصريح الأقوى أولى ولنا أن الشارع نقله من الإخبار إلى إنشاء الواحدة إذ لا يفهم من أنت طالق قط لازم الإخبار وهو احتمال الصدق والكذب فجعله موقعاً به ما شاء استعمال في غير المنقول إليه وملاحظة ما أن يصح يراد بالمصدر إنما يتفرع عن إرادة الاستعمال اللغوي ونقله إلى الإنشاء يباينه لأنه جعل اللفظ علة لدخول المعنى الخاص في الوجود المخالف لمقتضاه لغة على أن المصدر الذي يدل عليه اللفظ هو الانطلاق الذي هو وصفها وذلك لا يتعدد أصلاً. وبهذا يظهر عدم صحة إرادة الثلاث مطلقة في وطلقتك لأنه صار إنشاء في الواحدة غير ملاحظ فيه معنى اللغة، وعلى هذا فالعدد نجو «ثلاثاً» لا يكون صفة لمصدر الوصف بل لمصدر غيره أي طلاقاً أي تطليقاً ثلاثاً كما ينصب في الفعل مصدر غيره مثل أنبتكم من الأرض نباتا) [نوح: ١٧] أو يضمر له فعل على الخلاف فيه بخلاف طلقتها وطلقي نفسك لأن المصدر المحتمل للكل مذكور لغة فصح إرادته منه لأنه لا نقل فيه إلى إيقاع واحدة وفيه أبحاث مذكورة في فتح القدير .
The second: The occurrence of one [divorce] by it, and the intention of more, two or three, is not valid. And the three Imams said: What he intended occurs, and it is the saying of the first Imam because he intended a possible meaning of his word, because the mention of talaq is mention of the verbal noun 'talaq', since the description is like the act, part of its conceptual meaning is the verbal noun, and it can carry it by agreement. Hence, pairing the number with it is valid as an interpretation, so it is set as a tamyiz. The essence of tamyiz is nothing but specifying one of the possible meanings of the word, and hence the intention of three is valid in his saying 'anti ba'in', which is a kinayah, so in sarih which is stronger, it is more appropriate. And for us: The Lawgiver transferred it from khabar to insha of one [divorce], for one never understands from 'anti taliq' the necessary implication of khabar which is probability of truth and falsehood. He made it an instrument for bringing about what he wishes, used in something other than the transferred meaning. Noting what can be intended by the verbal noun derives only from intending the linguistic use, and transferring it to insha contradicts that, because he made the word a cause for the entry of a specific meaning into existence, contradicting its linguistic import. Moreover, the verbal noun indicated by the word is 'intilaq' which is a description of her, and that cannot be multiple at all. Thus it becomes clear that intending three absolutely in 'tallaqtuki' is invalid, because it has become insha of one, not noting the linguistic meaning. On this, the number 'thalatha' is not an adjective for the verbal noun of the description, but for another verbal noun, i.e., 'talaq' meaning 'tatliq' three times, like in the verb a different source verbal noun is set, such as {anbatakum min al-ardi nabatan} [Nuh:17], or a verb is implicit for it, differing on that. Unlike 'tallaqtaha' and 'talliqi nafsaki', because the verbal noun that can carry all is mentioned linguistically, so intending it from it is valid, because there is no transfer to effecting one. And there are discussions mentioned in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding intention for the number of divorces in sarih talaq. The Hanafi position is that only one divorce falls, and the intention for two or three is invalid. The three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that what the husband intends occurs. The Hanafi argument is that the Lawgiver (Shari'ah) has transferred the word 'taliq' from its original linguistic meaning (reporting) to a new meaning: creating one divorce. Intentions beyond one would require using the word in its original linguistic sense, which is no longer operative. The word 'talaq' as a verbal noun indicates 'intilaq' (leaving), which is a single description and cannot be multiplied. Therefore, intending three is ineffective. The author contrasts this with kinayah (like 'anti ba'in'), where intention does work because the word is not transferred to insha of one. The passage ends by referencing further discussions in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
الإنشاء (al-insha): Creating a legal effect (here, divorce) instantly by utterance, as opposed to reporting (khabar).
التمييز (al-tamyiz): A grammatical construction that specifies the meaning of a preceding ambiguous number or measure.
الصريح (al-sarih): Explicit divorce expression (e.g., 'anti taliq') that clearly indicates divorce without need for intention.
الكناية (al-kinayah): Implicit divorce expression (e.g., 'anti ba'in') that can carry multiple meanings; intention specifies which meaning is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'anti taliq' intending three divorces, what is the result?
A) Three divorces occur because of his intention.
B) One divorce occurs, and the intention for three is invalid.
C) The divorce is invalid because intention does not match the wording.
D) It becomes a kinayah and three divorces occur.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, sarih talaq (like 'anti taliq') always results in one divorce regardless of the husband's intention for a higher number.

Changing the Type of Revocable Divorce: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on post-divorce changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding changing a revocable divorce to ba'in or three, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf in each scenario.

Core Idea
Can a husband later change a revocable divorce? The three Hanafi imams differ on whether such a statement takes effect.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses whether a husband who pronounces one revocable divorce can later, by a subsequent statement, convert it into a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce or into three divorces. The ruling depends on which of the three Hanafi masters' position one follows. We'll examine the ikhtilaf and the reasoning behind each view, along with several specific scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ikhtilaf
What is the husband doing? He is trying to change the legal nature of a prior divorce. The ikhtilaf is over whether such a change is effective.
The act is a subsequent statement after one revocable divorce.
2
State each imam's position
Abu Hanifa allows both ba'in and three; Muhammad disallows both; Abu Yusuf allows ba'in only.
Memorize the three views for fatwa purposes.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
Abu Hanifa: because the husband could have made it ba'in initially, so he can annex it later. Muhammad: once a divorce is pronounced, its nature is fixed. Abu Yusuf: ba'in can be attached because the 'iddah can cut off revocability, but adding two more divorces requires a new action.
This is qiyas khafi: analogizing to initial ability.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, one statement can make it three; under Muhammad, it remains one revocable; under Abu Yusuf, it becomes ba'in (one irrevocable).
Practical consequence: the finality of the divorce differs.
5
Consider other scenarios in the text
After 'iddah, after revocation, conditional statements, and delayed statements. Each has its own ruling based on the same principles.
The passage also covers exception: if he revoked first, he cannot change; if he conditioned before, it fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: Intention of three is valid only in kinayah, because they operate according to their real meanings and they vary into severe and light. So in the absence of intention, the lighter is established for certainty. It is restricted by intention because if he divorced her after consummation once, then said 'I made it ba'in' or 'I made it three', the narrations differ. And if he said to her 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', then said 'I have made this divorce ba'in' or 'I have made it three', said: this statement before entering the house does not bind him because the divorce has not taken place. So in al-Khaniyyah. And if he divorced her revocably, then said 'I make it three at the beginning of the month', then took her back, said: it becomes three at the beginning of the month. He said: His statement 'I made it ba'in' is not like his statement 'I made it three'. As for Muhammad's saying, it is apparent. As for Abu Yusuf's saying, the revocable becomes ba'in by the end of the 'iddah, but one does not become three. As for the Imam's saying, because he has the power to effect it as ba'in from the beginning, so he can annex it to ba'in, because he can create ibanah in this situation as he could at the beginning. The meaning of making one three is that he adds two more divorces, not that he makes the one three. So in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: If he said 'You are divorced absolutely', it falls as ba'in, unless he intended another divorce besides his saying 'you are divorced', then they become two. End quote. The third: It does not depend on intention, and consensus of the jurists is reported about it, and because the possibility of intending divorce without the restriction of marriage is a remote possibility when addressing the woman, so it is disregarded. Thus the word becomes equivalent to the meaning, and the hadith of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) where he ordered him to take her back and did not ask him whether he intended...

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings about changing the nature of a revocable divorce by subsequent statement, with intra-Hanafi differences. It begins by clarifying that intention for number works in kinayah because those words operate literally. Then it discusses the scenario where a husband says after one revocable divorce 'I made it ba'in' or 'I made it three.' According to Abu Hanifa, it becomes ba'in or three; according to Muhammad, it does not; according to Abu Yusuf, ba'in is valid but three is not. Various details follow: if the 'iddah has passed, the husband's statement takes effect; if he revoked and then tries to change, it fails; if he made a conditional statement beforehand, it is ineffective if divorce hasn't occurred; and if delayed (e.g., 'at the beginning of the month'), the ruling depends on whether he revoked in the meantime. The passage concludes with the 'third' ruling that sarih talaq does not require intention to be effective, citing consensus and the hadith of Ibn Umar.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'ala): To make or render; here, a subsequent statement attempting to change the legal nature of a previously pronounced divorce.
الإلحاق (al-ilhaq): Annexation or attaching additional legal effect (like adding two divorces) to an existing one.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately and cannot be revoked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The effect of a post-divorce statement to change the type of divorce is disputed among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, with the practical consequence depending on which view is adopted.

Converting a Revocable Divorce: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ba'in and Triple
Intra-Hanafi dispute on converting a single divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on converting a single revocable divorce into ba'in or triple, and apply each position to practical divorce scenarios.

Core Idea
Can a husband later declare a past divorce irrevocable or triple? The Hanafi imams split three ways.

This lesson examines a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute: after pronouncing a single revocable divorce, can the husband make it irrevocable (ba'in) or triple by a subsequent statement? The three eponymous imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—each give different answers. We will explore their reasoning, the practical consequences, and how later jurists weighed the views. This dispute affects the finality of divorce and the possibilities of reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: Can a husband, after already pronouncing one revocable divorce, validly declare that same divorce to be ba'in (irrevocable) or to count as three divorces? The act is a retrospective alteration of the divorce's legal effect.
2
State the three positions
Abu Hanifa: the statement makes the divorce both ba'in and triple. Abu Yusuf: it makes it ba'in but not triple. Muhammad: it has no effect—the divorce remains a single revocable one.
Author marks Abu Hanifa's view as 'al-sahih' (the correct one).
3
Examine the reasoning (talil)
Abu Hanifa argues the husband has continuous power to issue a ba'in divorce, so he can attach it retroactively. Muhammad argues the past divorce is complete and cannot be altered. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: ba'in is like a new pronouncement, but triple would alter the count of past divorces, which is impermissible.
The article emphasizes Abu Hanifa's reasoning: 'he has power to initiate irrevocability from the beginning, so he can attach it.'
4
Analyze the scenarios
The passage gives multiple scenarios: after 'iddah, after reconciliation, prospective conditions, delayed statements. Each scenario tests the boundaries of the positions and shows consistent application.
For example, if he reconciles and later declares ba'in, the reconciliation cannot be undone, so the declaration fails.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the husband can turn a single divorce into three with immediate effect, ending the marriage irrevocably. Under Muhammad, the statement is ineffective, preserving the revocable nature. Under Abu Yusuf, it becomes irrevocable but not triple, so the wife must observe 'iddah and then remarriage is needed, but she is not divorced three times.
This is crucial for fatwa: a mufti must know which view is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما صحت نية الثلاث في الكنايات لأنها عاملة بحقائقها وهي متنوعة إلى غليظة وخفيفة، فعند عدم النية يثبت الأخف للتيقن به قيد بالنية لأنه لو طلقها بعد الدخول واحدة ثم قال جعلت تلك التطليقة بائنة أو جعلتها ثلاثاً اختلفت الروايات، والصحيح أن على قول أبي حنيفة تصير بائناً وثلاثاً، وعلى قول محمد لا تصير بائناً ولا ثلاثاً، وعلى قول أبي يوسف يصح جعلها بائناً ولا يصح جعلها ثلاثاً. ولو طلق امرأته بعد الدخول واحدة ثم بعد العدة ألزمت امرأتي ثلاث تطليقات بتلك التطليقة أو قال ألزمتها تطليقتين بتلك التطليقة فهو على ما قال إن ألزمها ثلاثاً فهي ثلاث وإن قال ألزمها تطليقتين فهي ثنتان. ولو طلقها واحدة ثم راجعها ثم قال جعلت تلك التطليقة بائنة لا تصير بائنة لأنه لا يملك إبطال الرجعة. ولو قال لها بعد الدخول إذا طلقتك واحدة فهي بائن أو هي ثلاث فطلقها واحدة فإنه يملك الرجعة ولا يكون بائناً ولا ثلاثاً لأنه قدم القول قبل نزول الطلاق. ولو قال لها إذا دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثم قال جعلت هذه التطليقة بائناً أو قال جعلتها ثلاثاً قال هذه المقالة قبل دخول الدار لا تلزمه هذه المقالة لأن التطليقة لم تقع عليها. كذا في الخانية. وفي التتمة: لو طلقها واحدة ثم قال جعلتها بائنة رأس الشهر قال: إن لم يراجعها فهي بائن، وإن راجعها فيما بين ذلك لا يكون بائناً. ولو طلقها رجعية ثم قال جعلتها ثلاثاً رأس الشهر ثم راجعها قال: تكون رأس الشهر ثلاثاً قال: وليس يشبه قوله جعلتها بائناً قوله جعلتها ثلاثاً أما قول محمد فظاهر، وأما قول أبي يوسف فإن الرجعية تصير بائنة بانقضاء العدة، وأما الواحدة فلا تصير ثلاثاً. وأما قول الإمام فلأنه يملك إيقاعها بائنة من الابتداء فيملك إلحاقها بالبائنة لأنه يملك إنشاء الإبانة في هذه الحالة كما كان يملكها في الابتداء، ومعنى جعل الواحدة ثلاثاً أنه ألحق بها تطليقتين أخريين لا أنه جعل الواحدة ثلاثاً. كذا في البدائع.
And the intention of three [divorces] is valid only in kinayat because they operate according to their lexical realities, and they vary into severe and light. In the absence of intention, the lighter [type] is established due to certainty. It is conditioned by intention because if he divorces her after consummation once, then says 'I made that divorce irrevocable' or 'I made it three,' the transmissions differ. The correct view is that according to Abu Hanifa it becomes irrevocable and three; according to Muhammad it does not become irrevocable nor three; according to Abu Yusuf it becomes irrevocable but does not become three. If he divorces his wife after consummation once, then after the waiting period says 'I made my wife three divorces with that divorce' or 'I made her two divorces with that divorce,' it is as he said: if he made it three then it is three, and if he said he made it two then it is two. If he divorced her once then took her back, then said 'I made that divorce irrevocable,' it does not become irrevocable because he does not have the power to invalidate the return. If he said to her after consummation 'when I divorce you once, it is irrevocable' or 'it is three,' then he divorces her once, he retains the right of return and it is neither irrevocable nor three because he made the statement before the divorce occurred. If he said to her 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' then said 'I made this divorce irrevocable' or 'I made it three' before she entered the house, this statement does not bind him because the divorce has not fallen on her. This is in al-Khaniyya. In al-Tatimma: if he divorced her once then said 'I make it irrevocable at the beginning of the month,' [it is said]: if he does not take her back, it is irrevocable; if he takes her back in between, it is not irrevocable. If he divorced her revocably then said 'I make it three at the beginning of the month' then took her back, [it is said]: it becomes three at the beginning of the month. He said: and his saying 'I make it irrevocable' is not similar to his saying 'I make it three.' As for Muhammad's view, it is apparent. As for Abu Yusuf's view, the revocable divorce becomes irrevocable by the expiry of the waiting period, but the one [divorce] does not become three. As for the Imam's view, it is because he has the power to effect an irrevocable divorce from the beginning, so he has the power to attach it to the irrevocable, because he has the power to initiate irrevocability in this situation just as he had it at the beginning. And the meaning of making one divorce three is that he attached two other divorces to it, not that he made the one divorce three. Thus in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the legal effect of a husband's statement after pronouncing a single revocable (raj'i) divorce that he intended that divorce to be ba'in (irrevocable) or triple (three divorces). The author explains that in kinayat (allusive words of divorce), intention is effective because kinayat operate according to their literal meanings, which can be severe or light. In the absence of intention, the lighter form is presumed due to certainty. However, when the husband explicitly says after a divorce that he made it ba'in or three, the imams differ. Abu Hanifa holds that it becomes both ba'in and three, because he can initiate irrevocability at any time. Muhammad holds that it does not become ba'in or three, because the divorce has already occurred and cannot be changed. Abu Yusuf makes a distinction: it becomes ba'in but not three. The passage then provides several scenarios illustrating the practical application. For example, if a husband says after the 'iddah (waiting period) that he made the previous single divorce into three, it is as he says. But if he had taken his wife back (raj'a) before making the statement, the reconciliation cannot be invalidated, so the statement does not turn the divorce into ba'in. If he makes a conditional statement before the divorce (e.g., 'if I divorce you once, it is ba'in'), then the divorce occurs, the condition is not fulfilled because the statement was prospective, so the divorce remains revocable. The author also discusses a delayed statement: if he says 'I make it ba'in at the beginning of the month,' if he does not take her back, it becomes ba'in at that time; if he takes her back before, it does not. For triple, if he says 'I make it three at the beginning of the month' and then takes her back, it becomes three at that time. The reason for the difference is that making it ba'in can be seen as a new pronouncement of irrevocability, while making it three is attaching additional divorces, which is not permissible after reconciliation. Abu Hanifa's view is based on his power to initiate irrevocability. This dispute affects actual divorce calculations: under Abu Hanifa, a subsequent statement can convert a single divorce into three, immediately finalizing the marriage; under the Sahibayn, the statement is largely ineffective, preserving the revocable nature unless specific conditions are met.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.
كنايات (kinayat): Allusive or indirect words that can imply divorce but require intention to make them effective.
رجعة (raj'a): The husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period of a revocable divorce without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which position is considered 'al-sahih' (the correct one)?
A) Abu Hanifa's view that it becomes ba'in and three.
B) Abu Yusuf's view that it becomes ba'in only.
C) Muhammad's view that it neither becomes ba'in nor three.
D) The view that there is no single correct position.

Key Takeaway
The three Hanafi imams differ sharply on whether a husband can retroactively convert a single revocable divorce into ba'in or triple; Abu Hanifa's view is considered the most authoritative, but later caution may favour Muhammad's stricter position.

Talaq — Divorce

The Sarih Word of Divorce: Intention Not Required
Establishes that the explicit word 'taliq' effects divorce without need for intention, with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that sarih divorce words do not require intention and apply it to scenarios where a husband utters 'anti taliq' without meaning divorce.

Core Idea
The explicit word 'taliq' effects divorce immediately; intention is irrelevant.

Divorce in Islamic law can be initiated by the husband through specific words. But what if he says the word without actually meaning to divorce? This lesson examines the third principle regarding divorce: that the explicit word 'taliq' is so clear that it does not require inner intention. We will explore the consensus of jurists, a rational argument, and a key hadith from Ibn Umar that confirms this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states the third principle: the word 'taliq' does not depend on intention. This is the ruling we are analyzing.
2
Recognize the evidence: ijma
The author reports consensus of the jurists on this point. Understand that ijma is a primary proof in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the rational proof
The possibility that the husband means divorce from something other than marriage is extremely remote when addressing his wife, so it is disregarded. The word becomes equivalent to the reality.
4
Examine the hadith of Ibn Umar
When Ibn Umar divorced his wife during her menses, the Prophet ordered him to take her back and did not ask about his intention. This omission indicates that intention is not a condition for the validity of the divorce.
5
Apply the legal maxim
The maxim 'ترك الاستفصال في وقائع الأحوال كالعموم في المقال' (the omission to inquire in similar occurrences is like a general statement) supports that intention is irrelevant for sarih words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عدم توقفه على النية ونقل فيه إجماع الفقهاء، ولأن احتمال إرادة الطلاق عن غير قيد النكاح احتمال بعيد عند خطاب المرأة فلا عبرة به، فصار اللفظ بمنزلة المعنى، وحديث ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما حيث أمره بالمراجعة ولم يسأله أنوى أم لا، يدل على ذلك فإن ترك الاستفصال في وقائع الأحوال كالعموم في المقال.
The third [principle]: it does not depend on intention. Consensus of the jurists is reported on it, and because the possibility of intending divorce for something other than the bond of marriage is a remote possibility when addressing the wife, so it is not considered. Thus the word becomes equivalent to the meaning. And the hadith of Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, where he ordered him to take her back and did not ask him whether he intended or not, indicates that. For the omission to inquire in similar legal occurrences is like a general statement in the discourse.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh: the explicit word 'taliq' (or 'anti taliq' when addressed to the wife) effects divorce immediately without any need to investigate the husband's intention. The author identifies this as the third principle regarding divorce pronouncements. The proof is based on two things: first, a reported consensus (ijma) of the jurists that the word is sarih (explicit) and does not require intention; second, a rational argument: when a husband says 'anti taliq' to his wife, the possibility that he means divorce from something other than marriage (e.g., from a bond or restraint) is extremely unlikely, so it is disregarded. The word 'taliq' is so clear in meaning that it is treated as if it is the reality itself. The author then cites the famous hadith of Ibn Umar: when he divorced his wife during her menses, the Prophet ﷺ ordered him to take her back and did not ask him whether he had intended divorce or not. This shows that once the word is uttered, the divorce is effective regardless of inner intention. The legal maxim 'ترك الاستفصال في وقائع الأحوال كالعموم في المقال' (the omission to inquire in similar occurrences is like a general statement) is used: since the Prophet ﷺ did not ask about intention, we conclude that intention is irrelevant for the validity of the divorce. This principle applies to all explicit words of divorce. However, if the husband claims he intended something else, such as divorce from a rope or bond, he is believed in his heart (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an) unless there is a plausible scenario.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): An explicit word that clearly indicates divorce and does not require intention to effect divorce.
نية (niyyah): Inner intention; for sarih words, it is not required for the divorce to take effect.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the jurists, cited as proof that intention is not required for explicit divorce words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife 'anti taliq' without any intention of divorce, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce does not occur because intention is required.
B) The divorce occurs only if he intended it.
C) The divorce occurs, and his lack of intention is ignored in court.
D) The divorce is considered kinayah and requires clarification.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: explicit divorce words (sarih) like 'taliq' effect immediate divorce regardless of the husband's intention; intention is relevant only for kinayah words.

The Three Words in Divorce: Wathaq, Qayd, and 'Amal
Explains the legal treatment of the words 'wathaq', 'qayd', and 'amal' when used in divorce pronouncements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal treatment of the three words 'wathaq', 'qayd', and 'amal' in divorce pronouncements and apply the rules for when divorce occurs qada'an vs diyanatan.

Core Idea
The word 'amal' is sarih in court; wathaq and qayd are kinayah requiring intention.

In the Hanafi school, the word 'taliq' (divorce) is typically sarih and does not require intention. However, when a husband uses qualifying words such as 'wathaq', 'qayd', or 'amal', the ruling changes. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines this by explaining that these three words are treated differently based on whether they are mentioned with a number, and whether the husband intends divorce. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly determining the occurrence of divorce in court versus in conscience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three words and their classification
Recognize that 'wathaq' (bond), 'qayd' (fetter), and 'amal' (action) are the words under discussion. Note that 'amal' is treated like a sarih in court, while wathaq and qayd are kinayah.
2
Determine the effect when mentioned with a number
If any of these words is accompanied by a number (e.g., 'three'), the number overrides the qualification, and the divorce occurs without intention. The husband is not believed in court if he claims otherwise.
Example: 'Anti taliq thalatha min hadha al-qayd' results in triple divorce.
3
Determine the effect for 'amal without a number
If 'amal is mentioned without a number, the divorce falls qada'an (in court) regardless of the husband's intention. Diyanatan (in conscience), he may be believed if he intended something else.
Example: 'Anti taliq min hadha al-'amal' - divorce is effective in court.
4
Determine the effect for wathaq and qayd without a number
For wathaq and qayd, if mentioned without a number, divorce does not occur unless the husband intended divorce (diyanatan). In court, he is believed if he claims he did not intend divorce unless he is coerced.
These are pure kinayah; intention is required.
5
Apply the rule: qada'an vs diyanatan
Distinguish between judicial effect (qada'an) and religious validity (diyanatan). The woman must treat the divorce as effective when she hears it in court-related contexts, but diyanatan the husband's intention matters for wathaq and qayd.
The woman is like the judge: if she hears it or a trustworthy person informs her, it is not lawful for her to remain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعدل المصنف عن قوله وإن نوى غيره ليفيد أنه لو نوى غيره صدق ولذا قال في فتح القدير: ثم قولنا لا يتوقف على النية معناه إذا لم ينو شيئاً أصلاً يقع لا أنه يقع وإن نوى شيئاً آخر لما ذكر أنه إذا نوى الطلاق عن وثاق صدق إلى آخره. وحاصل ما ذكروه هنا ثلاثة ألفاظ: الوثاق والقيد والعمل، وكل منهما إما أن يذكر أو ينوى، فإن ذكر فإما أن يقرن بالعدد أو لا، فإن قرن بالعدد لا يلتفت إليه ويقع الطلاق بلا نية كما لو قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً من هذا القيد تطلق ثلاثاً ولا يصدق في القضاء كما في المحيط. وإن لم يقرن بالعدد وقع في ذكر العمل قضاء لا ديانة نحو أنت طالق من هذا العمل كما في البزازية وغيرها، وهو يدل على أنه لو قال علي الطلاق من ذراعي لا أفعل كذا كما يحلف به بعض العوام أنه يقع قضاء بالأولى. وفي لفظي الوثاق والقيد لا يقع يذكر شيئاً من هذه الثلاثة وإنما نواها لا يدين في لفظ العمل أصلاً ويدين في الوثاق والقيد، ويقع قضاء إلا أن يكون مكرها. والمرأة كالقاضي إذا سمعته أو أخبرها عدل لا يحل لها أصلاً.
And the author deviated from his statement 'even if he intended something else' to indicate that if he intended something else, he is believed. Thus it is said in Fath al-Qadir: 'Our statement that it does not depend on intention means that if he intended nothing at all, it falls; it does not mean that it falls even if he intended something else, because it was mentioned that if he intended divorce from a bond, he is believed.' And the summary of what they mentioned here is three words: al-wathaq (bond), al-qayd (fetter), and al-'amal (action). Each of them either is mentioned or intended. If mentioned, it either is accompanied by a number or not. If accompanied by a number, it is not paid attention to, and the divorce falls without intention, as if he said 'you are divorced three times from this fetter' – she is divorced three times, and he is not believed in court, as in al-Muhit. If not accompanied by a number and the word 'amal' is mentioned, it falls in court but not in conscience, e.g., 'you are divorced from this action' as in al-Bazzaziyya and others. And this indicates that if he says 'I take an oath of divorce from my arm that I will not do such and such,' as some common people swear by, it falls in court by even stronger reason. For the two words 'wathaq' and 'qayd', if he mentions something from these three and intends them, he is not believed (diyanatan) regarding the word 'amal' at all, but he is believed regarding 'wathaq' and 'qayd'. And it falls in court unless he is coerced. And the woman is like the judge: if she hears it or a trustworthy person informs her, it is not lawful for her [to remain with him] at all.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage refines the earlier principle that the word 'taliq' does not require intention by distinguishing three specific words that can be used: 'al-wathaq' (bond/rope), 'al-qayd' (fetter/restraint), and 'al-'amal' (action). The author explains that if the husband uses these words in a divorce context, their legal effect depends on whether they are mentioned explicitly and whether they are accompanied by a number. The key point: if the husband says 'anti taliq min hadha al-'amal' (you are divorced from this action), the divorce occurs in court (qada'an) even if he intended something else, because the word 'amal' is treated like a sarih in this context. But diyanatan (in his conscience), he may be believed if he had a plausible intention (like divorce from a specific task). For 'wathaq' and 'qayd', if he mentions them without a number, the divorce does not occur unless he intended divorce (they are pure kinayat). If he intends divorce with them, it occurs only if he is not coerced. However, if he accompanies the word with a number (e.g., 'three'), then the number overrides the qualification, and the divorce counts as triple regardless of his intention. The author then draws an analogy: if a layperson swears 'ala al-talaq min dhira'i' (by divorce from my arm) that he will not do something, the divorce occurs in court even more so, because the common usage is to swear by divorce, not to intend divorce of the wife. The woman, upon hearing such a statement, must treat the divorce as effective, similar to a judge's ruling. This passage shows the Hanafi attention to contextual usage and the distinction between judicial and religious validity.

Key Terms
وثاق (wathaq): A bond or restraint; when used in divorce, it is a kinayah that requires intention.
قيد (qayd): A fetter or restriction; similar to wathaq, it is a kinayah for divorce.
عمل (‘amal): An action; when used in divorce (e.g., 'anti taliq min hadha al-'amal'), it is considered sarih in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says 'Anti taliq min hadha al-'amal' without intending divorce, what is the ruling in court?
A) The divorce does not occur because intention is missing.
B) The divorce occurs, and the husband is believed if he claims he did not intend divorce.
C) The divorce occurs, and the husband is not believed in court.
D) It is treated as an oath, not a divorce.

Key Takeaway
The word 'amal is sarih in court for divorce, while wathaq and qayd are kinayah that require intention; a number overrides the qualification for all three.

The Two Levels of Talaq: Qada and Diyanah
Ruling on the mistaken and joker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) effects of talaq and apply the distinction to the mistaken (mukhti') and joker (hazil).

Core Idea
Explicit divorce binds the court without intention; before Allah, only the mistaken is excused, not the joker.

In Hanafi fiqh, a divorce pronounced in court may be legally binding even if the husband did not intend it, but its spiritual validity before Allah depends on his true intention. This lesson explores that crucial distinction through the rulings on the mistaken and the joker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two registers
Recognize that Hanafi law operates on two levels: qada' (judicial, outward effect) and diyanah (religious, between the person and Allah). The same act may be valid on one level but not the other.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi jurisprudence.
2
Understand the principle for sarih talaq
Explicit (sarih) words of divorce are effective in court even without intention. This is the basis for the qada' ruling.
Sarih includes words like 'anti taliq'.
3
Distinguish the mukhti' from the hazil
The mukhti' (mistaken) did not intend to divorce; his divorce is valid only qada'an, not diyanatan. The hazil (joker) intended the words but not the effect; his divorce is valid both qada'an and diyanatan because he is willful (mukabir).
The joker is treated seriously to preserve the sanctity of marriage.
4
Contrast the ruling for the la'ib (player)
The la'ib is similar to the hazil; his divorce is also valid on both levels, as he uttered the words willfully.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that the mukhti' is believed religiously (yudinu); Abu Yusuf disagreed. The madhhab follows Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أو تهرب، وليس له أن يقتلها إذا حرمت عليه ولا يقدر أن يتخلص منها . أنه كلما هرب ردته بالسحر . بسبب الكل في شرح المنظومة لابن الشحنة، وسيأتي في فصل ما تحل به المطلقة أنه هل لها أن تتزوج بغيره في غيبته اللفظ وعن أبي حنيفة يدين لأنه يستعمل للتخلص فكأنه قال أنت متخلصة عن العمل، وعلل وقوعه أيضاً فيما لو ذكر العدد بأنه يظن أنه طلق ثم وصل لفظ العمل استدراكاً بخلاف ما لو وصل لفظ الوثاق حيث يصدق قضاء لأنه طلاق الهازل واللاعب والمخطئ واقع كما قدمناه لكنه في القضاء، وأما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى فلا يقع على المخطئ. وما في الخلاصة من أن طلاق المخطئ واقع أي في القضاء بدليل أنه قال بعده ولو كان بالعتاق يدين لأنه لا فرق بين العتاق والطلاق وهو الظاهر من قول الإمام في الخانية خلافاً لأبي يوسف، ولا خلاف أن المنذور يلزمه، ولا خلاف أنه لو جرى على لسانه الكفر مخطئاً لا يكفر كما في الخانية أيضاً، وكذا إذا تلفظ به غير عالم بمعناه وإنما يقع قضاء فقط بدليل ما في الخلاصة : قالت لزوجها اقرأ علي اعتدي أنت طالق ثلاثاً ففعل طلقت ثلاثاً في القضاء لا فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى إذا لم يعلم الزوج ولم ينو بخلاف الهازل فإنه يقع عليه قضاء وديانة لأنه مكابر باللفظ فيستحق التغليظ وما في الخلاصة معزياً إلى الأصل له امرأتان زينب وعمرة فقال يا زينب فأجابته عمرة فقال أنت طالق ثلاثاً طلقت المجيبة، فلو قال نويت زينب طلقت هذه بالإشارة وتلك بالاعتراف ا هـ . محمول على القضاء، أما في الديانة فلا يقع على واحدة منهما لما في الحاوي معزياً إلى الجامع الصغير أن أراد أن يقول زينب طالق فجرى على لسانه عمرة على أيهما يقع الطلاق فقال : في القضاء تطلق التي سمي، وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لا تطلق واحدة منهما، أما فلانة لم يردها، وأما غيرها فلأنها لو طلقت طلقت بمجرد النية. قال في فتح القدير : وأما ما روى عنهما نصير من أن من أراد أن يتكلم فجرى على لسانه الطلاق يقع ديانة وقضاء فلا يعول عليها ا هـ . والحاصل أن قولهم الصريح لا يحتاج إلى النية إنما هو في القضاء، أما في الديانة فعليه الفتوى نص عليه في هبة النوازل ا هـ قوله : يقاس على النكاح قال الرملي : الذي ذكره قاضيخان في كتاب النكاح في الفصل الأول يقتضي قياس النكاح على الطلاق والعتاق لا قياسهما عليه فإن عبارته بعد الكلام عليهما وإذا عرف الجواب في الطلاق والعتاق ينبغي أن يكون الجواب في النكاح كذلك فراجعه قوله : (فلا يقع على المخطئ) قيد به لأن طلاق الهازل واللاعب واقع ديانة أيضاً كما يأتي قريباً. وتقدمت المسألة أيضاً عند قول المتن ولو مكرها ومر ما فيها من المخالفة أيضاً بين الخانية والبزازية. قوله : (أما في الديانة فلا يقع على واحدة منهما الخ فيه نظر والذي يظهر وقوعه على المجيبة قضاء وديانة لأنه خاطبها بالطلاق وعلى زينب قضاء فقط كما هو مفاد تعليل الأصل، وأما ما في الحاوي فليس فيه إشارة ومخاطبة بل مجرد التسمية بلا قصد تأمل قوله : (والحاصل أن قولهم الصريح لا يحتاج إلى نية إنمنا هو في القضاء هذا خاص بالمخطئ، أما الهازل فلا يحتاج إليها مطلقاً، وما ذكره المؤلف هنا تبع فيه ما حققه في فتح القدير وهو ما حققه أيضاً في التحرير فقال : ثم من ثبوت حكم الصريح بلا نية جريانه على لسانه غلطاً في نحو سبحان الله واسقني أما قصد الصريح مع
She can have him swear. And thus the commentators restricted. And it is mentioned in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Uzjandi that she raises the matter to the judge; if she has no evidence, she can have him swear; if he swears, the sin is upon him. There is no difference between the irrevocable divorce of one and three. And is it permissible for her to kill him if he wants intercourse after she knows of the irrevocable divorce? There are two sayings, and the fatwa is that she cannot kill him; if it is said she can kill him, she kills him with medicine; if she kills him with a weapon, retaliation is required. And she cannot kill herself; she must ransom herself with wealth or flee. And he cannot kill her if she is forbidden to him, nor can he get rid of her. That whenever she flees, he returns her by sorcery. Due to the whole in Sharh al-Manzoomah by Ibn al-Shihnah. And it will come in the section on what makes a divorced woman permissible that whether she can marry another in his absence if she knows of the irrevocability and he denies. He said in al-Misbah: al-wathaq with fat-h the waw and kasr is the fetter, its plural wuthuq like ribat and rabt. And it benefits that its non-dependence on intention means that knowledge of its meaning is not required; if she prompts him with the word of divorce and he utters it without knowing its meaning, it occurs judicially but not religiously. And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all, to protect people's properties from loss due to deception, as in al-Bada'i. Similarly in al-Bazzaziyya. And manumission, tadbir, and release of dower are like divorce as in al-Bazzaziyya. And divorce and what is with it are analogized to his addressing his wife and not adding it to her; it is like what passed from al-Bazzaziyya of his saying: 'Do not go out except with my permission, for I have sworn by divorce'; if she goes out, it does not occur because he did not mention his oath by her divorce; and if the custom is not that, then the apparent is occurrence because it is like 'if you do so, you are divorced' as passed from al-Fath. So his statement afterward 'from my forearms' is like his statement 'from this work'; ponder his statement: (He is not believed regarding the word 'work'). He said in al-Fath: because divorce is for removing a restriction, and she is not restricted by work, so the word does not carry that meaning; from Abu Hanifa, he is believed because it is used for release, as if he said 'you are released from work'; and he also reasoned its occurrence if he mentioned the number, thinking he divorced, then connected the word 'work' as a correction, unlike if he connected the word 'wathaq' where he is believed judicially because it is used for it rarely. His statement: (And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all). He said in al-Tatarakhaniyya: it is narrated from al-Qadi al-Imam Mahmud al-Uzjandi regarding one whose wife prompted him to divorce and he divorced not knowing that: he said this issue occurred in Uzjand and I consulted my companions and our opinions agreed that fatwa should not be given for the occurrence of divorce to protect people's properties from invalidation by a type of deception. And if she prompted him to take khul' from her for her dower and 'idda maintenance and she took khul', and he divorced her, some scholars said it is valid but if the husband does not accept it is not valid; some said it is not valid and that is given as fatwa. And he said in al-Bazzaziyya elsewhere: She prompted him to divorce in Arabic and he does not know, or manumission or tadbir, or the husband prompted her to release of dower and 'idda maintenance in Arabic and she does not know, al-Faqih or al-Layth said: it does not occur religiously. And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all to protect people's properties from invalidation by deception, and like if he sells or buys in Arabic and he does not know; and some distinguished between sale and purchase and divorce, manumission, khul' and gift considering that consent has effect in the existence of sale but not in divorce; and a gift is complete only by taking possession, which only happens by delivery; similarly if she is prompted to khul' and she does not know, it is said the khul' is valid by her acceptance, and the chosen is what we mentioned; similarly if the debtor prompted the creditor to release of the debt in a language the creditor does not know, he is not released. In al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol.3 p.295. Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. And like marriage, unlike sale and release: they are not valid if the meaning is not known, as in al-Khaniyya. And it is stated that divorce of the joking, playing, and mistaken person occurs as we previously mentioned, but only judicially; as for between him and Allah, it does not occur for the mistaken one. And what is in al-Khulasa that the divorce of the mistaken occurs, meaning judicially, as evidence that he said afterward: if it were manumission, he would be believed religiously because there is no difference between manumission and divorce; and that is apparent from the statement of the Imam in al-Khaniyya contrary to Abu Yusuf; and there is no disagreement that the vowed (manzdhur) is binding; and there is no disagreement that if disbelief runs on his tongue mistakenly he does not become a disbeliever, as in al-Khaniyya; and similarly if he utters it without knowing its meaning, it occurs only judicially, as evidence from al-Khulasa: she said to her husband 'recite to me: I am observing 'idda, you are divorced three times' and he did, she is divorced three times judicially not between him and Allah if the husband did not know and did not intend, unlike the joker, for it occurs upon him judicially and religiously because he is defiant with the word, so he deserves severity. And what is in al-Khulasa attributed to al-Asl: he had two wives, Zaynab and Amrah; he said 'O Zaynab' and Amrah answered; he said 'you are divorced three times,' then the one who answered is divorced; if he said 'I intended Zaynab,' this one is divorced by indication and that one by confession. That is carried on judicially; as for religiously, it does not occur for either of them, as in al-Hawi attributed to al-Jami' al-Saghir: if he intended to say 'Zaynab is divorced' and his tongue slipped to 'Amrah', on which one does divorce fall? He said: judicially the one named is divorced; between him and Allah neither is divorced; as for one, he did not intend her; as for the other, because if she were divorced, it would be by mere intention. He said in Fath al-Qadir: as for what is narrated from Nasir from them that whoever intends to speak and divorce runs on his tongue, it occurs religiously and judicially, that is not relied upon. The conclusion is that their saying 'explicit (divorce) does not need intention' is only judicially; as for religiously, the fatwa is upon it, as stated in Hibat al-Nawazil. His statement: 'analogized to marriage.' Al-Ramli said: what Qadi Khan mentioned in the book of marriage in the first chapter requires analogy of marriage to divorce and manumission, not the reverse; for his statement after the discussion on them: 'When the answer is known in divorce and manumission, the answer in marriage should be likewise,' so consult it. His statement: 'so it does not occur for the mistaken one.' He restricted it because the divorce of the joker and player occurs religiously as well, as will come shortly. The issue also preceded at the statement of the matn: 'even under compulsion,' and there is also some discrepancy between al-Khaniyya and al-Bazzaziyya. His statement: 'As for religiously, it does not occur for either of them,' etc. There is consideration; what appears is that it occurs for the answering wife judicially and religiously because he addressed the divorce to her, and for Zaynab only judicially, as indicated by the reasoning of the original. But what is in al-Hawi has no indication and address, merely naming without intention. Ponder his statement: (The conclusion that their saying 'explicit does not need intention' is only judicially) this is specific to the mistaken one; as for the joker, he does not need it at all. What the author mentioned here follows what he verified in Fath al-Qadir, which is also verified in al-Tahrir: then from the establishment of the ruling of explicit without intention is its running on his tongue mistakenly in the manner of 'subhanallah' and 'give me water' [but] the intention of the explicit with...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the effect of ignorance, mistake (mukhti'), joking (hazil), and trickery (talqin) on the validity of divorce (talaq). The author, Ibn Nujaym, synthesizes multiple authorities, distinguishing between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) validity.

The passage opens with a ruling about a wife's recourse when her husband denies pronouncing an irrevocable divorce: she may take him to the qadi, and if she lacks evidence, he may be made to swear. No distinction is made between one or three irrevocable divorces. Then, a controversial issue: if she knows the divorce is final, can she kill him to repel intercourse? Two views exist, but the fatwa is that she cannot. If she does, she must do so with medicine; a weapon would require retaliation (qisas). She cannot kill herself but must ransom herself or flee. He cannot kill her either.

The core discussion begins with the requirement of knowledge. The Hanafi position in Zahir al-Riwayah is that if a husband is prompted to say 'you are divorced' without understanding the meaning, the divorce occurs judicially (qada'an) but not religiously (diyanatan). This is because the word is explicit (sarih), and explicit divorce is effective without intention in court, but in the sight of Allah, intention matters. However, the scholars of Uzjand held a contrary fatwa: it does not occur at all, to protect people's marriages from deception. This is a Nawadir position given weight in practical fatwa.

The same principle applies to manumission, tadbir, and release of dower. The passage then examines conditional phrases: if a husband says 'do not go out except with my permission, because I have sworn by divorce', and the wife goes out, divorce does not occur because the oath was not explicitly linked to her. But if custom dictates otherwise, it occurs as an implied condition. Then, the interpretation of figurative expressions: if he says 'you are divorced from my forearms' or 'from this work', the ruling depends on whether the word can carry that meaning in 'urf.

A detailed report from al-Tatarakhaniyya recounts that in Uzjand, the qadi and jurists agreed not to give fatwa for the occurrence of talaq in cases of talqin (trickery). This is extended to khul' and ibra' as well. The distinction is that sale and purchase require consent (rida), which is absent if the person does not understand; but talaq is an unilateral repudiation that does not require consent, so it is more strictly applied.

Moving to mistake: the speaker who mistakenly utters talaq incurs it judicially but not religiously. This is analogous to manumission. However, the joker (hazil) and the one playing (la'ib) incur it both judicially and religiously because they are 'mukabir' (defiant) – their utterance is willful. The passage then presents a scenario: a wife tells her husband to recite a phrase including 'you are divorced three times'; if he recites it without knowing or intending, divorce occurs only judicially. Another scenario: a man calls one wife but another answers; if he says 'you are divorced', the answering wife is divorced judicially, but religiously neither is divorced if he did not intend either. This is based on al-Hawi and al-Jami' al-Saghir.

The conclusion is that the absence of need for intention in explicit talaq applies only judicially. Religiously, intention is required. This is the operative (mufta bihi) position. The passage ends by correcting a possible misinterpretation: the ruling is specific to the mistaken person; the joker does not get this leniency.

The authority weight: The core rulings are from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad). The fatwa of the scholars of Uzjand is from Nawadir, but it is cited as the practical fatwa. The distinction between qada' and diyanah is central to Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'an): Judicially: the ruling issued by a qadi in court, which has legal effect outwardly.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religiously: the ruling between the person and Allah, based on intention and truth. A person may be divorced in court but not before Allah if he did not intend.
مخطئ (mukhti'): Mistaken one: a person who utters divorce unintentionally, either by slip of the tongue or without knowledge.
هازل (hazil): Joker: one who utters divorce in jest, fully aware of what he says but not intending divorce. His divorce is effective both judicially and religiously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi, what is the religious (diyanah) status of a mistaken (mukhti') divorce?
A) It is valid both judicially and religiously.
B) It is valid judicially but not religiously.
C) It is not valid at all.
D) It is valid religiously only if the couple reconciles.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, explicit talaq does not require intention for judicial effect, but religious validity depends on intent.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Trickery in Divorce (Talqin): The Fatwa of the Scholars of Uzjand
Ruling on trickery in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on talqin (trickery) in divorce and identify the mufta bihi as the Uzjandi position that the divorce does not occur at all.

Core Idea
When a husband is tricked into uttering divorce without knowledge, the mufta bihi is that it does not occur at all, neither judicially nor religiously.

The issue of a wife tricking her husband into pronouncing divorce raises questions about the role of knowledge and consent in talaq. This lesson explores two positions within the Hanafi school and the practical fatwa that protects marriages from deception.

Guided Path
1
Define talqin
Talqin is when someone prompts another to utter a word of divorce without the speaker understanding its meaning. The typical scenario is a wife telling her husband in Arabic 'say: anti taliq' and he repeats it without knowing what it means.
This is different from the mukhti' (slip of the tongue) because here the utterance is prompted.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah view
According to the transmitted texts of Imam Muhammad, the divorce occurs judicially (qada'an) because the word is sarih (explicit) and does not require intention in court. However, it does not occur religiously (diyanatan).
This is the view of the early authorities, recorded in al-Bada'i' and al-Bazzaziyya.
3
State the Uzjandi view
The scholars of Uzjand (mashayikh Uzjand) held that the divorce does not occur at all (la yaqa' aslan), neither judicially nor religiously. Their reasoning is to protect people's properties (marriages) from being invalidated through deception.
This is an istihsani departure from the literal application of the rule.
4
Identify the istihsan reasoning
The Uzjandis argued that implementing the literal rule would allow deceptive practices to destroy marriages, which contradicts the maqasid of shari'ah. The principle 'sawanan li amlak al-nas' (protection of people's properties) overrides the general rule that sarih talaq does not require knowledge.
This is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The later fatwa works (al-Tatarakhaniyya, al-Bazzaziyya) state that the Uzjandi view is given as fatwa. This is the operative position for qadis and muftis.
The passage cites al-Tatarakhaniyya: 'اتفقت آراؤنا أنه لا يفتى بوقوع الطلاق'.

أيضاً بين الخانية والبزازية. قوله : (أما في الديانة فلا يقع على واحدة منهما الخ فيه نظر والذي يظهر وقوعه على المجيبة قضاء وديانة لأنه خاطبها بالطلاق وعلى زينب قضاء فقط كما هو مفاد تعليل الأصل، وأما ما
