هنا تبع فيه ما حققه في فتح القدير وهو ما حققه أيضاً في التحرير فقال : ثم من ثبوت حكم الصريح بلا نية جريانه على لسانه غلطاً في نحو سبحان الله واسقني أما قصد الصريح مع
She can have him swear. And thus the commentators restricted. And it is mentioned in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Uzjandi that she raises the matter to the judge; if she has no evidence, she can have him swear; if he swears, the sin is upon him. There is no difference between the irrevocable divorce of one and three. And is it permissible for her to kill him if he wants intercourse after she knows of the irrevocable divorce? There are two sayings, and the fatwa is that she cannot kill him; if it is said she can kill him, she kills him with medicine; if she kills him with a weapon, retaliation is required. And she cannot kill herself; she must ransom herself with wealth or flee. And he cannot kill her if she is forbidden to him, nor can he get rid of her. That whenever she flees, he returns her by sorcery. Due to the whole in Sharh al-Manzoomah by Ibn al-Shihnah. And it will come in the section on what makes a divorced woman permissible that whether she can marry another in his absence if she knows of the irrevocability and he denies. He said in al-Misbah: al-wathaq with fat-h the waw and kasr is the fetter, its plural wuthuq like ribat and rabt. And it benefits that its non-dependence on intention means that knowledge of its meaning is not required; if she prompts him with the word of divorce and he utters it without knowing its meaning, it occurs judicially but not religiously. And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all, to protect people's properties from loss due to deception, as in al-Bada'i. Similarly in al-Bazzaziyya. And manumission, tadbir, and release of dower are like divorce as in al-Bazzaziyya. And divorce and what is with it are analogized to his addressing his wife and not adding it to her; it is like what passed from al-Bazzaziyya of his saying: 'Do not go out except with my permission, for I have sworn by divorce'; if she goes out, it does not occur because he did not mention his oath by her divorce; and if the custom is not that, then the apparent is occurrence because it is like 'if you do so, you are divorced' as passed from al-Fath. So his statement afterward 'from my forearms' is like his statement 'from this work'; ponder his statement: (He is not believed regarding the word 'work'). He said in al-Fath: because divorce is for removing a restriction, and she is not restricted by work, so the word does not carry that meaning; from Abu Hanifa, he is believed because it is used for release, as if he said 'you are released from work'; and he also reasoned its occurrence if he mentioned the number, thinking he divorced, then connected the word 'work' as a correction, unlike if he connected the word 'wathaq' where he is believed judicially because it is used for it rarely. His statement: (And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all). He said in al-Tatarakhaniyya: it is narrated from al-Qadi al-Imam Mahmud al-Uzjandi regarding one whose wife prompted him to divorce and he divorced not knowing that: he said this issue occurred in Uzjand and I consulted my companions and our opinions agreed that fatwa should not be given for the occurrence of divorce to protect people's properties from invalidation by a type of deception. And if she prompted him to take khul' from her for her dower and 'idda maintenance and she took khul', and he divorced her, some scholars said it is valid but if the husband does not accept it is not valid; some said it is not valid and that is given as fatwa. And he said in al-Bazzaziyya elsewhere: She prompted him to divorce in Arabic and he does not know, or manumission or tadbir, or the husband prompted her to release of dower and 'idda maintenance in Arabic and she does not know, al-Faqih or al-Layth said: it does not occur religiously. And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all to protect people's properties from invalidation by deception, and like if he sells or buys in Arabic and he does not know; and some distinguished between sale and purchase and divorce, manumission, khul' and gift considering that consent has effect in the existence of sale but not in divorce; and a gift is complete only by taking possession, which only happens by delivery; similarly if she is prompted to khul' and she does not know, it is said the khul' is valid by her acceptance, and the chosen is what we mentioned; similarly if the debtor prompted the creditor to release of the debt in a language the creditor does not know, he is not released. In al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol.3 p.295. Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. And like marriage, unlike sale and release: they are not valid if the meaning is not known, as in al-Khaniyya. And it is stated that divorce of the joking, playing, and mistaken person occurs as we previously mentioned, but only judicially; as for between him and Allah, it does not occur for the mistaken one. And what is in al-Khulasa that the divorce of the mistaken occurs, meaning judicially, as evidence that he said afterward: if it were manumission, he would be believed religiously because there is no difference between manumission and divorce; and that is apparent from the statement of the Imam in al-Khaniyya contrary to Abu Yusuf; and there is no disagreement that the vowed (manzdhur) is binding; and there is no disagreement that if disbelief runs on his tongue mistakenly he does not become a disbeliever, as in al-Khaniyya; and similarly if he utters it without knowing its meaning, it occurs only judicially, as evidence from al-Khulasa: she said to her husband 'recite to me: I am observing 'idda, you are divorced three times' and he did, she is divorced three times judicially not between him and Allah if the husband did not know and did not intend, unlike the joker, for it occurs upon him judicially and religiously because he is defiant with the word, so he deserves severity. And what is in al-Khulasa attributed to al-Asl: he had two wives, Zaynab and Amrah; he said 'O Zaynab' and Amrah answered; he said 'you are divorced three times,' then the one who answered is divorced; if he said 'I intended Zaynab,' this one is divorced by indication and that one by confession. That is carried on judicially; as for religiously, it does not occur for either of them, as in al-Hawi attributed to al-Jami' al-Saghir: if he intended to say 'Zaynab is divorced' and his tongue slipped to 'Amrah', on which one does divorce fall? He said: judicially the one named is divorced; between him and Allah neither is divorced; as for one, he did not intend her; as for the other, because if she were divorced, it would be by mere intention. He said in Fath al-Qadir: as for what is narrated from Nasir from them that whoever intends to speak and divorce runs on his tongue, it occurs religiously and judicially, that is not relied upon. The conclusion is that their saying 'explicit (divorce) does not need intention' is only judicially; as for religiously, the fatwa is upon it, as stated in Hibat al-Nawazil. His statement: 'analogized to marriage.' Al-Ramli said: what Qadi Khan mentioned in the book of marriage in the first chapter requires analogy of marriage to divorce and manumission, not the reverse; for his statement after the discussion on them: 'When the answer is known in divorce and manumission, the answer in marriage should be likewise,' so consult it. His statement: 'so it does not occur for the mistaken one.' He restricted it because the divorce of the joker and player occurs religiously as well, as will come shortly. The issue also preceded at the statement of the matn: 'even under compulsion,' and there is also some discrepancy between al-Khaniyya and al-Bazzaziyya. His statement: 'As for religiously, it does not occur for either of them,' etc. There is consideration; what appears is that it occurs for the answering wife judicially and religiously because he addressed the divorce to her, and for Zaynab only judicially, as indicated by the reasoning of the original. But what is in al-Hawi has no indication and address, merely naming without intention. Ponder his statement: (The conclusion that their saying 'explicit does not need intention' is only judicially) this is specific to the mistaken one; as for the joker, he does not need it at all. What the author mentioned here follows what he verified in Fath al-Qadir, which is also verified in al-Tahrir: then from the establishment of the ruling of explicit without intention is its running on his tongue mistakenly in the manner of 'subhanallah' and 'give me water' [but] the intention of the explicit with...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the effect of ignorance, mistake (mukhti'), joking (hazil), and trickery (talqin) on the validity of divorce (talaq). The author, Ibn Nujaym, synthesizes multiple authorities, distinguishing between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) validity.

The passage opens with a ruling about a wife's recourse when her husband denies pronouncing an irrevocable divorce: she may take him to the qadi, and if she lacks evidence, he may be made to swear. No distinction is made between one or three irrevocable divorces. Then, a controversial issue: if she knows the divorce is final, can she kill him to repel intercourse? Two views exist, but the fatwa is that she cannot. If she does, she must do so with medicine; a weapon would require retaliation (qisas). She cannot kill herself but must ransom herself or flee. He cannot kill her either.

The core discussion begins with the requirement of knowledge. The Hanafi position in Zahir al-Riwayah is that if a husband is prompted to say 'you are divorced' without understanding the meaning, the divorce occurs judicially (qada'an) but not religiously (diyanatan). This is because the word is explicit (sarih), and explicit divorce is effective without intention in court, but in the sight of Allah, intention matters. However, the scholars of Uzjand held a contrary fatwa: it does not occur at all, to protect people's marriages from deception. This is a Nawadir position given weight in practical fatwa.

The same principle applies to manumission, tadbir, and release of dower. The passage then examines conditional phrases: if a husband says 'do not go out except with my permission, because I have sworn by divorce', and the wife goes out, divorce does not occur because the oath was not explicitly linked to her. But if custom dictates otherwise, it occurs as an implied condition. Then, the interpretation of figurative expressions: if he says 'you are divorced from my forearms' or 'from this work', the ruling depends on whether the word can carry that meaning in 'urf.

A detailed report from al-Tatarakhaniyya recounts that in Uzjand, the qadi and jurists agreed not to give fatwa for the occurrence of talaq in cases of talqin (trickery). This is extended to khul' and ibra' as well. The distinction is that sale and purchase require consent (rida), which is absent if the person does not understand; but talaq is an unilateral repudiation that does not require consent, so it is more strictly applied.

Moving to mistake: the speaker who mistakenly utters talaq incurs it judicially but not religiously. This is analogous to manumission. However, the joker (hazil) and the one playing (la'ib) incur it both judicially and religiously because they are 'mukabir' (defiant) – their utterance is willful. The passage then presents a scenario: a wife tells her husband to recite a phrase including 'you are divorced three times'; if he recites it without knowing or intending, divorce occurs only judicially. Another scenario: a man calls one wife but another answers; if he says 'you are divorced', the answering wife is divorced judicially, but religiously neither is divorced if he did not intend either. This is based on al-Hawi and al-Jami' al-Saghir.

The conclusion is that the absence of need for intention in explicit talaq applies only judicially. Religiously, intention is required. This is the operative (mufta bihi) position. The passage ends by correcting a possible misinterpretation: the ruling is specific to the mistaken person; the joker does not get this leniency.

The authority weight: The core rulings are from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad). The fatwa of the scholars of Uzjand is from Nawadir, but it is cited as the practical fatwa. The distinction between qada' and diyanah is central to Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
تلقين (talqin): Trickery or prompting: when someone is made to utter a word of divorce without knowing its meaning.
صيانة لأملاك الناس (sawanan li amlak al-nas): Protection of people's properties: a juristic principle used by the Uzjandis to prevent divorce in cases of trickery, preserving marital rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi) in the text, what is the ruling when a husband is tricked into uttering divorce without understanding the meaning?
A) The divorce occurs both judicially and religiously.
B) The divorce occurs judicially but not religiously.
C) The divorce does not occur at all, neither judicially nor religiously.
D) The divorce occurs only if the man intended it.

Key Takeaway
In cases of trickery (talqin), the Hanafi fatwa protects the marriage by ruling that no divorce occurs, overriding the literal application of the sarih rule.

Module 9 · Lesson 111

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
Applying the Ignorance Principle to Manumission, Khul', and Release
Extension of ignorance ruling to other legal acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the same ruling on ignorance to manumission, tadbir, and ibra', and distinguish talaq from contracts like sale that require consent.

Core Idea
Like divorce, manumission and release of dower do not require understanding; but sale and purchase require consent.

The Hanafi school extends the talqin ruling to other unilateral dispositions: manumission, tadbir, and release of dower. However, bilateral contracts like sale and purchase require mutual consent and are treated differently. This lesson explores the analogical reasoning and practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the analogy between talaq and other acts
The passage states that the same rule applies to manumission (itq), tadbir (deferred manumission), and ibra' (release of dower). If the husband is tricked into uttering these without understanding, the ruling follows the same pattern.
These are unilateral dispositions (ikhtiyariyat) that do not require acceptance.
2
Differentiate contracts requiring consent
Sale (bay') and purchase (shira') require the consent (rida) of both parties, which includes understanding the transaction. Therefore, if a person is tricked into a sale without understanding, it is invalid (bati or fasid).
The presence of consent is a condition for the validity of such contracts.
3
Examine the khul' ambiguity
The passage mentions that some scholars hold that khul' (divorce by redemption) follows the rule of talaq, while others distinguish because khul' requires the wife's acceptance, which implies consent. The mufta bihi in the text is that khul' is not valid when the wife is tricked without understanding.
The chosen view is that khul' does not occur at all in talqin, similar to divorce.
4
Consider the ibra' from debt case
If a debtor prompts his creditor to release him from debt in a language the creditor does not understand, the release is not valid. This is because ibra' is a unilateral disposition, but the creditor's consent (even if tacit) requires understanding.
The ruling may differ if the creditor voluntarily accepts without understanding.
5
Apply the underlying principle
The common principle is that unilateral acts (talaq, itq) are effective with mere utterance in court, but for bilateral acts (sale, khul'), the consent element is essential and requires knowledge.
This distinction is based on the nature of the act: whether it requires acceptance (qabul) or not.

She can have him swear. And thus the commentators restricted. And it is mentioned in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Uzjandi that she raises the matter to the judge; if she has no evidence, she can have him swear; if he swears, the sin is upon him. There is no difference between the irrevocable divorce of one and three. And is it permissible for her to kill him if he wants intercourse after she knows of the irrevocable divorce? There are two sayings, and the fatwa is that she cannot kill him; if it is said she can kill him, she kills him with medicine; if she kills him with a weapon, retaliation is required. And she cannot kill herself; she must ransom herself with wealth or flee. And he cannot kill her if she is forbidden to him, nor can he get rid of her. That whenever she flees, he returns her by sorcery. Due to the whole in Sharh al-Manzoomah by Ibn al-Shihnah. And it will come in the section on what makes a divorced woman permissible that whether she can marry another in his absence if she knows of the irrevocability and he denies. He said in al-Misbah: al-wathaq with fat-h the waw and kasr is the fetter, its plural wuthuq like ribat and rabt. And it benefits that its non-dependence on intention means that knowledge of its meaning is not required; if she prompts him with the word of divorce and he utters it without knowing its meaning, it occurs judicially but not religiously. And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all, to protect people's properties from loss due to deception, as in al-Bada'i. Similarly in al-Bazzaziyya. And manumission, tadbir, and release of dower are like divorce as in al-Bazzaziyya. And divorce and what is with it are analogized to his addressing his wife and not adding it to her; it is like what passed from al-Bazzaziyya of his saying: 'Do not go out except with my permission, for I have sworn by divorce'; if she goes out, it does not occur because he did not mention his oath by her divorce; and if the custom is not that, then the apparent is occurrence because it is like 'if you do so, you are divorced' as passed from al-Fath. So his statement afterward 'from my forearms' is like his statement 'from this work'; ponder his statement: (He is not believed regarding the word 'work'). He said in al-Fath: because divorce is for removing a restriction, and she is not restricted by work, so the word does not carry that meaning; from Abu Hanifa, he is believed because it is used for release, as if he said 'you are released from work'; and he also reasoned its occurrence if he mentioned the number, thinking he divorced, then connected the word 'work' as a correction, unlike if he connected the word 'wathaq' where he is believed judicially because it is used for it rarely. His statement: (And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all). He said in al-Tatarakhaniyya: it is narrated from al-Qadi al-Imam Mahmud al-Uzjandi regarding one whose wife prompted him to divorce and he divorced not knowing that: he said this issue occurred in Uzjand and I consulted my companions and our opinions agreed that fatwa should not be given for the occurrence of divorce to protect people's properties from invalidation by a type of deception. And if she prompted him to take khul' from her for her dower and 'idda maintenance and she took khul', and he divorced her, some scholars said it is valid but if the husband does not accept it is not valid; some said it is not valid and that is given as fatwa. And he said in al-Bazzaziyya elsewhere: She prompted him to divorce in Arabic and he does not know, or manumission or tadbir, or the husband prompted her to release of dower and 'idda maintenance in Arabic and she does not know, al-Faqih or al-Layth said: it does not occur religiously. And the scholars of Uzjand said: it does not occur at all to protect people's properties from invalidation by deception, and like if he sells or buys in Arabic and he does not know; and some distinguished between sale and purchase and divorce, manumission, khul' and gift considering that consent has effect in the existence of sale but not in divorce; and a gift is complete only by taking possession, which only happens by delivery; similarly if she is prompted to khul' and she does not know, it is said the khul' is valid by her acceptance, and the chosen is what we mentioned; similarly if the debtor prompted the creditor to release of the debt in a language the creditor does not know, he is not released. In al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol.3 p.295. Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. And like marriage, unlike sale and release: they are not valid if the meaning is not known, as in al-Khaniyya. And it is stated that divorce of the joking, playing, and mistaken person occurs as we previously mentioned, but only judicially; as for between him and Allah, it does not occur for the mistaken one. And what is in al-Khulasa that the divorce of the mistaken occurs, meaning judicially, as evidence that he said afterward: if it were manumission, he would be believed religiously because there is no difference between manumission and divorce; and that is apparent from the statement of the Imam in al-Khaniyya contrary to Abu Yusuf; and there is no disagreement that the vowed (manzdhur) is binding; and there is no disagreement that if disbelief runs on his tongue mistakenly he does not become a disbeliever, as in al-Khaniyya; and similarly if he utters it without knowing its meaning, it occurs only judicially, as evidence from al-Khulasa: she said to her husband 'recite to me: I am observing 'idda, you are divorced three times' and he did, she is divorced three times judicially not between him and Allah if the husband did not know and did not intend, unlike the joker, for it occurs upon him judicially and religiously because he is defiant with the word, so he deserves severity. And what is in al-Khulasa attributed to al-Asl: he had two wives, Zaynab and Amrah; he said 'O Zaynab' and Amrah answered; he said 'you are divorced three times,' then the one who answered is divorced; if he said 'I intended Zaynab,' this one is divorced by indication and that one by confession. That is carried on judicially; as for religiously, it does not occur for either of them, as in al-Hawi attributed to al-Jami' al-Saghir: if he intended to say 'Zaynab is divorced' and his tongue slipped to 'Amrah', on which one does divorce fall? He said: judicially the one named is divorced; between him and Allah neither is divorced; as for one, he did not intend her; as for the other, because if she were divorced, it would be by mere intention. He said in Fath al-Qadir: as for what is narrated from Nasir from them that whoever intends to speak and divorce runs on his tongue, it occurs religiously and judicially, that is not relied upon. The conclusion is that their saying 'explicit (divorce) does not need intention' is only judicially; as for religiously, the fatwa is upon it, as stated in Hibat al-Nawazil. His statement: 'analogized to marriage.' Al-Ramli said: what Qadi Khan mentioned in the book of marriage in the first chapter requires analogy of marriage to divorce and manumission, not the reverse; for his statement after the discussion on them: 'When the answer is known in divorce and manumission, the answer in marriage should be likewise,' so consult it. His statement: 'so it does not occur for the mistaken one.' He restricted it because the divorce of the joker and player occurs religiously as well, as will come shortly. The issue also preceded at the statement of the matn: 'even under compulsion,' and there is also some discrepancy between al-Khaniyya and al-Bazzaziyya. His statement: 'As for religiously, it does not occur for either of them,' etc. There is consideration; what appears is that it occurs for the answering wife judicially and religiously because he addressed the divorce to her, and for Zaynab only judicially, as indicated by the reasoning of the original. But what is in al-Hawi has no indication and address, merely naming without intention. Ponder his statement: (The conclusion that their saying 'explicit does not need intention' is only judicially) this is specific to the mistaken one; as for the joker, he does not need it at all. What the author mentioned here follows what he verified in Fath al-Qadir, which is also verified in al-Tahrir: then from the establishment of the ruling of explicit without intention is its running on his tongue mistakenly in the manner of 'subhanallah' and 'give me water' [but] the intention of the explicit with...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the effect of ignorance, mistake (mukhti'), joking (hazil), and trickery (talqin) on the validity of divorce (talaq). The author, Ibn Nujaym, synthesizes multiple authorities, distinguishing between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) validity.

The passage opens with a ruling about a wife's recourse when her husband denies pronouncing an irrevocable divorce: she may take him to the qadi, and if she lacks evidence, he may be made to swear. No distinction is made between one or three irrevocable divorces. Then, a controversial issue: if she knows the divorce is final, can she kill him to repel intercourse? Two views exist, but the fatwa is that she cannot. If she does, she must do so with medicine; a weapon would require retaliation (qisas). She cannot kill herself but must ransom herself or flee. He cannot kill her either.

The core discussion begins with the requirement of knowledge. The Hanafi position in Zahir al-Riwayah is that if a husband is prompted to say 'you are divorced' without understanding the meaning, the divorce occurs judicially (qada'an) but not religiously (diyanatan). This is because the word is explicit (sarih), and explicit divorce is effective without intention in court, but in the sight of Allah, intention matters. However, the scholars of Uzjand held a contrary fatwa: it does not occur at all, to protect people's marriages from deception. This is a Nawadir position given weight in practical fatwa.

The same principle applies to manumission, tadbir, and release of dower. The passage then examines conditional phrases: if a husband says 'do not go out except with my permission, because I have sworn by divorce', and the wife goes out, divorce does not occur because the oath was not explicitly linked to her. But if custom dictates otherwise, it occurs as an implied condition. Then, the interpretation of figurative expressions: if he says 'you are divorced from my forearms' or 'from this work', the ruling depends on whether the word can carry that meaning in 'urf.

A detailed report from al-Tatarakhaniyya recounts that in Uzjand, the qadi and jurists agreed not to give fatwa for the occurrence of talaq in cases of talqin (trickery). This is extended to khul' and ibra' as well. The distinction is that sale and purchase require consent (rida), which is absent if the person does not understand; but talaq is an unilateral repudiation that does not require consent, so it is more strictly applied.

Moving to mistake: the speaker who mistakenly utters talaq incurs it judicially but not religiously. This is analogous to manumission. However, the joker (hazil) and the one playing (la'ib) incur it both judicially and religiously because they are 'mukabir' (defiant) – their utterance is willful. The passage then presents a scenario: a wife tells her husband to recite a phrase including 'you are divorced three times'; if he recites it without knowing or intending, divorce occurs only judicially. Another scenario: a man calls one wife but another answers; if he says 'you are divorced', the answering wife is divorced judicially, but religiously neither is divorced if he did not intend either. This is based on al-Hawi and al-Jami' al-Saghir.

The conclusion is that the absence of need for intention in explicit talaq applies only judicially. Religiously, intention is required. This is the operative (mufta bihi) position. The passage ends by correcting a possible misinterpretation: the ruling is specific to the mistaken person; the joker does not get this leniency.

The authority weight: The core rulings are from Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad). The fatwa of the scholars of Uzjand is from Nawadir, but it is cited as the practical fatwa. The distinction between qada' and diyanah is central to Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
رضا (rida): Consent: the agreement of a party to a contract, which requires understanding of the subject matter and terms.
عتاق (itaq): Manumission: freeing a slave. It is a unilateral act that does not require the slave's consent.
إبراء (ibra'): Release: a unilateral discharge of a debt or obligation. It can be from dower or from a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a sale conducted in Arabic when the buyer does not understand Arabic?
A) The sale is valid because the words were exchanged.
B) The sale is not valid because consent requires understanding.
C) The sale is valid only if the seller knew the buyer did not understand.
D) The sale is valid but the buyer may rescind.

Key Takeaway
Unilateral acts (talaq, itq, tadbir) and bilateral contracts (sale, khul') are treated differently regarding the effect of ignorance; the former may be effective in court even without understanding, but fatwa often protects against deception.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Ruling on Saying 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' and the Role of Intention and Grammar
Analyzes the legal effect of the phrase 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' and grammatical factors determining the number of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of divorces that occur when a husband says 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' with or without specific intention, and explain the grammatical factors (raf' vs nasb of 'thalath') that affect the ruling.

Core Idea
The number of divorces depends on intention and grammar: one divorce unless three intended, but the case of 'thalath' and the definite article can change the outcome.

In this lesson, we analyze the Hanafi ruling on a husband's statement 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' (you are divorce and divorce) and its variants. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the interplay between the speaker's intention and the grammatical structure of the utterance, including a famous fatwa from Imam Abu Yusuf and the grammarian al-Kisa'i. You will learn how the Hanafi school harmonizes linguistic precision with legal intent in matters of divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the default ruling for 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' when no number is intended. According to the passage, it is one revocable divorce.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the view of Fakhr al-Islam (only one divorce regardless of intention) with the majority (each word can effect divorce, so two if intended).
The mufta bihi is the view of Fakhr al-Islam according to al-Nahr.
3
Analyze the grammatical story
Follow the case of the poet's verse and the fatwa of al-Kisa'i: raf' of 'thalath' yields one divorce, nasb yields three.
Al-Kisa'i's ruling is based on the grammatical function of the case ending.
4
Evaluate the linguistic arguments
Examine Ibn Hisham's analysis of the definite article 'al-' in 'al-Talaq' – possibilities: real genus (impossible), metaphorical genus (one divorce), prior reference (three divorces).
The choice between these interpretations affects the number of divorces.
5
Apply the rulings
Determine the number of divorces in given scenarios based on intention and grammatical form, e.g., 'Anti al-Thalath' yields no divorce because it describes the woman.
Practice with the tasks below.

لامرأته أنت مني بثلاث ونوى الطلاق طلقت لأنه نوى ما يحتمله وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لم يصدق إن كان في حالة مذاكرة الطلاق لأنه لا يحتمل الرد، ولو قال أنت بثلاث وأضمر. جعل قوله : (وأقول أن الصواب الخ) قال الرملي : قائله ابن هشام المذكور في كتابه «المغني» قوله : (وأما الرفع فلامتناع الجنس الحقيقي الجار والمجرور في قوله «فلامتناع» متعلق بما بعده وهو قوله بقي) فهو علة مقدمة على معلولها قوله : (آخره) مخالف لأوله أي قوله إن جواب محمد بناء على ما هو الظاهر» مخالف لقوله قبله إن العهد الذكري أظهر الاحتمالين فيقع ثلاث .
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. [The ruling:] It occurs as one revocable divorce without intention or if he intended one or two; if he intended three, then three. And if he adds with the مصدر (verbal noun) like its cancellation with the number, otherwise it would occur with 'Taliq' as one and with 'al-Talaq' as two when he intended three, so two would be binding by the مصدر, and they (the Hanafis) do not say with it. It is conditioned that he intended two by the total, because if he intended two by distribution, e.g., he means by his statement 'you are divorced once' and by 'al-Talaq' another, two occur, contrary to Fakhr al-Islam, because 'Taliq' is a descriptor and 'Talaq' is its مصدر, so only one revocable occurs. The first view is that each is suitable for effecting divorce, so it becomes like his saying 'you are divorced, divorced' and it is preferable over the statement of some 'divorced and divorced' because there is nothing in the speech indicating the waw. And the first was preferred in Fath al-Qadir because 'Talaqan' is accusative and it is not raised after the suitability of the word for multiplicity and the validity of intention with it except by ignoring the necessity of correct grammatical case in effecting divorce from the scholar and the ignorant. And in al-Mughni by Ibn Hisham from the first chapter of the discussion of the article (Tanbeeh): Al-Rashid wrote one night to judge Abu Yusuf asking him about the statement of the poet: 'If you are gentle, O Hind, then gentleness is more auspicious; and if you are harsh, O Hind, then harshness is more unlucky; So you are divorce, and divorce is a determined three; and whoever is harsh becomes [؟] and more unjust.' He said: What becomes binding on him if he raises 'three' and if he gives it accusative case? Abu Yusuf said: I thought this is a grammatical and juristic issue, and I do not feel safe from error if I speak in it with my opinion. So I came to al-Kisa'i while he was in his bed and asked him. He said: If he raises 'three', she is divorced once because he said 'you are divorce', then he informed that complete divorce is three. And if he gives it accusative, she is divorced three times because its meaning is 'you are divorced three times', and what is between them is a parenthetical sentence. So I wrote that to al-Rashid, and he sent me gifts, which I forwarded to al-Kisa'i. End summary. And I say: The correct view is that both the nominative and accusative are possible for the occurrence of three and for the occurrence of one. As for the nominative, because 'al' in 'al-Talaq' is either for the metaphorical genus, as you say 'Zayd is the man' meaning he is the considered man, or for the previous mental reference, like it in 'And Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' meaning 'this mentioned divorce is a determined three', and it cannot be for the real genus to avoid informing about the general by the specific, as one says 'the animal is man', which is false because not every animal is man and not every divorce is a determined thing. And 'three' [in accusative] then on the previous reference reading, three occur, and on the genus reading, one occurs as al-Kisa'i said. As for the accusative, it is possible to be a absolute object, in which case it necessitates the occurrence of three, because the meaning is 'you are divorced three times', and then there is an interjection with his statement 'and divorce is a determined thing', and it can be a حال from the implicit pronoun in 'determined thing', in which case the occurrence of three is not necessary because the meaning is 'divorce is a determined thing when it is three', so only what he intended occurs. This is what the meaning of this wording requires. And what this particular poet intended is three, because he later said: 'So separate from me with it if you are not kind, and there is no proceeding for a man after three.' End quote. (And the first was preferred in Fath al-Qadir; thus in the copies, the correct is the second, because the preference is for the speech of Fakhr al-Islam. And it is mentioned in al-Nahr that it is the preferred in the madhhab.) Similar to it, he said: three. And it was objected in Fath al-Qadir that after it being a mistake, it is far from knowing the station of ijtihad, because its condition is knowledge of Arabic and its styles, because ijtihad occurs in Arabic scriptural evidences. And what the reliable people narrated about this issue from the one who read the fatwa when it arrived contradicts that, and that al-Kisa'i sent it to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, and there is no involvement of Abu Yusuf at all nor al-Rashid. And the station of Abu Yusuf is greater than to need in such a construction, given his imamate, ijtihad, and proficiency in the implications of words. Then: 'Wa in takhruqi' with damma on the ra, is a present tense verb from 'kharaqa' with kasra, and 'al-kharq' with damma is the noun, which is the opposite of gentleness. And it is not hidden that the apparent in the accusative is it being an absolute object deputizing for the مصدر due to the paucity of benefit on intending that divorce is a determined thing if it is three. As for the nominative, it is due to the impossibility of real genus. It remains that it can be intended as metaphorical genus, so one occurs, or as prior mental reference, and it is the more apparent of the two possibilities, so three occur. Hence it appears from the poet that he intended it, as the last verse indicates. So the answer of Muhammad is based on what is apparent, as is required for one who takes the wording on its apparent meaning and does not pay attention to the possibility. End. And it is not hidden that the prior mental reference, since it is the more apparent of the two possibilities, it would have been fitting for Muhammad to answer with what it implies, so the speech of Ibn al-Humam later contradicts its beginning, as is not hidden. Then know that Ibn al-Sa'igh objected to Ibn Hisham in disallowing it being for real genus, by saying it is permissible for it to mean 'every collective' not 'every individual', so the meaning becomes 'the total individuals of divorce are three' not that the actual one is three. And al-Shumnni refuted it by saying that 'al' is not among its meanings the collective 'every', even if that is a meaning of 'kull'. And al-Damamin also objected to Ibn Hisham in considering 'three' as حال from the pronoun in 'determined thing' by saying that the speech is possible for the occurrence of three even on the reading of prior reference by making 'al' for prior reference. Al-Shumnni refuted him by saying that he only negated the necessity of three, which is true with the possibility of three. And al-Shumnni also objected to Ibn Hisham in that the accusative can be an absolute object necessitating three, by saying that it only necessitates it if it is an absolute object for the first divorce or for the second divorce and 'al' is for prior reference. But if it is an absolute object for the second divorce and 'al' is for genus, it does not necessitate that. End. And it is conditioned by his statement 'you are divorced', because if he said 'you are the three' and intended, it does not occur because 'the three' is an attribute of the woman, not an attribute of the implicit divorce, so he intended what his wording does not bear, so it is not valid. And if he said to his wife 'you are from me with three' and intended divorce, she is divorced because he intended what it bears. And if he said 'I did not intend divorce', he is not believed if he is in a state of discussing divorce because it does not accept rejection. And if he said 'you are with three' and implied... Made. His statement: (And I say the correct view etc.) al-Ramli said: Its speaker is Ibn Hisham mentioned in his book al-Mughni. His statement: (And as for the nominative, due to the impossibility of real genus) the prepositional phrase 'due to impossibility' is attached to what follows, which is his statement 'it remains') so it is a cause placed before its effect. His statement: (later) contradicts its beginning, i.e., his statement 'The answer of Muhammad is based on what is apparent' contradicts his previous statement that 'the prior mental reference is the more apparent of the two possibilities, so three occur'.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a man's statement to his wife: 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' without specifying a number. The base ruling says that if he does not intend a specific number, one revocable divorce (talqah raj'iyyah) occurs. However, if he intends three, then three occur. The author then discusses a variant where the man adds the word 'al-Talaq' after 'Anti Talaq'. The text explains that if he intends two divorces by the total phrase, two occur according to many Hanafis, contrary to Fakhr al-Islam who holds that 'Taliq' is an adjective and 'Talaq' is its verbal noun, so only one divorce occurs. The author prefers the view that each word is valid for effecting divorce, so it is like saying 'Anti Taliq Taliq' (you are divorced, divorced). Then the text presents a famous story involving Caliph al-Rashid, Imam Abu Yusuf, and the grammarian al-Kisa'i about a poet's verse that uses similar phrasing. The poet says: 'You are divorce, and divorce is a determined three.' The question is whether nominative or accusative case of 'three' changes the ruling. Al-Kisa'i opines that raising (nominative) yields one divorce, while nabbing (accusative) yields three. The commentator (Ibn Hisham) then provides a detailed grammatical analysis, exploring the different meanings of the definite article 'al-' (genus, metaphorical genus, prior reference) and how each affects the outcome. Later commentators criticize aspects of this analysis, and the passage ends with further specifications about related phrases. The lesson emphasizes that in Hanafi fiqh, the intention of the speaker and the grammatical construction of the utterance are both crucial in determining the number of divorces. The relied-upon position according to many is that of Fakhr al-Islam (preferring one divorce) as mentioned in al-Nahr, but the passage shows the depth of linguistic reasoning that Hanafi jurists employ.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-Talaq): The verbal noun (masdar) of divorce, used here as a substitute for the verbal noun of the statement 'Anti Taliq', and also as the definite noun 'al-Talaq' meaning divorce in a general or specified sense.
العهد الذكري (al-'Ahd al-Dhikri): The previous mention reference, where 'al-' refers to the divorce mentioned earlier in the same sentence (the one intended by 'Anti Talaq').
مجاز الجنس (Majaz al-Jins): Metaphorical genus, where 'al-' indicates the ideal or exemplary type, e.g., 'Zayd is the man' meaning he is the perfect man, not the entire genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says 'Anti al-Thalath' (you are the three) intending three divorces, what is the ruling?
A) Three divorces occur because he intended three
B) No divorce occurs because 'al-Thalath' describes the woman, not the divorce
C) One revocable divorce
D) Two divorces

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces from 'Anti Talaq wa al-Talaq' hinges on the grammatical case of 'thalath' and the interpretation of the definite article, demonstrating the Hanafi school's integration of Arabic linguistics into legal rulings.

Talaq — Divorce

Adding Talaq to the Woman's Whole Body or Parts
Ruling on talaq when added to the whole woman or expressions that denote the whole

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which expressions effect talaq when added to the woman's whole or parts, and apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing between common parts and specific limbs.

Core Idea
Talaq occurs when the husband uses expressions that conventionally denote the whole woman, but not when referring to a specific limb without such connotation.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wording of talaq can be explicit or metaphorical. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that when talaq is added to the woman's whole body or to expressions that stand for the whole, like 'neck' or 'soul', the talaq is effective. However, if he points to a specific part without it being a conventional expression for the whole, talaq does not occur. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying talaq rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify expressions denoting the whole woman
The passage lists literal expressions like 'Anti taliq' and metaphorical ones like 'neck', 'soul', 'body', 'trunk', 'private part', and 'face'. These are conventionally understood as standing for the whole woman.
Also includes common parts like half or third.
2
Distinguish between common parts and specific limbs
Adding talaq to a common part (e.g., half, third) is effective. For specific limbs (e.g., head, hand), talaq occurs only if the husband makes it an expression for the whole, such as by pointing or context.
The passage gives the example of 'this head' with pointing being valid, but 'this limb' without pointing is not.
3
Apply the rule: does the expression conventionally denote the whole?
If yes, talaq occurs. If no, then no talaq unless the husband intends to use it as an expression for the whole and the context supports that.
Words like 'belly', 'back', 'private part' (in some views), and 'blood' (in the most correct view) do not effect talaq.
4
Consider the role of intention
The passage states that if the husband says he meant the limb literally, he is not believed in court (qada'an) but is believed religiously (diyanatan). This shows that external expression takes precedence in legal rulings, but intention matters in the afterlife.
This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding ambiguous expressions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطلاق يقع كأنه قال أنت طالق بثلاث كذا في المحيط وظاهرة أن أنت مني بثلاث وأنت بثلاث بحذف مني سواء في كونه كناية وأما أنت الثلاث فليس بكناية . قوله : (وإن أضاف الطلاق إلى جملتها أو إلى ما يعبر به عنها كالرقبة والعنق والروح والبدن والجسد والفرج والوجه أو إلى جزء شائع منها كنصفها وثلثها تطلق) أراد بالإضافة إلى الجملة أن يكون بطريق الوضع كانت طالق وبما يعبر به عن الجملة بطريق التجوز كرقبتك، وإلا فالكل يعبر به عن الجملة. كذا في فتح القدير وذكر الشارح أن ما يضاف إلى الجملة أنت والروح والبدن والجسد وأما ما يعبر به عنها ما عداها والظاهر الأول كما لا يخفى. وأشار بالتعبير به عنها إلى أنه لا بد أن يقول مثلاً رقبتك طالق أما لو قال الرقبة منك طالق أو الوجه أو وضع يده على الرأس أو العنق وقال هذا العضو طالق لم يقع في الأصح لأنه لم يجعله عبارة عن الكل بل عن البعض بخلاف ما إذا لم يضع يده بل قال هذا الرأس طالق وأشار إلى رأس امرأته الصحيح أنه يقع كما لو قال رأسك هذا طالق، ولهذا لو قال لغيره بعت منك هذا الرأس بألف درهم وأشار إلى رأس عبده فقال المشتري قبلت جاز البيع. كذا في الخانية. وقيد بالرقبة وما بعدها لأنه لا يقع بالبطن والظهر والبضع والدم على الصحيح، ولهذا لو قال دمك حر لا يعتق، وقد صححوا صحة التكفل بالدم لما يقال دمه هدر أي نفسه فكان العرف جرى به في الكفالة دون العتق والطلاق وصحح في الجوهرة وقوع الطلاق. يقال ذهب دمه هدراً فحينئذ لا فرق بين الطلاق والكفالة، وتقييدهم الجزء بالشائع ليس للاحتراز عن المعين لما في الخلاصة : لو قال نصفك الأعلى طالق واحدة ونصفك الأسفل ثنتين فقد وقعت هذه المسألة ببخارى فأفتى بعضهم بوقوع الواحدة لأن الرأس في الأعلى، وبعضهم اعتبر الإضافتين لأن الفرج في الأسفل ا.هـ. وقد علم به أنه لو اقتصر على أحدهما وقعت واحدة اتفاقاً. وقد أطلق المصنف وقوع الطلاق بما ذكر فأفاد أنه يتوقف على النية، فلو قال أردت به العضو حقيقة لم يصدق قضاء ويصدق ديانة لكنه كيف
Talaq occurs, as if he said 'Anti taliq bi-thalath'. Thus in al-Muhit. And it is apparent that 'Anti minni bi-thalath' and 'Anti bi-thalath' with the omission of 'minni' are the same in being kinayah. As for 'Anti al-Thalath', it is not kinayah. His statement: (And if he adds talaq to her whole or to what expresses her whole, like the neck, the throat, the soul, the body, the trunk, the private part, and the face, or to a common part of her like her half or her third, she is divorced). As for what expresses her whole, it is other than these. And the first is apparent, as is not hidden. He indicated by 'by what expresses her whole' that he must say, for example, 'Raqabatuki taliq'. Contrary to if he did not place his hand, but said 'this head is taliq' and pointed to his wife's head; the correct view is that it occurs, just as if he said 'Ra'suki hadha taliq'. Hence, if he said to another, 'I sold to you this head for a thousand dirhams' and pointed to his slave's head, and the buyer said 'I accept', the sale is valid. Thus in al-Khaniyyah. He restricted it to the neck and what follows because it does not occur with the belly, back, private part, and blood in the most correct view. Hence, if he said 'damuki hurr', he is not freed. However, they have verified the validity of guaranteeing blood (kafalah) because it is said 'blood of him is waste' i.e., his life, so custom has applied it in kafalah but not in manumission and talaq. In al-Jawharah, it is verified that talaq occurs. It is said: 'His blood went waste' so then there is no difference between talaq and kafalah. Their restriction of the part to the common part is not to exclude the specified part, according to what is in al-Khulasah: If he said 'Your upper half is taliq with one and your lower half with two', this issue occurred in Bukhara. Some gave fatwa that one talaq occurs because the head is in the upper part; others considered both additions because the private part is in the lower part. End. It is known from this that if he confined himself to one of them, one talaq occurs by agreement. The author (of Kanz) has unconditionally stated the occurrence of talaq in what he mentioned, so it indicates that it depends on intention. If he said, 'I meant the limb literally', he is not believed in court but is believed religiously. However, how...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the validity of talaq when the husband utters a phrase that adds the talaq to the entire woman or to parts that conventionally represent the whole woman. The author states that talaq occurs if he says 'Anti taliq' (you are divorced) or uses metaphorical expressions that stand for the whole woman, such as 'neck', 'throat', 'soul', 'body', 'trunk', 'private part', or 'face'. It also occurs if he adds talaq to a common part like 'half' or 'third'. The reasoning is that such expressions are understood as referring to the whole person. However, if he specifically points to a particular limb without making it an expression for the whole, e.g., he says 'this limb is taliq' while placing his hand on it, talaq does not occur because he is only referring to that part. The exception is if he points without touching, as in 'this head', which is considered an expression for the whole. The passage also notes that some parts like belly, back, private part, and blood do not effect talaq, though there is a difference in the case of blood due to custom and its use in kafalah. The ruling is that the intention matters: if the husband says he meant the limb literally, his statement is not accepted in court (to avoid talaq) but is accepted religiously (between him and Allah). The passage emphasizes the importance of customary usage and metaphorical expressions in determining legal effects.

Key Terms
الجزء الشائع (al-juz' al-shayi'): A common part, like half or third, that represents a fraction of the whole woman.
الجزء المعين (al-juz' al-mu'ayyan): A specific limb, like the head or hand, that does not conventionally represent the whole woman.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that requires intention for talaq to occur, such as 'Anti minni bi-thalath'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No talaq occurs because the neck is a specific part.
B) One talaq occurs because the neck conventionally represents the whole woman.
C) Three talaqs occur because it is a kinayah.
D) Talaq occurs only if he points to the neck.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of talaq hinges on whether the husband's expression conventionally denotes the whole woman; common parts and metaphorical expressions like 'neck' or 'soul' effect talaq, while specific limbs like 'hand' do not unless used as an expression for the whole.

Classifying Divorce Expressions: Sarih and Kinayah
Explains when divorce occurs by adding it to whole body or parts thereof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a husband's divorce statement as sarih or kinayah based on Hanafi rules of custom, and determine whether intention is required for the divorce to occur in court.

Core Idea
Expressions that refer to the whole wife (e.g., neck) are sarih; parts like hand are kinayah—intention decides court enforcement.

In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of a divorce statement often depends on whether the words are explicitly about divorce or only imply it. This lesson unpacks the rules for adding divorce to body parts, where custom determines if a term stands for the whole person or just a limb. Understanding these categories is crucial for judges and scholars to correctly rule on the occurrence of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's exact expression
Listen to what the husband said: did he say 'your neck is divorced', 'your hand is divorced', 'your half is divorced', or something like 'your tooth is divorced'? The specific term matters.
Also note if he pointed or used gestures, as that can affect classification.
2
Classify the expression as whole-body, common part, or specific limb
Expressions like 'your body', 'your soul', 'your neck' are considered whole-body terms. 'Half of you' is a common (sha'i') part. 'Hand', 'foot', 'anus' are specific limbs. 'Tooth' or 'saliva' are parts that never represent the whole.
The Hanafi school relies on customary usage (urf) to decide.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on custom
If the term is customarily used to express the entire person (e.g., 'neck' in Arabic culture), it is treated as sarih (explicit). If it is not customarily used for the whole but is a general part (like hand), it is kinayah. If it is a part that never represents the whole (like tooth), it is neither.
Custom may vary by time and place; Hanafi jurists consider dominant practice.
4
Determine the legal effect
If sarih: divorce occurs in court without needing the husband's intention. If kinayah: divorce only occurs if the husband intended divorce (he is believed religiously but not in court). If neither: no divorce, even if he intended.
For common parts like half, if it can stand for the whole (e.g., upper half includes head), it is sarih.

الطلاق إلى جملتها أو إلى ما يعبر به عنها كالرقبة والعنق والروح والبدن والجسد والفرج
الطلاق يقع
كأنه قال أنت طالق بثلاث كذا في المحيط وظاهرة أن أنت مني بثلاث وأنت
بثلاث بحذف مني سواء في كونه كناية وأما أنت الثلاث فليس بكناية .
قوله : (وإن أضاف الطلاق إلى جملتها أو إلى ما يعبر به عنها كالرقبة والعنق والروح والبدن والجسد والفرج والوجه أو إلى جزء شائع منها كنصفها وثلثها تطلق) أراد بالإضافة إلى الجملة أن يكون بطريق الوضع كانت طالق وبما يعبر به عن الجملة بطريق التجوز كرقبتك، وإلا فالكل يعبر به عن الجملة. كذا في فتح القدير وذكر الشارح أن ما يضاف إلى الجملة أنت والروح والبدن والجسد وأما ما يعبر به عنها ما عداها والظاهر الأول كما لا يخفى. وأشار بالتعبير به عنها إلى أنه لا بد أن يقول مثلاً رقبتك طالق أما لو قال الرقبة منك طالق أو الوجه أو وضع يده على الرأس أو العنق وقال هذا العضو طالق لم يقع في الأصح لأنه لم يجعله عبارة عن الكل بل عن البعض بخلاف ما إذا لم يضع يده بل قال هذا الرأس طالق وأشار إلى رأس امرأته الصحيح أنه يقع كما لو قال رأسك هذا طالق، ولهذا لو قال لغيره بعت منك هذا الرأس بألف درهم وأشار إلى رأس عبده فقال المشتري قبلت جاز البيع. كذا في الخانية. وقيد بالرقبة وما بعدها لأنه لا يقع بالبطن والظهر والبضع والدم على الصحيح، ولهذا لو قال دمك حر لا يعتق، وقد صححوا صحة التكفل بالدم لما يقال دمه هدر أي نفسه فكان العرف جرى به في الكفالة دون العتق والطلاق وصحح في الجوهرة وقوع الطلاق. يقال ذهب دمه هدراً فحينئذ لا فرق بين الطلاق والكفالة، وتقييدهم الجزء بالشائع ليس للاحتراز عن المعين لما في الخلاصة : لو قال نصفك الأعلى طالق واحدة ونصفك الأسفل ثنتين فقد وقعت هذه المسألة ببخارى فأفتى بعضهم بوقوع الواحدة لأن الرأس في الأعلى، وبعضهم اعتبر الإضافتين لأن الفرج في الأسفل ا.هـ. وقد علم به أنه لو اقتصر على أحدهما وقعت واحدة اتفاقاً. وقد أطلق المصنف وقوع الطلاق بما ذكر فأفاد أنه يتوقف على النية، فلو قال أردت به العضو حقيقة لم يصدق قضاء ويصدق ديانة لكنه كيف

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Divorce. Divorce when added to her entirety or to what is used to express her, like the neck, the soul, the body, the physical frame, and the private parts: divorce occurs. It is as if he said: 'You are thrice divorced' — thus in al-Muhit. And its apparent meaning is that 'You from me are three' and 'You are three' by omitting 'from me' are the same in being kinayah. As for 'You are the three', it is not kinayah. His statement: (And if he adds divorce to her entirety or to what is used to express her, like the neck, the soul, the body, the physical frame, the private parts, the face, or to a common part of her like half or a third, she is divorced). By addition to the entirety he meant that it be by direct wording: 'You are divorced'. And by that which is used to express the entirety by figure of speech, like 'your neck'. Otherwise, everything expresses the entirety. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. The commentator mentioned that what is added to the entirety is 'you', 'the soul', 'the body', 'the physical frame'. As for what is used to express her, it is other than these. The apparent is the first, as is not hidden. By his expression 'used to express her', he indicated that he must say, e.g., 'your neck is divorced'. But if he said 'the neck from you is divorced' or 'the face', or placed his hand on the head or neck and said 'this limb is divorced', it does not occur in the most correct view, because he did not make it an expression for the whole but for a part. Contrary to when he did not place his hand but said 'this head is divorced' and pointed to his wife's head: the correct view is that it occurs, as if he said 'this head of yours is divorced'. For this, if he said to someone else 'I sold you this head for a thousand dirhams' and pointed to his slave's head, and the buyer said 'I accept', the sale is valid. Thus in al-Khaniyyah. He restricted to the neck and what follows because it does not occur with the belly, back, vulva, and blood in the correct view. For this, if one says 'your blood is free', he is not freed. And they have validated guarantee with blood because it is said 'his blood is wasted' meaning his life, so custom has applied it to guarantee (kafalah) but not to emancipation or divorce. And in al-Jawharah, the occurrence of divorce is validated. It is said 'his blood is wasted'. Then there is no difference between divorce and guarantee. Their restriction of the part to the common (sha'i') is not to exclude the specific (mu'ayyan) because in al-Khulasah: If he said 'your upper half is divorced once and your lower half twice', this case occurred in Bukhara. Some muftis gave fatwa of one divorce because the head is in the upper half, and some considered both additions because the private part is in the lower half. From this it is known that if he confined himself to one of them, one divorce occurs by agreement. The author absolutely stated occurrence of divorce by what was mentioned, and this indicates that it depends on intention. So if he said 'I meant the limb literally', he is not believed in court but is believed religiously. But how? ... The one that is not explicit half. No. His statement: (And their restriction of the part to the common is not to exclude the specific. He said in al-Nahr: I say, rather it is to exclude the specific that does not express the whole, as will come. And occurrence by the upper half or both is only because each contains what expresses the whole, as the reasoning clarifies. I say: In it, that the exclusion of the specific that expresses the whole is excluded by his statement 'or to what expresses her.' Also, the common part is contrasted with the specific part whether it expresses the whole or not. His statement: (And it is known from this that if he confined himself to one of them, one divorce occurs by agreement.) He said in al-Nahr: The second is not allowed, as is apparent. It is as he said based on what is apparent from the expression, but it is unlikely that this was the author's intent. Rather, it should be interpreted that he meant he confined himself to one of them, i.e., he said 'one divorce' because his aim is to prove that she is divorced by adding divorce to half, whether upper or lower. Page 456: And the face, or to a common part like half or third, she is divorced. And to the hand, foot, and anus: it is not explicit, even though it is only by predominant usage as we have mentioned? Indeed, al-Zayla'i has gone far in saying in the discussion of his statement 'I am from you divorced' is nonsense, and its being unconventional in execution does not remove it from being a figure, like his saying 'your tenth is divorced' or 'your private part' or 'I divorced you half a divorce'. Because explicitness is only by predominant usage. And the truth is that occurrence in court is only when the expression for the whole is well-known customarily. And if he confined himself to the expression for the whole, that would be more appropriate because the addition to the whole is known from the beginning of the chapter from ... His statement: 'like you are divorced'. His statement: (And to the hand, foot, and anus: no, i.e., she is not divorced by addition to what is mentioned, i.e., to what does not express the whole. This includes hair, nose, shin, thigh, back, belly, tongue, ear, mouth, chest, chin, tooth, saliva, vein, liver, heart. He applied it generally, covering if he intended the whole body. But in al-Bazzaziyyah: Imam al-Halwani mentioned that if he mentions a limb that is used to express the entire body and intends to restrict divorce to it, it is not unlikely that he is believed. And if he mentioned the hand and foot and meant the whole body, we can say divorce occurs. And if it is a part that is not used for benefit, like the tooth and saliva, it does not occur. In al-Zahiriyyah: If he adds it to her vulva, there is no narration on this in the book. In Fath al-Qadir from the Book of Guarantee: Muhammad did not mention if he guarantees with an eye. Al-Balkhi said: It is invalid as in divorce, unless he intends by it the body. And the correct view is that it should be valid in guarantee and divorce, because the eye is used to express the whole: one says 'the eye of the people' and he is an eye among people. Perhaps it was not known in their time. As for our time, there is no doubt about it. Similar to divorce is zihar, ila', pardon from qisas, and emancipation: even if he emancipates his finger, it does not occur. We restricted it to what does not express the whole, because the hand and what is with it, if among a people they express the whole with it, divorce occurs. This is the interpretation of what is reported from them intending the whole, like the hadith 'On the hand is what it takes until it returns' and His statement 'May the hands of Abu Lahab perish' (Surah al-Masad: 1). The summary is three: explicit that occurs in court without intention, like the neck; kinayah that occurs only with intention, like the hand; and what is neither explicit nor kinayah does not occur even if he intends, like the tooth and saliva. No, but occurrence by agreement in the lower half is incorrect, because those who gave fatwa of one divorce by the upper half do not cause anything to occur by the lower half. His statement: (Indeed, al-Zayla'i went far, etc.) It may be said there is no farness in his statement, for the explicit is what contains the root letters t-l-q, like 'divorced', 'divorce', 'divorcing' and the like. So his statement 'you are divorced' is explicit, and his word 'you' has no input in its explicitness. Likewise, predominant usage has no input in its explicitness; rather, it is a condition for occurrence without intention. Evidence for what we say is what was mentioned from al-Hidayah at the beginning of the chapter in the rationale that these are explicit by usage in the meaning of divorce and not others, and by their not needing intention due to predominant usage. Thus it appears that if they are not predominantly used except for divorce, they are explicit, but their occurrence without intention depends on being customary. So lack of custom does not remove it from being explicit, as al-Muhaqqiq al-Zayla'i said. This is what appeared to me. His statement: (And in al-Zahiriyyah: If he adds it to her heart, there is no narration.)
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the types of expressions that can effect divorce when added to a woman's body. The author explains that if a husband says 'your neck is divorced' or similar expressions that are conventionally used to refer to the whole person (الجملة), divorce occurs immediately and without need for intention, because these are considered explicit (صريح). However, if he adds divorce to a specific limb that does not conventionally stand for the whole, like 'your hand is divorced' or 'your foot', it is kinayah: divorce only occurs if he intended divorce by that expression. The passage further clarifies that adding divorce to a common part (شائع) like 'half of you' is treated like adding to the whole, provided the half is designated in a way that includes the whole. There is also discussion about whether expressions like 'blood' or 'eye' are used to refer to the whole; in the Hanafi school, custom (العرف) plays a key role. The commentator also debates with al-Zayla'i regarding the definition of explicit (صريح). According to the majority of Hanafis, explicit terms are those that contain the root ط ل ق and are predominantly used for divorce; they do not require intention for occurrence in court. Other expressions that refer to the whole body by convention (like 'neck') are also considered explicit by the author, though there is some disagreement. The passage concludes with a tripartite classification: explicit (occurs without intention), kinayah (occurs only with intention), and neither (does not occur even with intention, e.g., 'tooth' or 'saliva'). This classification is crucial for judges and scholars when adjudicating divorce cases where the husband uses indirect expressions.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): An expression that clearly indicates divorce by its wording (containing root t-l-q) or by customary usage as a whole-body term, and which effects divorce without need for intention.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that can imply divorce but requires the husband's intention to effect divorce in court.
الجملة (al-jumlah): The whole person of the wife; expressions that denote the entirety (e.g., 'you', 'your body') or substitutes (e.g., 'your neck') are treated like clear divorce.
الشائع (al-sha'i'): An undivided part, like half or third, which can stand for the whole; adding divorce to such a part is like adding to the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following expressions is considered sarih (explicit) for divorce?
A) 'Your hand is divorced'
B) 'Your neck is divorced'
C) 'Your tooth is divorced'
D) 'Your foot is divorced'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, divorce expressions are classified as sarih (explicit, no intention needed), kinayah (indirect, intention required), or neither (no effect), based on customary usage of the term to refer to the whole wife.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Divorce Expressions Added to Body Parts: Ṣarīḥ and Kināyah
Definition and classification of divorce expressions with body parts, including an intra-madhhab critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ṣarīḥ and kināyah when divorce is attached to a body part, apply the custom criterion, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Al-Zayla‘i.

Core Idea
A body part expression is ṣarīḥ only if customarily used for the whole person; otherwise it is kināyah.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq tackles a crucial nuance in divorce law: when a husband says “your hand is divorced” or similar, does divorce occur automatically or does it require intention? The answer hinges on whether the expression is classified as ṣarīḥ (explicit) or kināyah (metaphorical). The author, Ibn Nujaym, explores the definition of ṣarīḥ and critiques a view of Al-Zayla‘ī that would make any expression containing ṭ-l-q ṣarīḥ regardless of custom. This lesson unpacks the analytical framework and the practical consequences.

The result is that it is three: explicit (ṣarīḥ) that occurs judicially without intention, like the neck; metaphor (kināyah) that occurs only with intention, like the hand; and what is neither explicit nor metaphor, it does not occur even with intention, like the saliva and tooth. But the occurrence by agreement in the lower half is not correct because whoever gave a fatwa of one divorce by the upper half does not effect anything by the lower half. His statement: 'Al-Zayla'i the commentator went far' etc. It may be said there is no remoteness in his statement because the explicit is what contains the letters ṭ-l-q like 'ṭāliq', 'ṭalāq', 'taṭlīq' etc. So his statement 'you are divorced' is explicit, and his saying 'you' has no entry into its explicitness. Likewise, predominant usage has no entry into its explicitness; rather it is a condition for occurrence without intention. And what indicates what we said is what passed from Al-Hidāyah at the beginning of the chapter: the explanation of their being explicit is due to usage in the meaning of divorce and not other, and because they do not require intention due to predominant usage. So it appears that if they are not used predominantly except for divorce, they are explicit, but their occurrence without intention depends on it being customary. So lack of custom does not remove it from explicitness as the investigator Al-Zayla'i said. This is what appeared to me. His statement: 'In Al-Ẓahīriyyah: if he added to her heart, there is no narration.'
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the classification of divorce expressions when added to body parts. It defines three categories: explicit (ṣarīḥ) which occurs without intention, metaphor (kināyah) which requires intention, and neither which cannot effect divorce even with intention. The key criterion is customary usage: a body part expression is explicit only if it is predominantly used to refer to the whole person. The author critiques Al-Zayla'i for claiming that 'I am divorced from you' (annā mink ṭāliq) is explicit despite not being customary. He argues that ṣarāḥa (explicitness) does not depend on custom, but occurrence without intention does. The passage also covers specific cases: adding divorce to a common part (half, third) is effective; adding to hand, foot, anus is not explicit. It includes a discussion of 'ayn (eye) as a term for the whole person, citing Quran and hadith. The ruling is limited to divorce but extends to ẓihār, īlā’, pardon of qiṣāṣ, and manumission. The author distinguishes between parts that have no enjoyment value (like tooth, saliva) where divorce never occurs. The passage ends with a note from Al-Ẓahīriyyah about the heart (qalb) where there is no narration, but later Al-Maqdisi's commentary is mentioned separately.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): A divorce expression that is explicit and effects divorce without intention, because it is predominantly used for divorce.
كناية (kināyah): A divorce expression that requires intention (niyyah) for divorce to occur, because it is not predominantly used for divorce.
غلبة الاستعمال (ghalabah al-istiʿmāl): Predominant usage; the criterion for a word to be considered ṣarīḥ in divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'your hand is divorced', what is the ruling?
A) The divorce is ṣarīḥ and occurs without intention.
B) It is kināyah and requires intention to effect divorce.
C) It is neither ṣarīḥ nor kināyah, so no divorce occurs even with intention.
D) It is treated as a joke and no divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
A divorce expression using a body part is ṣarīḥ only if that part is customarily used to refer to the whole person; otherwise it is kināyah requiring intention, or neither if the part has no enjoyment value.

Divorce by Fractions: Indivisibility Principle
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fractional divorce expressions and the principle of indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that fractional references in divorce count as full divorces, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning exception of fractions, and apply the rule to various scenarios.

Core Idea
Divorce is indivisible: mentioning a fraction of a divorce counts as one full divorce.

When a husband uses fractions in his divorce pronouncement—like 'half a divorce' or 'a third of a divorce'—what legal effect does it produce? The Hanafi school holds that divorce is indivisible (lā yatajazza'), so any fractional mention is treated as a complete divorce. This lesson unpacks this principle, contrasts it with divisible acts like manumission, and examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding exceptions in fractional expressions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of indivisibility
Recognize that divorce, unlike manumission, cannot be split into parts. Therefore, mentioning a fraction of a divorce (e.g., half) is legally equivalent to pronouncing a full divorce.
This is based on the maxim: 'mentioning part of what is indivisible is like mentioning the whole.'
2
Apply the principle to basic fractions
Work through simple cases: 'half a divorce' → one divorce; 'third of a divorce' → one divorce; 'three halves of one divorce' → two divorces (since 1.5 rounds up to a full divorce).
The text specifically states: 'Wa niṣf al-ṭalqah aw thulthuhā ṭalqah'.
3
Distinguish divorce from divisible acts
Compare with manumission (freeing half a slave frees only half) and marriage (marrying half a woman is invalid). Note that divorce is treated differently because its nature prevents division.
This distinction is made in the passage to highlight the unique character of ṭalāq.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on exception
Examine the scenario: 'You are divorced three times except half a divorce.' Abu Yusuf rules that two divorces occur (since half is indivisible, the exception is treated as 'except one'), while Muhammad rules that three occur (because the exception of an indivisible half is void).
The text from Al-Ẓahīriyyah is cited, and the author indicates Muhammad's view is preferred.
5
Synthesize the twelve cases
Review the summary of twelve possible combinations of halves (one, two, three, or four) added to one, two, or three divorces. Use the pattern to predict outcomes.
The passage ends with a systematic enumeration: 'the added is either one, two, three, or four, and each of them, the added to it is...'

أربعاً وكل منها إما أن يكون المضاف إليه
And half a divorce or its third is one divorce, and three halves of two divorces are three divorces. And from one or between... and hair, nail, vein, liver, heart. He restricted with anus because if he said 'your anus is divorced' it occurs like your vagina in Al-Khulāṣah. The anus, even if synonymous, does not necessitate equality in ruling because consideration is as it is familiar here because the word is used to refer to the whole. Do you not see that 'buḍʿ' is synonymous with 'farj' but its ruling here is not like its ruling in expression. He restricted with divorce in the common part to avoid manumission and its followers, because it is of the type that is divisible. For this reason, pardon of some of qiṣāṣ is considered a pardon for all. Since divorce has no part, it is like mentioning all for correction like pardon. It is said according to Abu Yusuf's view that two fall because the divorce as it is indivisible in effect is indivisible in exception, so it is as if he said 'except one'. According to Muhammad, three fall because half in divorce is indivisible in effect and in exception. If he said 'You are divorced one divorce except half of it', one falls. This is an indication to what Muhammad said. It may be said that it does not indicate Muhammad's view because Abu Yusuf only did not say completion in exception due to lack of benefit, because then it is invalid as it is an exception of the whole from the whole. If he said 'and part of the divorce here is a divorce', it would be more concise, comprehensive, and better. His statement: 'Three halves of two divorces are three because half of two divorces is one divorce. So when three halves are combined, they are necessarily three, unless he intended a half of each of the two divorces, then their halves are four, so three of them are one and a half, so two divorces occur in conscience (diyānah) but he is not believed in judicial ruling because it is a possibility contrary to the apparent, because the apparent is that half of two divorces is one divorce, not two halves of two divorces. He restricted by saying 'two divorces' because if he said 'three halves of one divorce', two divorces occur because it is one and a half, so it completes. This is transmitted in Al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr, chosen by Al-Nāṭifī, and corrected by Al-ʿAttābī. It is known from this that if he said 'four halves of one divorce', two also occur. It is also known from this that if he said 'two halves of one divorce', one divorce occurs. The total is twelve cases because the added (i.e., half) is either one, two, three, or four, and each of them, the added to it is...
