Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the legal treatment of fractional expressions in divorce. The underlying principle is that divorce is indivisible (lā yatajazza'), so mentioning a fraction of a divorce is treated as a full divorce. The author explains various scenarios: saying 'half a divorce' or 'a third of a divorce' results in one full divorce. 'Three halves of two divorces' results in three divorces unless the husband intended a half of each of two divorces, in which case only two divorces occur in conscience (diyānah) but not in judicial ruling. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding exception in fractional divorces: if one says 'three divorces except half a divorce,' Abu Yusuf holds that two fall (since the exception is considered one whole), while Muhammad holds that three fall (since half is indivisible and exception of an indivisible part is void). The passage also contrasts divorce with manumission (ʿitāq) and marriage (nikāḥ): manumission is divisible (freeing half a slave only frees that half), and marriage to a fractional part is invalid. The author then summarizes twelve possible cases based on the number of halves and the number of divorces added to.

Key Terms
جزء التطليقة (juzʾ al-taṭlīqah): A fraction of a divorce (e.g., half, third) which is treated as a full divorce due to indivisibility.
لا يتجزى (lā yatajazzaʾ): Indivisible; a characteristic of divorce that prevents fractional application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Fractional expressions in divorce are treated as full divorces due to the indivisibility of ṭalāq; intra-Hanafi differences exist on exception scenarios.

The Validity of Divorce by Referring to the Heart: Al-Maqdisi's Proof
Al-Maqdisi's gloss providing a ruling and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Al-Maqdisi's ruling on divorce by referring to the heart and explain how the Quranic verse and analogy to the soul support it.

Core Idea
Divorce by heart is valid because the heart represents the whole person, like the soul.

Al-Maqdisi, a later Hanafi authority, addresses a subtle point: if a husband says 'your heart is divorced,' does divorce occur? In the earlier authoritative texts (Zahir al-Riwayah), no explicit narration exists on this phrase. Al-Maqdisi nevertheless rules that it does occur, grounding his view in an analogy with the soul and a Quranic verse that attributes sin to the heart. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the strength of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
State the specific expression under discussion: adding divorce to the heart (e.g., 'your heart is divorced'). Then state Al-Maqdisi's ruling: the divorce should occur (yanbaghi an yaqa'a).
This is a ruling on the effectiveness of a particular divorce expression.
2
Locate the proof
Al-Maqdisi provides two proofs: an analogy (it is like the soul) and a Quranic verse (2:283). Identify each as a separate dalil.
Analogy is qiyas; Quran is naqli.
3
Analyze the analogy: heart like the soul
The soul (rūḥ) represents the entire living person. If saying 'your soul is divorced' would be effective, then 'your heart' should be equally effective because the heart is the seat of life and consciousness.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the common attribute of representing the person.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Quran 2:283 says 'then indeed his heart is sinful' – sin is attributed to the heart, but the actual subject of sin is the person. Thus the heart can stand for the whole person in legal discourse.
The verse provides linguistic support that 'heart' can be used metonymically for the person.
5
Evaluate the strength of the proof
The Quranic text is qat'i al-thubut but its dalala (indication) is zanni – it does not directly address divorce. The analogy is plausible but not recorded in the authoritative texts. Al-Maqdisi's view is a later fatwa position, not part of al-zahir al-riwayah.
This explains why the ruling is considered mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخ) قال المقدسي في شرحه : ينبغي أن يقع لأنه كالروح وقال تعالى فإنه آثم قلبه) [البقرة: ٢٨٣]
etc.) Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: It should occur because it is like the soul, and He said: 'Then indeed his heart is sinful' (Quran 2:283).
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This short passage is a gloss from Al-Maqdisi's commentary on the earlier discussion of adding divorce to the heart. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq had noted that in Al-Ẓahīriyyah there is no narration about the heart. Al-Maqdisi here provides his own reasoning: divorce by adding to the heart should be effective because the heart is analogous to the soul (rūḥ), and the Quran refers to the heart as the seat of sin, indicating that it represents the person. Therefore, saying 'your heart is divorced' is a valid divorce expression.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): The heart; used metaphorically to refer to the whole person in the context of divorce.
كالروح (ka al-rūḥ): Like the soul; an analogy used to justify that divorce by heart is effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) An analogy to the soul and a Quranic verse.
C) A consensus of scholars.
D) A ruling from Al-Ẓahīriyyah.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maqdisi's ruling that divorce by referring to the heart is valid demonstrates that later Hanafi jurists may use qiyas and Quranic evidence to address cases not explicitly covered in the early texts.

Fraction of Talaq and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Exception
Fraction of talaq counts as one and intra-Hanafi dispute on exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a fraction of talaq counts as one complete talaq, distinguish it from manumission and marriage, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding exception with a fraction, applying the rulings of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
A fraction of talaq counts as one full talaq; the dispute is whether a fraction in exception is treated as an indivisible whole or invalid.

In Hanafi fiqh, talaq (divorce) is considered an indivisible act. This means that uttering 'half a talaq' results in a complete talaq, not a fraction. However, when a fraction appears in an exception (istithna'), a dispute arises between the two leading disciples of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the ruling and the nuances of the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on fraction of talaq
Identify the ruling: a fraction of a talaq (e.g., half, third) counts as one complete talaq because talaq is indivisible. This is established by analogy to pardon of retaliation (qisas).
Contrast with manumission (which can be divided) and marriage (where a fraction is invalid).
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
The dispute concerns exception (istithna') with a fraction. If a man says 'thrice except half a talaq', what is the effect? Abu Yusuf treats the fraction as one in exception (so two fall), while Muhammad treats the exception as invalid (so three fall).
Both rely on the indivisibility of talaq but apply it differently to exception.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf argues that talaq is indivisible both in execution and in exception. Therefore, 'half a talaq' in exception becomes 'one talaq', reducing the count by one.
Example: 'thrice except half' becomes 'thrice except one', leaving two.
4
Follow Muhammad's reasoning
Muhammad argues that talaq is indivisible in execution, but in exception, the fraction cannot become a whole because the exception itself would then be indivisible. Hence the exception is invalid, and the original number stands.
Example: 'thrice except half' results in three talaq.
5
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator suggests that the simple example of 'one except half' does not necessarily prove Muhammad's view, because Abu Yusuf would also consider that exception invalid (exempting the whole from the whole). The dispute is more nuanced.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether two or three talaq fall, affecting the possibility of reconciliation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصف التطليقة أو ثلثها طلقة وثلاثة أنصاف تطليقتين ثلاث. وقيد بالطلاق في الجزء الشائع للاحتراز عن العتاق وتوابعه فإنه من قبيل ما يتجزى، فلو أعتق نصف عبده لم يعتق كله عند الإمام وللاحتراز عن النكاح فإنه لو تزوج نصفها لم يصح النكاح احتياطاً كما في الخانية. وبه ضعف قول الشارح أن الجزء الشائع محل للنكاح والعفو عن دم العمد وتسليم الشفعة كالطلاق والأصل أن ذكر بعض ما لا كذكر كله. قوله : ( ونصف التطليقة أو ثلثها طلقة) ومراده أن جزء الطلقة تطليقة ولو جزءاً من جزء لأن الشرع ناظر إلى صون كلام العاقل عن الإلغاء وتصرفه ما أمكن، ولذا اعتبر العفو عن بعض القصاص عفواً عنه فلما لم يكن للطلاق جزء كان كذكر كله تصحيحاً كالعفو. وفي الظهيرية : أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا نصف تطليقة ؛ قيل على قول أبي يوسف يقع ثنتان لأن التطليقة كما لا تتجزى في الإيقاع لا تتجزى في الاستثناء فيصير كأنه قال إلا واحدة، وعند محمد يقع الثلاث لأن النصف في الطلاق لا يتجزى في الإيقاع ولا في الاستثناء، ولو قال أنت طالق تطليقة إلا نصفها تقع واحدة وهذا إشارة إلى ما قال محمد ا هـ. وقد يقال إنه لا يشير إلى قول محمد لأن أبا يوسف إنما لم يقل بالتكميل في الاستثناء لعدم فائدته لأنه حينئذ لا يصح لكونه استثناء الكل من الكل.
And half of a talaq or a third of it is one talaq, and three halves of two talaqs are three. And it is restricted to talaq in the common part to exclude manumission and its consequences, for it is of the type that can be divided; so if someone frees half his slave, the whole does not become free according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], and to exclude marriage, for if one marries half of her, the marriage is not valid out of precaution, as in al-Khaniyah. Thereby the statement of the commentator that the common part is a place for marriage, pardon for intentional killing, and acceptance of pre-emption like talaq is weakened, and the principle is that the mention of part of what is indivisible is like the mention of all of it. His saying: (Half of a talaq or a third of it is one talaq) means that a part of a talaq is a talaq, even if a part of a part, because the Lawgiver looks to preserve the speech of a sane person from nullification and giving effect to it as much as possible. Hence pardon of some retaliation was considered as pardon for all. So since talaq has no part, it is like mentioning all of it for validity, like pardon. And in al-Zahiriyyah: 'You are divorced thrice except half a talaq'; it was said according to Abu Yusuf: two fall because a talaq, just as it is indivisible in execution, is indivisible in exception, so it becomes as if he said 'except one'. According to Muhammad: three fall because half in talaq is indivisible in execution and in exception. And if he said: 'You are divorced one talaq except half of it', one falls, and this indicates the view of Muhammad. It may be said that it does not indicate Muhammad's view because Abu Yusuf only did not say completion in exception due to lack of benefit, since then it would be invalid because it would be excepting the whole from the whole.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, dealing with the ruling that a fraction of a talaq counts as a full talaq. The matn states: 'Half of a talaq or a third of it is one talaq, and three halves of two talaqs are three.' The commentator explains that this applies only to the common part (al-juz' al-shai') in talaq, to distinguish it from cases like freeing half a slave, where the whole does not become free according to Abu Hanifa because manumission can be divided. Similarly, marrying half a woman is invalid as a precautionary measure. The commentator also refutes the idea that the common part applies to marriage, pardon, and pre-emption as it does to talaq. The reasoning is that the Lawgiver aims to preserve the sanity of speech; if a man says 'half a talaq', since talaq cannot be divided, it is treated as the whole. This is analogous to pardoning part of retaliation, which counts as full pardon. The commentary then presents a dispute from al-Zahiriyyah: if a man says 'you are divorced thrice except half a talaq', according to Abu Yusuf two talaq fall because just as a talaq is indivisible in execution, the exception is also indivisible, so 'except half a talaq' is treated as 'except one talaq', leaving two. According to Muhammad, three fall because half is also indivisible in exception, so the exception is ineffective and the original three stand. The commentator adds a critique: this example does not necessarily indicate Muhammad's view because Abu Yusuf's position is based on the exception being invalid (since it would be excepting the whole from the whole). Thus the dispute revolves around whether talaq is indivisible in exception as it is in execution.

Key Terms
تطليقة (talqah): A single divorce pronouncement; considered indivisible in the Hanafi school.
جزء شائع (juz' shai'): A common, undifferentiated part of a whole (e.g., half, third). In talaq, it is treated as the whole.
استثناء (istithna'): Exception; a phrase like 'except half a talaq'. The validity and effect of exception in talaq is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, how many talaq fall if a man says 'thrice except half a talaq'?
A) Three
B) Two
C) One
D) None

Key Takeaway
A fraction of talaq counts as a full talaq; the intra-Hanafi dispute on exception hinges on whether the fraction is treated as a whole for exception or the exception is invalid.

The Twelve Cases of Fractional Talaq Expressions
Enumerative parsing of twelve fraction-talaq combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of talaq that fall for each combination of halves (1-4) added to one, two, or three talaqs, and explain the reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
A fraction of talaq counts as a whole talaq, but multiple fractions combine to form full talaqs based on the idafah construction.

When a husband expresses divorce using fractions like 'half of two talaqs', the resulting number of divorces depends on both the numerator (how many halves) and the denominator (how many talaqs the fraction is added to). This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq enumerates all twelve possible combinations and derives the rulings from transmitted texts and analogy. Understanding this system is essential for correctly applying Hanafi law in cases of ambiguous talaq expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudaf and mudaf ilayh
In each expression, determine the fraction (mudaf: e.g., 'half', 'two halves') and the base number of talaqs (mudaf ilayh: e.g., 'one talaq', 'two talaqs'). The idafah construction shows which number the fraction applies to.
The mudaf indicates the numerator; the mudaf ilayh indicates the denominator.
2
Calculate the total fraction value
Multiply the numerator (number of halves) by 1/2 to get the fractional value. Then multiply this by the denominator (number of talaqs) to get the number of whole talaqs the fraction represents. For example, three halves of two talaqs = 3 × 1/2 × 2 = 3 talaqs.
This calculation works for the default interpretation where the fraction applies to the total count.
3
Consider the alternative intention (diyanatan)
If the husband intended to halve each individual talaq separately (e.g., 'three halves of two talaqs' meaning halving each of the two talaqs creates four halves), then the fraction value changes. In such cases, the husband's claim is accepted in private (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent meaning.
The default assumption is that the fraction applies to the whole number of talaqs, not to each talaq individually.
4
Enumerate the twelve cases
List all combinations where the number of halves (1-4) is added to 1, 2, or 3 talaqs. Apply the calculation to each pair to determine the number of talaq that fall. For example, one half of one talaq = 1 talaq; one half of two talaqs = 1 talaq; one half of three talaqs = 2 talaqs; and so on.
The text provides the rulings for all twelve from transmission or analogy.
5
Identify exceptions and analogies
Note that the rulings for three halves and four halves are derived by analogy (qiyas) from the explicit rulings for one half and two halves, as no direct transmission exists. The statement 'three halves of three talaqs are three' and the four-halves cases are analogical extensions.
This shows the role of qiyas in extending fiqh rules to new cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وثلاثة أنصاف تطليقتين ثلاث لأن نصف التطليقتين تطليقة فإذا جمع بين ثلاثة أنصاف تكون ثلاثاً ضرورة إلا إذا نوى تنصيف كل من التطليقتين فتكون أنصافها أربعاً فثلاثة منها طلقة ونصف فتقع طليقتان ديانة ولا يصدق في القضاء لأنه احتمال خلاف الظاهر لأن الظاهر أن نصف التطليقتين تطليقة لا نصفا تطليقتين . قيد بقوله تطليقتين» لأنه لو قال ثلاثة أنصاف تطليقة وقعت تطليقتان لأنها طلقة ونصف فتتكامل وهو المنقول في الجامع الصغير واختاره الناطفي وصححه العتابي وعلم منه أنه لو قال أربعة أنصاف تطليقة وقعت ثنتان أيضاً، وعرف منه أيضاً أنه لو قال نصفي تطليقة وقعت واحدة. وفي الذخيرة: لو قال أنت طالق نصف تطليقتيتن فواحدة ولو قال نصفي تطليقتين فثنتان وكذا نصف ثلاث تطليقات، ولو قال نصفي ثلاث تطليقات فثلاث. وحاصلها أنها اثنتا عشرة مسألة لأن المضاف أعني النصف إما أن يكون واحداً أو ثنتين أو ثلاثاً أو أربعاً وكل منها إما أن يكون المضاف إليه واحدة أو ثتنين أو ثلاثاً، فإن كان النصف مضافاً إلى الطلقة فقط فواحدة، فإن كان النصف مضافاً إلى الطلقتين فواحدة وإن كان النصف مضافاً إلى الثلاث فئنتان، وإن كان النصفان مضافاً إلى الواحدة فواحدة وإلى الثنتين فثنتان وإلى الثلاث فثلاث. وإن كان الثلاثة أنصاف مضافاً إلى الواحدة فثنتان وإلى الثنتين فثلاث وإلى الثلاث فكذلك استنباطاً مما قبلها لا نقلاً. وإن كان المضاف أربعة الأنصاف فثنتان فإن إلى الواحدة وإن إلى الثنتين أو إلى الثلاث فثلاث استنباطاً.
His saying: (And three halves of two talaqs are three) because half of two talaqs is one talaq, so when three halves are combined, they become three necessarily, unless he intended to halve each of the two talaqs, then their halves would be four, so three of them are a talaq and a half, thus two talaq fall privately, but he is not believed in court because it is an improbable interpretation; the apparent meaning is that half of two talaqs is one talaq, not half of two talaqs. He restricted it by saying 'two talaqs' because if he said 'three halves of a talaq', two talaq would fall because it is a talaq and a half, so it is completed. This is transmitted in al-Jami' al-Saghir, chosen by al-Natifi, and corrected by al-Attabi. From this it is known that if he said 'four halves of a talaq', two also fall; and also that if he said 'two halves of a talaq', one falls. In al-Dhakhirah: if he said 'You are divorced half of two talaqs', one falls; if he said 'two halves of two talaqs', two fall; similarly, half of three talaqs [falls one], and if he said 'two halves of three talaqs', three fall. The total is twelve cases because the added noun (i.e., half) is either one, two, three, or four, and each of them is added to either one, two, or three. If half is added to one talaq only, one falls; if half is added to two talaqs, one falls; if half is added to three talaqs, two fall. If two halves are added to one talaq, one falls; to two talaqs, two; to three talaqs, three. If three halves are added to one talaq, two fall; to two talaqs, three; to three talaqs, likewise, derived by analogy, not by transmission. If the added noun is four halves, then two fall [if added to one], and if to two or three, three fall, derived by analogy.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the matn 'three halves of two talaqs are three'. The author clarifies the logic: half of two talaqs is one talaq, so three halves equal three talaqs. However, if the husband intended to halve each of the two talaqs separately, then there would be four halves, so three halves would be one and a half talaqs, resulting in two talaq privately (but not in court because that interpretation is not apparent). The commentator then expands to enumerate twelve cases (masa'il) based on the combination of the number of halves (1-4) and the number of talaqs to which they are added (1-3). The cases are derived from explicit texts (like al-Jami' al-Saghir) and by analogy. For example, one half of one talaq = one talaq; one half of two talaqs = one talaq; one half of three talaqs = two talaq; two halves of one talaq = one; two halves of two = two; two halves of three = three; three halves of one = two; three halves of two = three; three halves of three = three; four halves of one = two; four halves of two or three = three. These rulings are based on the principle that a fraction of a talaq counts as a complete talaq, but when multiple fractions are added, they combine to form whole talaqs.

Key Terms
مضاف (mudaf): The noun being added to (in idafah construction); here it refers to the fraction (half, two halves, etc.)
مضاف إليه (mudaf ilayh): The noun to which the fraction is added; here it is the number of talaqs (one, two, or three).
ديانة (diyanatan): In private religious duty, as opposed to in court (qada'an). The husband is believed in what is concealed between him and Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'You are divorced two halves of three talaqs', how many talaq fall?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The number of talaq from a fraction expression is obtained by multiplying the fraction (halves) by the base number of talaqs, rounding up fractional results to the next integer, and capping at three.

Repeating Indefinite Noun in Talaq with Fractions
Ruling on counting talaq with fractions using indefinite nouns and pronouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between repeating an indefinite noun and using a pronoun in talaq expressions, and determine the number of talaq that fall accordingly.

Core Idea
When the indefinite noun 'talaq' is repeated, each mention is a separate talaq; a pronoun refers back to the same talaq.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a subtle but crucial distinction in counting talaq when a husband uses fractional expressions. The key lies in Arabic grammar: repeating an indefinite noun (nakira) as indefinite creates a new entity, whereas a pronoun maintains the same entity. This directly affects whether a divorce is counted as one, two, or three talaq. A special rule applies to a non-consummated wife (ghayr madkhul biha) who can only be divorced once in any such scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording pattern
Look at whether the husband repeats the word 'talaq' each time (e.g., 'half a talaq, a third of a talaq') or uses a pronoun ('half of it, a third of it').
Repeating the indefinite noun means each fraction is a separate talaq; using a pronoun combines them into one.
2
Apply the Arabic grammar rule
In Arabic, when an indefinite noun (nakirah) is repeated as indefinite, the second occurrence refers to a different entity. Thus, 'talaq' repeated yields distinct talaqs.
The rule is: المنكر إذا أعيد منكراً كان الثاني غير الأول.
3
Calculate the number of talaq
If the fractions are from separate talaq mentions, each fraction contributes to a separate talaq; if they refer to the same talaq, they add up to at most one talaq. If the sum exceeds one (e.g., 1.5 of the same talaq), the chosen view is that it still counts as one.
The text states that the chosen (mukhtar) view is that excess from a single talaq does not create a second talaq.
4
Handle the non-consummated wife exception
For a wife who has not been consummated (ghayr madkhul biha), regardless of the wording, only one talaq falls. This is a special rule from the Hanafi school.
This is because a non-consummated wife can only be divorced once in a single pronouncement.
5
Differentiate the two example cases
Study the examples: 'half a talaq and a third of a talaq' yields three talaqs; 'half a talaq and a third of it' yields one talaq. Understand why the difference occurs.
The phrase 'one and a half of it' (واحدة ونصفها) gives one talaq, while 'one and a half' (واحدة ونصفاً) is distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال للمدخول بها أنت طالق نصف تطليقة وثلث تطليقة وسدس تطليقة وقع ثلاث لأن المنكر إذا أعيد منكراً كان الثاني غير الأول فيتكامل كل جزء بخلاف ما إذا قال أنت طالق نصف تطليقة وثلثها وسدسها حيث تقع واحدة لأن الثاني والثالث عين الأول فالكل أجزاء طلقة واحدة حتى لو زاد على الواحدة وقعت ثانية وكذا في الثالثة وهو مختار جماعة من المشايخ وفي المحيط والولوالجية : وهو المختار. وهكذا ذكر الحسن في المجرد لأنه زاد على أجزاء تطليقة واحدة فلا بد وأن تكون الزيادة من تطليقة أخرى فتتكامل الزيادة والأصح في اتحاد المرجع. وإن زادت أجزاء واحدة أن تقع واحدة لأنه أضاف الأجزاء إلى واحدة. نص عليه في المبسوط وعلى هذا لو قال أنت طالق واحدة ونصفها تقع واحدة كما في الذخيرة بخلاف واحدة ونصفاً، وأما غير المدخول بها فلا يقع عليها إلا واحدة في الصور كلها كما في البدائع.
And the author indicated that if he says to a consummated wife: 'You are divorced half a talaq and a third of a talaq and a sixth of a talaq', three talaq fall because when an indefinite noun is repeated as an indefinite, the second is different from the first, so each part is completed, unlike if he says 'half a talaq and a third of it and a sixth of it', where one talaq falls because the second and third are the same as the first, so all are parts of one talaq. If they exceed one, a second falls, and similarly for a third. This is the choice of a group of mashayikh, and in al-Muhit and al-Walwaljiyyah: it is the chosen one. Al-Hasan mentioned similarly in al-Mujarrad because he exceeded the parts of one talaq, so the excess must be from another talaq, thus it is completed. The more correct view regarding the unity of the referent is that if the parts of one talaq exceed, one talaq falls because he added the parts to one. This is stated in al-Mabsut. Based on this, if he says 'one and a half of it', one talaq falls, as in al-Dhakhirah, unlike 'one and a half'. As for a non-consummated wife, only one talaq falls in all these cases, as in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage addresses a crucial distinction in talaq expressions involving fractions. If the husband repeats the indefinite noun 'talaq' each time (e.g., 'half a talaq and a third of a talaq and a sixth of a talaq'), each mention is a separate talaq because when a indefinite noun is repeated indefinitely (manakir), the second occurrence refers to a different entity. Thus, the fractions combine to form three separate talaqs. However, if he uses a pronoun to refer back to the same talaq (e.g., 'half a talaq and a third of it and a sixth of it'), then all fractions refer to the same original talaq, so they only add up to at most one talaq. If the total fractions exceed one, they may constitute a second talaq, but the preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that if the sum of fractions of one talaq exceeds one, it still counts as one talaq because the excess is not from a separate talaq. The text then clarifies examples: 'one and a half of it' (i.e., one talaq and half of that same talaq) yields one talaq, whereas saying 'one and a half' (without the pronoun) would be ambiguous. Importantly, for a wife who has not been consummated (ghayr madkhul biha), in all these scenarios only one talaq falls, because a non-consummated wife cannot be divorced more than once with a single pronouncement.

Key Terms
نكرة (nakirah): An indefinite noun; when repeated as indefinite, it refers to a different entity (e.g., 'a book and a book' are two different books).
غير مدخول بها (ghayr madkhul biha): A wife who has not been physically consummated with; she can only be divorced once in a single pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The number of talaq in fractional pronouncements depends on whether the noun 'talaq' is repeated as indefinite or referred to by pronoun; a non-consummated wife always receives only one.

Distributing Talaq Among Multiple Wives: The 'Bayn' Rule
Rulings on talaq when a husband addresses multiple wives with a single number or uses 'between' or 'I share you'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for distributing a single or multiple talaq statements made to multiple wives using 'bayn' or 'ashraktukunna' and identify exceptions.

Core Idea
Talaq is indivisible: each wife receives a full integer share, never a fraction.

When a husband addresses multiple wives with a talaq count using 'bayn' (between) or 'ashraktukunna' (I share you), the Hanafi school distributes the talaq equally among them. However, talaq is indivisible, so each wife must receive a whole number of talaqs. This lesson explores the default distribution rule and the effect of the husband's intention, as well as special cases involving prior divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression and number of wives
Determine whether the husband used 'bayn' or 'ashraktukunna' and how many wives are addressed (e.g., four).
These expressions trigger distribution rules.
2
Apply the default distribution rule
For a stated number N of talaq to W wives, each wife receives the smallest integer >= N/W. This is because each talaq is indivisible and must be assigned wholely to each wife.
Examples: N=1, W=4 → each gets 1; N=2, W=4 → each gets 1; N=5, W=4 → each gets 2; N=9, W=4 → each gets 3.
3
Consider the husband's specific intention
If the husband intends that each talaq is shared among all wives collectively (i.e., each wife receives the full stated number), then for N talaq to W wives, each gets N. This overrides the default distribution.
Text example: for two talaq with this intent, each gets two; for three, each gets three.
4
Handle 'ashraktukunna' similarly
The phrase 'ashraktukunna' (I share you) has the same effect as 'bayn'. So the same rules apply.
Exception: when prior talaq have already been pronounced individually, different rules apply (see next step).
5
Analyze special cases with prior divorces
If the husband has already divorced two wives each with one talaq, then says to a third 'I share you in what I have pronounced', she receives two talaq (one from each prior talaq). If he divorces one wife with three, then says 'I share you' to another, she receives three because the three are fixed.
This differs from the default because the prior talaq are already assigned to individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودل كلامه أنه لو قال لأربع نسوة بينكن تطليقة طلقت كل واحدة واحدة لأن الربع يتكامل، وكذا بينكن تطليقتان أو ثلاث أو أربع إلا إذا نوى أن كل تطليقة بينهن جميعاً فيقع في التطليقتين على كل منهما تطليقتان وفي الثلاث ثلاث. ولو قال بينكن خمس تطليقات وقع على كل واحدة ثنتان إلى ثمان ولو قال بينكن تسع وقع على كل واحدة ثلاث. ولفظ «اشرکتکن کلفظ (بین) بخلاف ما لو طلق امرأتين كل واحدة ثم قال لثالثة شركتك فيما أوقعت عليهما يقع عليها تطليقتان لأنه شركها في كل تطليقة. ولو طلقها تم قال لأخرى أشركتك معها في الطلاق وقع على الثانية ثلاث بخلاف ما تقدم لأن هناك لم يسبق وقوع شيء فلم يقسم بينهن وهنا قد أوقع الثلاث على الأولى فلا يمكنه رفع شيء منه.
And his statement indicates that if he says to four women 'between you is a talaq', each one is divorced by one talaq because the quarter is completed. Similarly, 'between you are two talaq' or three or four, except if he intended that each talaq is between all of them, then in the case of two talaq, each gets two, and in three, three. If he says 'between you are five talaq', each gets two up to eight; if he says 'nine', each gets three. And the wording 'I share you' (ashraktukunna) is like the wording 'between' (bayn), unlike if he divorces two women each one [talaq] then says to a third 'I share you in what I have pronounced upon them', one talaq falls upon her? Actually the text says: 'يقع عليها تطليقتان'? Let me check: it says 'يقع عليها تطليقتان لأنه شركها في كل تطليقة'. So if he already divorced two women each one, then says to a third 'I share you', two talaq fall on her because he shares her in each talaq. And if he divorces her with three then says to another 'I share you with her in the divorce', three fall on the second, unlike before, because there nothing had occurred to be divided, whereas here he has already pronounced three on the first, so he cannot remove any part of it.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage covers the distribution of talaq among multiple wives when the husband uses expressions like 'between you' or 'I share you'. The default rule is that if he says 'between you is one talaq' to four wives, each wife receives one full talaq because talaq is indivisible and the fraction (quarter) is completed to whole. Similarly for two, three, four talaq, the number is divided equally among the wives, with each getting a full talaq for each unit. However, if the husband intends that each talaq is shared among all wives collectively, then in the case of two talaq, each wife gets two; in three, each gets three. For larger numbers like five, each gets two up to eight (i.e., five divided among four gives one each with remainder; but due to indivisibility, each gets two? Actually the text says 'five talaq' to four wives, each gets two? That would total eight, so it seems the rule is that each wife gets the smallest integer that is at least the share? Let's examine: 'بينكن خمس تطليقات وقع على كل واحدة ثنتان إلى ثمان' means each gets two, total eight, which is more than five. This seems to be based on the principle that each talaq is indivisible and each wife must get a whole number, so the total is increased to the nearest multiple of the number of wives. Similarly for nine, each gets three. The expression 'ashraktukunna' (I share you) is treated like 'bayn' (between). However, a distinction is made when the husband has already divorced some wives individually: if he says 'I share you' to a third woman after divorcing two each with one, she gets two talaq because she is included in each of those prior talaqs. Conversely, if he divorces one wife with three then says to another 'I share you', the second also gets three because the three are already fixed on the first and cannot be reduced.

Key Terms
بين (bayn): Preposition meaning 'between'; in talaq, it indicates that the given number of talaqs is shared equally among the wives, with each receiving a whole number due to indivisibility.
أشركتكن (ashraktukunna): I share you (plural); it has the same effect as 'bayn' in distributing talaq among the addressees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One each
B) Two each
C) Five each
D) Nine each

Key Takeaway
When talaq is addressed to multiple wives using 'bayn' or 'ashraktukunna', each wife receives a whole number of talaqs, either by default rounding up or by the husband's specific intention.

Counting Talaqat with Fractional Expressions
Explaining the number of talaqat when husband uses fractional expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of talaqat that fall when a husband uses fractional expressions and explain the distinction between indefinite repetition and definite reference.

Core Idea
Fractions of a talaq add up separately only if each fraction is expressed as an indefinite 'talaqah'.

When a husband expresses divorce in fractions like half or third, the number of talaqat that take effect depends on how the fractions are phrased. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, which hinges on a subtle grammatical distinction: repeating an indefinite noun creates new instances, while referring back to a definite noun keeps all parts within the same unit. We will walk through the specific cases and the rational deduction (istinbat) behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression pattern
Determine whether each fraction is attached to an indefinite 'talaqah' (e.g., 'half a talaq, third of a talaq') or to a definite referent via a pronoun (e.g., 'half of it, third of it').
The key is whether the word 'talaqah' is repeated without alif-lam or referred back with a pronoun.
2
Apply the nakirah rule
In Hanafi usul, repeating an indefinite noun (nakirah) affirms a new entity each time. Therefore, each fractional expression attached to a new indefinite 'talaqah' counts as a separate talaq.
Example: 'half a talaq, third of a talaq, sixth of a talaq' → three separate talaqat.
3
Apply the ma'rifah rule
When fractions refer back to a single definite talaq using a pronoun (e.g., 'its half, its third'), all fractions are parts of that one talaq. Only one talaq falls, unless the sum of parts exceeds one whole talaq, in which case the excess triggers additional talaqat.
Example: 'half of it, third of it, sixth of it' → one talaq (since total = 1). If parts exceed one, e.g., 'half and half' → one talaq from the first half, the second half requires another talaq.
4
Use the table for halves
Memorize the rulings for common fractional patterns: one half added to one/ two/ three talaqat; two halves; three halves; four halves. The number of resulting talaqat follows a consistent pattern based on the number of halves and the base.
The passage provides a complete table; e.g., three halves added to one talaq yields two talaqat.
5
Note the exception for non-consummated wife
For a wife who has not been consummated (ghayr madkhul biha), only one talaq falls regardless of the fractional expression, because her talaq is effective with a single utterance.
This is based on the rule that a non-consummated wife’s talaq is treated as a single, irrevocable divorce.

One or two or three: if the half is added to 'the talaq' only, then one; if the half is added to 'the two talaqs', then one; if the half is added to 'the three', then two. If the two halves are added to the one, then one; to the two, then two; to the three, then three. If three halves are added to the one, then two; to the two, then three; to the three, likewise—by deduction from what precedes, not by transmission. If four halves are added, then two if to the one, and three if to the two or three—by deduction. The author indicated that if he says to a consummated wife, 'You are divorced half a talaq, third of a talaq, and sixth of a talaq,' three fall, because when an indefinite noun is repeated as indefinite, the second is different from the first, so each part is complete. Unlike if he says, 'You are divorced half a talaq, its third, and its sixth,' where one falls, because the second and third are identical to the first, so all are parts of one talaq. Even if he adds to the one, a second falls, and similarly for a third. This is the chosen view of a group of mashayikh, and in al-Muhit and al-Walwaljiyyah it is the chosen view. Al-Hasan also mentioned it in al-Mujarrad because he added to the parts of one talaq, so the addition must come from another talaq, thereby completing the addition. The most correct regarding the unity of the referent is that if parts of one increase, one falls because he added the parts to one. This is textually stated in al-Mabsut. Accordingly, if he says, 'You are divorced one and its half,' one falls, as in al-Dhakhirah, unlike 'one and a half.' As for the unconsummated wife, only one falls in all scenarios, as in al-Bada'i. His statement: 'And the most correct regarding the unity of the referent...' I say: The first is supported by what recently passed of his saying 'restricted by his saying two talaqs' because if he said 'three halves of a talaq' two talaqs fall, except that a distinction is made: his talaq, the added-to, is indefinite, and the idafa operates like the definite article alif-lam, so it denotes the genus, unlike the talaq to which the pronoun 'its half, its third, its quarter' refers, which is a specific one, so the additional part is ignored. Reflect.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the number of talaqat that fall when a husband expresses divorce using fractions (half, third, sixth). The author, Ibn Nujaym, follows the Hanafi madhhab. The ruling distinguishes between two cases: (1) When the fractions are attached to the word 'talaqah' each time using the indefinite form (e.g., 'half a talaq, third of a talaq'), each fraction is considered a separate talaq because repeating an indefinite noun makes each instance a distinct entity. Thus, three halves/thirds/sixths add up to three talaqat (or as per the table). (2) When the fractions are attached to one definite talaq (e.g., 'half of it, third of it, sixth of it'), all fractions refer to the same talaq, so only one talaq falls. The text provides a detailed breakdown for various combinations (one half, two halves, three halves, four halves) and their outcomes. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if parts of a single talaq are added, they still constitute one talaq, as stated in al-Mabsut and al-Dhakhirah. For a non-consummated wife, only one talaq falls regardless of the expression because her talaq is effective with a single utterance. The marginal note clarifies that the indefinite idafa (construct) functions like the definite article, so 'half of a talaq' denotes the genus, making each occurrence independent, whereas the pronoun 'its' in 'its half' refers back to a specific, definite talaq, making all fractions part of that one talaq.

Key Terms
تطليقة (talqah): A single instance or unit of divorce; the minimal count of talaq.
منكر (nakirah): An indefinite noun (without alif-lam) that, when repeated, denotes a new instance.
استنباط (istinbat): Legal deduction from established principles rather than transmitted text.
مختار (mukhtar): The chosen, relied-upon view in the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his consummated wife, 'You are divorced half a talaq, third of a talaq, and sixth of a talaq,' how many talaqat fall according to the Hanafi mufta bihi?
A) One talaq
B) Two talaqat
C) Three talaqat
D) No talaq

Key Takeaway
The number of talaqat in fractional expressions hinges on whether the fractions are attached to indefinite instances (each counts separately) or to a single definite referent (all belong to one talaq, but excess parts trigger additional talaqat).

Distribution of Talaq Among Multiple Wives: Bayn vs Ishrak
Explaining the distribution of talaq using bayn and ashrakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings when a husband uses 'bayn' (between) and 'ashrakat' (I share you) and apply the correct distribution in scenarios involving prior talaq.

Core Idea
When no talaq has fallen, 'between' divides equally; after a talaq has fallen, 'I share you' duplicates the number.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle but critical point in Hanafi talaq law: how to distribute divorce pronouncements when a husband addresses multiple wives using the words 'bayn' or 'ashrakat'. The key distinction hinges on whether a talaq has already fallen on one wife before the sharing expression. Understanding this prevents mistaken application that could lead to invalid divorces or unlawful remarriages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase used
Determine whether the husband said 'bayn' (between) or 'ashrakat' (I share you). This determines the default ruling: 'bayn' implies division, 'ashrakat' implies duplication of an existing talaq.
2
Check for prior talaq
Ask whether any talaq has already fallen on a wife before the current pronouncement. If yes, the prior talaq is fixed and cannot be reduced.
This is the critical condition that changes the ruling.
3
Apply distribution rule for 'bayn' without prior talaq
If no prior talaq exists, divide the total number of talaqat equally among all wives named. If the division results in a fraction, Hanafi rule treats it as a full talaq per wife (the 'fraction is completed').
Example: 1 talaq among 4 wives = each gets 1 (quarter is completed).
4
Apply rule for 'ashrakat' with prior talaq
If a prior talaq has already fallen on at least one wife, the second wife receives the same number of talaqat as the first. The prior talaq is not divided; it is duplicated.
Example: first wife got 3, second gets 3, not 1.5.
5
Note the exception for collective intention
If the husband says 'bayn' but intends each talaq to be between all wives collectively, then each wife receives the full number of talaqat, not a divided portion.
This exception applies only when the intention is clearly stated or proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودل كلامه أنه لو قال لأربع نسوة بينكن تطليقة طلقت كل واحدة واحدة لأن الربع يتكامل، وكذا بينكن تطليقتان أو ثلاث أو أربع إلا إذا نوى أن كل تطليقة بينهن جميعاً فيقع في التطليقتين على كل منهما تطليقتان وفي الثلاث ثلاث. ولو قال بينكن خمس تطليقات وقع على كل واحدة ثنتان إلى ثمان ولو قال بينكن تسع وقع على كل واحدة ثلاث. ولفظ «اشرکتکن کلفظ (بین) بخلاف ما لو طلق امرأتين كل واحدة ثم قال لثالثة شركتك فيما أوقعت عليهما يقع عليها تطليقتان لأنه شركها في كل تطليقة. ولو طلقها تم قال لأخرى أشركتك معها في الطلاق وقع على الثانية ثلاث بخلاف ما تقدم لأن هناك لم يسبق وقوع شيء فلم يقسم بينهن وهنا قد أوقع الثلاث على الأولى فلا يمكنه رفع شيء منه ولو قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً ثم قال لأخرى أشركتك فيما أوقعت عليها، ثم ثلاثاً. قوله: (بخلاف ما تقدم أي من قوله بينكن تطليقة أو تطليقتان أو ثلاث أو أربع أو خمس، وعبارة الفتح بخلاف ما تقدم لأن هناك لم يسبق وقوع شيء فينقسم الثلاث بينهن نصفين قسمة واحدة، وهنا قد أوقع الثلاث على الأولى فلا يمكنه أن يرفع شيئاً مما أوقع عليها بإشراك الثانية وإنما يسوي الثانية بها بإيقاع الثلاث عليها ولأنه لما وقع الثلاث على الأولى فكلامه في حق الثانية إشراك في كل واحدة من الثلاث ا.هـ. وبه علم أن قول المؤلف فلا يقسم بينهن صوابه فيقسم بإسقاط لا . قوله: (ولو نوى في (الثانية أي في المسألة الثانية من مسألتي المتن وهي التي غايتها إلى ثلاث
And his speech indicates that if he says to four women, 'Between you all one talaq,' each one is divorced one talaq because the quarter is completed. Similarly, 'between you two talaqat' or three or four, except if he intended that each talaq is between all of them collectively, then in two talaqat each gets two, and in three each gets three. If he says 'between you five talaqat,' each gets two up to eight; if he says 'nine,' each gets three. The word 'I share you' is like 'between'. Unlike if he divorced two women with one each, then said to a third, 'I share you in what I effected on both,' two talaqat fall on her because he shared her in each talaq. If he divorced her completely then said to another, 'I share you with her in the divorce,' three fall on the second, unlike the previous case because there no prior talaq had fallen so they were not divided; here he had already affected three on the first so he cannot remove anything from it. If he says 'You are divorced three,' then said to another, 'I share you in what I effected on her,' then three. His statement: 'Unlike what preceded' meaning from his statement 'between you one talaq' or two or three or four or five. The wording of al-Fath: 'Unlike what preceded because there no talaq had fallen yet, so the three are divided between them in one division; here he has already caused three to fall on the first, so he cannot remove anything from what he effected on her by sharing the second. Rather, he makes her equal to the second by affecting three on her. And because when three fell on the first, his speech regarding the second is sharing in each of the three.' End quote. From this it is known that the author's statement 'so it is not divided between them' should be 'so it is divided' by dropping 'not'. His statement: 'And if he intended in the second' i.e., in the second of the two issues of the text, which ends at three.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, now addressing the distribution of talaq among multiple wives when the husband uses phrases like 'baynakunna' (between you all) or 'ashrakatkunna' (I share you). The Hanafi ruling: When the expression is general without prior talaq, the number mentioned is divided equally among the wives. For example, with four wives and 'one talaq between you,' each gets one (one-fourth of the total is deemed a complete talaq per wife). With two or three talaqat, the division is according to the number intended. If the husband intends to share the same talaqat among all, the full number applies to each. However, if the husband has already divorced one wife with a specific number, and then says to another wife 'I share you in that,' the second wife receives the same number of talaqat as the first. This is because the first talaq is already fixed and cannot be reduced; sharing adds the same number. The key distinction: If no talaq has yet fallen, the number is divided fractionally; if a talaq has already fallen, sharing duplicates that number on the second wife. The marginal note from al-Fath clarifies this logical point. Also, when the husband says 'I share you in the separation' in cases like an emancipated slave woman's choice, or regarding a defect ('inan), li'an, ila', or khul', the same principle applies: the divorce is effective as a talaq.

Key Terms
بين (bayn): Between; used to indicate distribution among multiple recipients.
أشركتك (ashrakatuk): I share you; making the second wife a partner in the talaq already effected.
فرقة (firqah): Separation; in this context, a type of divorce (e.g., from khul', li'an).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband divorces his first wife with three talaqat. He then says to his second wife, 'I share you in what I effected on her.' How many talaqat fall on the second wife?
A) One talaq
B) One and a half talaqat
C) Three talaqat
D) No talaq

Key Takeaway
When 'between' is used without prior talaq, the number is divided equally; when 'I share you' is used after a prior talaq, the second wife receives the same number as the first.

Sharing Divorce with a Third Wife
Explaining the ruling and resolving a misunderstanding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband shares divorce with a third wife and explain the reasoning from Fath al-Qadir.

Core Idea
Sharing divorce includes the third wife in all divorces given to others, but she receives only three.

Imagine a husband divorces his first wife three times and his second wife three times. He then tells his third wife, 'I have shared you in what I pronounced upon them.' How many divorces does she receive? The answer hinges on the husband's intention and the principle that each wife can receive a maximum of three divorces. This lesson unpacks the ruling and addresses a common misunderstanding that the fatwa changed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Husband's Intention
The husband utters 'I have shared you in what I have pronounced upon them.' His intention is to include the third wife in the total divorces given to the first two wives.
The key term is 'ishrāk' (sharing).
2
Trace the Reasoning from Fath al-Qadir
The commentator explains that the third wife is considered shared in six divorces (three for each of the first two). Since a woman cannot receive more than three, three are effective and three are void.
This reasoning is based on the principle of intention in ambiguous utterances.
3
Address the Potential Misunderstanding
Some might think the fatwa changed, but the text clarifies it is the same: the third wife receives three divorces. There is no contradiction.
The fatwa is consistently that she gets three.
4
Confirm the Operative Ruling
The reliable Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that the third wife receives three divorces. This is based on the intention to share in all divorces.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and affirmed by later scholars.

Source Text

واحدة إلى ثنتين واحدة وإلى ثلاث ثنتان وواحدة في ثنتين واحدة إن لم ينو شيئاً أو نوى قال لثالثة أشركتك فيما أوقعت عليهما قال في فتح القدير : وقد ورد استفتاء فيها فبعد أن كتبنا تطلق الثلاث ثلاثاً ثلاثاً قلنا إن وقوعهن على الثالثة باعتبار أنه أشركها في ستة ا هـ . أنه يعني علل وقوع الثلاث على الثالثة بعد الإفتاء بأنه أشركها في ست أوقعها فيقع عليها الثلاث ويلغو ثلاث، وليس معناه أنه ظهر له شيء بخلاف ما أفتى به كما قد توهم.
From one to two is one, and to three is two, and one in two is one if he did not intend anything or intended. He said to a third: I have shared you in what I have pronounced upon them. He said in Fath al-Qadir: And a request for a fatwa came regarding this, so after we wrote that three women are divorced three each, we said that their occurrence on the third is based on the consideration that he shared her in six. It means he explained the occurrence of three on the third after the fatwa as that he shared her in six, so she falls three and three are considered void. It does not mean that something different from what he gave as a fatwa became apparent to him, as might be imagined.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the case where a husband, after divorcing two wives, says to a third wife: 'I have shared you in what I have pronounced upon them.' The text from Fath al-Qadir clarifies that the husband's intention is to share the third wife in the total divorces already given. Since the first two received three each (six total), the third also receives three. The reasoning is that by saying 'shared you in what I have pronounced,' he includes her in all six, but three of those are considered void (since a woman cannot be given more than three), so she effectively gets three. The passage stresses that this does not contradict the earlier fatwa; it is the same consistent ruling.

Key Terms
إشراك (ishrāk): The act of including another person in a divorce pronouncement originally made for someone else.
استفتاء (istiftā’): A request for a formal legal opinion (fatwa) on a specific issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) One

Key Takeaway
The intention to share divorce includes the third wife in all divorces given, but the maximum per wife is three, so three take effect and any excess are void.

Shared Divorce and Intention in Hanafi Fiqh
Providing rulings on shared divorce between two or four wives with intention and without

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shared divorce when the husband intends division, and distinguish between private intention (between him and Allah) and court enforcement (literal meaning).

Core Idea
In divorce, intention matters privately but the literal words bind in court.

This passage from al-Mabsut and al-Muhit examines scenarios where a husband pronounces divorce on multiple wives with an inner intention to share the number. The Hanafi school distinguishes between what is accepted between the husband and Allah (his true intention) and what is enforced in court (the apparent meaning). We will explore these rulings and understand the concept of mudayyin (one whose statement is accepted privately but not judicially).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the first case: husband says to two wives 'You are both thrice divorced' but intends the three to be divided between them. Determine the literal and intended meanings.
Literal: each gets three; intended: each gets 1.5? Actually he intends the total three to be split, so each gets one and a half? The text says each gets two because he intends the three between them, so each gets 1.5? No, the text says 'tutlaqu kullu wahidatin thintayn' – each gets two. So he intended to give three total, but when shared between two, each gets two? Wait, that seems off: three divided between two gives 1.5 and 1.5? But the text says each gets two, meaning he intended to give two to each? Actually the text: 'intending the three to be between them' means the three divorces are to be distributed: one to one wife and two to the other? But it says 'he is truthful between himself and Allah, so each is divorced twice.' The logic: if he intends the three to be between them, the maximum possible distribution under the words is two per wife (since he said 'both of you are thrice' – the phrase 'both' implies each gets three literally, but the intention is to share the set of three. The apparent meaning is that each is thrice, so court gives each thrice. Inwardly, if he intended the three to be divided, the maximum he could have meant is two for one and one for the other? But the text says each gets two. This is a nuance: the author considers the possible interpretations. We'll follow the text.
2
Understand the inward vs outward ruling
Note the principle: the husband is mudayyin (his statement accepted between him and Allah) but not in court. Inwardly, follow intention; outwardly, follow literal meaning.
This applies only when the literal meaning is contrary to intention but is a possible interpretation.
3
Apply to different numbers of wives
For four wives, if he intends three to be between them, each gets one inwardly but three in court.
The pattern: inward share = 3/number of wives, rounded? But text says each gets one for four wives. So inward share = total divorces intended divided equally? Actually he intended three total to be shared among four, so each gets 0.75? Text says each gets one. Likely because fractions are not possible, so the minimum integer distribution is one each, leaving some remaining? The author doesn't explain further; we follow the text.
4
Examine ishrak scenarios
When a husband first divorces one wife then later includes another, the second receives the same number as the first already received (if no payment) or less if the divorce was conditional on payment.
The ishrak transfers the number of divorces already given to the first.
5
Understand the compensation twist
If the first divorce was for a specified payment (khula'), including another wife does not obligate her to pay unless explicitly stated and accepted.
Sharing is only in the divorce itself, not in the financial obligation.

Source Text

وفي المبسوط : لو قال لامرأتين أنتما طالقتان ثلاثاً ينوي أن الثلاث بينهما فهو مدين فيما بينه الله تعالى فتطلق كل منهما ثنتين لأنه من محتملات لفظه لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يدين في القضاء فتطلق كل ثلاثاً، وكذا لو قال لأربع أنتن طوالق ثلاثاً . ينوي أن الثلاث بينهن فهو مدين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى فتطلق كل واحدة واحدة وفي القضاء تطلق كل ثلاثاً. وفي المحيط : فلانة طالق ثلاثاً وفلانة معها أو قال أشركت فلانة معها طلقتا ثلاثاً ثلاثاً، ولو طلق امرأته ثم قال لأخرى قد أشركتك في طلاقها طلقت واحدة، ولو قال لثالثة قد أشركتك في طلاقهما طلقت ثنتين ولو قال للرابعة قد أشركتك في طلاقهن طلقت ثلاثاً، ولو كان الطلاق على الأولى بمال مسمى ثم قال للثانية قد أشركتك في طلاقها طلقت ولم يلزمها المال لأن الاشتراك وجد في الطلاق لا في المال، ولو قال أشركتك في طلاقها على كذا من المال فإن قبلت لزمها الطلاق والمال وإلا فلا ا هـ . يتكلم على كونه بائناً أو رجعياً حيث لم يقل على كذا وينبغي أن يكون في المسألة الأولى رجعياً لأن البينونة لأجل المال ولم يوجد وينبغي أنه لو قال لها أنت طالق بائن أو بائن ناوياً ثم قال لأخرى أشركتك في طلاقها أن يقع على الثانية بائناً أيضاً.
And in al-Mabsut: If he said to two wives, 'You are both thrice divorced,' intending the three to be between them, then he is truthful between himself and Allah, so each is divorced twice because it is a possible interpretation of his words, but it is contrary to the apparent meaning so he is not believed in court, so each is divorced thrice. Similarly, if he said to four wives, 'You are all thrice divorced,' intending the three to be between them, he is truthful between himself and Allah, so each is divorced once, but in court each is divorced thrice. And in al-Muhit: 'So-and-so is thrice divorced and so-and-so with her,' or he said, 'I have shared so-and-so with her,' both are divorced thrice each. If he divorced his wife then said to another, 'I have shared you in her divorce,' she is divorced once. If he said to a third, 'I have shared you in their divorce,' she is divorced twice. If he said to a fourth, 'I have shared you in their divorce,' she is divorced thrice. If the divorce of the first was for a specified payment, then he said to the second, 'I have shared you in her divorce,' she is divorced but the payment is not obligatory because the sharing occurred in the divorce not in the payment. If he said, 'I have shared you in her divorce for such and such payment,' and she accepted, then the divorce and payment are obligatory; otherwise no. He speaks about whether it is ba'in or raj'i where he did not say 'for such and such.' And it should be that in the first case it is raj'i because the separation is due to the payment and it did not occur. And it should be that if he said to her 'You are divorced ba'in' or with ba'in intention, then said to another 'I have shared you in her divorce,' the second also becomes ba'in.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from al-Mabsut and al-Muhit on various scenarios of shared divorce. The first ruling distinguishes between a husband's intention (which is accepted between him and Allah) and court rulings (which follow the literal meaning). For two wives, if he intends the three divorces to be divided, each gets two inwardly but three in court. For four wives, each gets one inwardly but three in court. Then, al-Muhit presents specific cases: when a husband says a woman is thrice divorced and includes another with her, both are thrice divorced. If he divorces one then later includes another in that same divorce, the second gets one. The pattern follows that the shared amount equals the number of divorces already given to the previous ones. The passage also discusses cases where the first divorce was for compensation: the second wife does not owe that compensation unless explicitly stated and accepted. Finally, the commentary notes that in the absence of specifying a payment, the divorce is raj'i (revocable), but if the husband intended ba'in or said 'ba'in', then the second wife also becomes ba'in.

Key Terms
مدين (mudayyin): A person whose statement is accepted between him and Allah (inwardly) but not in court due to contrary apparent meaning.
إشراك (ishrāk): Including another wife in a previously pronounced divorce, transferring the number and type of divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his two wives 'You are both thrice divorced' but intends the three to be divided, what is the ruling in court?
A) Each gets one divorce
B) Each gets two divorces
C) Each gets three divorces
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, divorce rulings in court are based on the apparent meaning of the husband's words, not his inner intention.

Rulings on Sharing Divorce in Special Situations
Providing rulings on sharing divorce in cases of emancipation, impotence, li'an, ila', and khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on sharing divorce (ishrak) to special situations involving emancipation, impotence, li'an, ila', and khul', and identify the limitations on redirecting void divorces.

Core Idea
Sharing in divorce is effective only if the original separation is a divorce; void divorces cannot be redirected.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on 'ishrak fil-furqa'—the act of a husband telling another wife that he shares her in the separation from a first wife. The passage from al-Muhit clarifies which types of furqa allow effective sharing and which do not, including a case where a husband tries to redistribute extra divorces. Understanding these nuances prevents errors in talaq that could lead to unintended consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of furqa
Determine whether the separation from the first wife is classified as talaq or faskh (annulment). The ruling on ishrak hinges on this distinction.
2
Apply the ishrak ruling for divorce-type furqa
If the original separation is a divorce (talaq) or analogous (like li'an, ila', or khul'), the second wife is divorced ba'in upon the husband's statement of ishrak.
Impotence (unna) is also treated as a divorce for this purpose.
3
Consider the exception: slave woman's choice
For the freed slavewoman who chooses separation, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The majority view holds that ishrak is effective, but Muhammad (via Abu Sulayman) holds it is not, as her choice is not a divorce.
This shows the nuance: not all separations are equal.
4
Handle the void divorces scenario
If a husband says 'you are divorced five' and the wife accepts three, the remaining two are void (since more than three are not possible). Attempting to redirect these void divorces to other wives has no effect.
Void divorces cannot be transferred or redirected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال في المحيط أيضاً : ولو أعتقت الأمة المنكوحة فاختارت نفسها فقال زوجها لامرأة أخرى له قد أشركتك في فرقة هذه طلقت بائناً وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث وحكى أبو سليمان عند محمد أنها لا تطلق. ولو قال في فرقة العنين واللعان والإيلاء والخلع قد أشركتك في فرقة هذه طلقت لأن هذه الفرقة فرقة طلاق بخلاف الأولى، ولو قال لامرأته أنت طالق خمس تطليقات فقالت ثلاث تكفيني فقال ثلاث لك والباقي على صواحبك وقع الثلاث عليها ولم يقع شيء على غيرها لأن الباقي بعد الثلاث صار لغواً فقد صرف اللغو إلى صواحبها فلا يقع شيء
Then he said in al-Muhit also: If a married slave woman was emancipated and chose herself, then her husband said to another wife of his, 'I have shared you in the separation from this one,' she is divorced ba'in, and if he intended three then three. And Abu Sulayman reported from Muhammad that she is not divorced. And if he said regarding separation from an impotent man, or li'an, or ila', or khul', 'I have shared you in the separation from this one,' she is divorced because this separation is a separation by divorce, unlike the first. And if he said to his wife, 'You are divorced five times,' and she said, 'Three suffice me,' and he said, 'Three for you and the rest on your co-wives,' then three fall upon her and nothing falls upon others because the remainder after three became void, and he directed the void to her co-wives, so nothing falls.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit covers rulings on sharing in divorce (ishrak) in special situations. First, if a married slave woman is emancipated and chooses to leave her husband (the option of puberty), and the husband then tells another wife 'I have shared you in the separation from this one,' the second wife becomes ba'in divorced. If he intended three, she gets three. However, there is a narration from Abu Sulayman from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) that she is not divorced. Second, for separations due to impotence (unna), li'an (mutual imprecation), ila' (vow of abstinence), and khul' (divorce by payment), if the husband tells another wife 'I have shared you in the separation from this one,' she is divorced because these are considered divorces (or legal separations analogous to divorce). This contrasts with the first case of the slave woman's choice, which is not considered a divorce but a dissolution due to the option. Third, a case where a husband says 'you are divorced five' and the wife accepts three, he says 'three for you and the rest on your co-wives.' Since a woman cannot receive more than three, the extra two are void, and redirecting them to other wives has no effect because they were already void.

Key Terms
فرقة (furqa): Separation or dissolution of marriage; here it encompasses various types including divorce, khul', etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says to another woman 'I have shared you in the separation from this one' in a case of khul', what happens?
A) She is not divorced
B) She is divorced
C) Only if she accepts
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
The effectiveness of sharing in divorce depends on the nature of the original separation: only those that are considered talaq or analogous to talaq allow ishrak to cause divorce.

Effect of 'From One to Two' in Divorce
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the word 'to' includes the endpoint in divorce expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the inclusion of the endpoint in divorce expressions and apply the distinction between permissive and risky matters to determine the number of divorces.

Core Idea
In divorce, 'to' does not include the final number because divorce is a matter of risk, not permission.

You might assume that saying 'from one to three' in divorce means three divorces, but the Hanafi school has a nuanced view. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his disciples, showing how the nature of the subject—permissive vs. risky—affects linguistic interpretation and leads to different conclusions within the same school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify what Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say about 'from one to two' and 'from one to three'.
Abu Hanifa: first endpoint included, second not. Sahibayn: both included.
2
Analyze the basis of each view
Understand the proofs: Abu Hanifa relies on the risky nature of divorce; the Sahibayn rely on common custom (ta'aruf) and istihsan.
Divorce is originally restricted, so custom does not apply as broadly.
3
Apply the permissive vs. risky distinction
See how Abu Hanifa uses examples from permissive matters (buying, selling) to distinguish them from risky ones (divorce).
In permissive contexts, custom includes endpoints; in risky, it does not.
4
Examine the logical argument
Work through the dependency: in 'from one to three', the second divorce depends on the first, but the third is not necessary, showing the second endpoint is not included.
The text explains this in the commentary.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Recognize that the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view, as supported by detailed reasoning and later authorities.
Tarjih is based on the risk factor and logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن واحدة أو ما بين واحدة إلى ثنتين واحدة وإلى ثلاث ثنتان) يعني عند أبي حنيفة فتدخل الغاية الأولى دون الثانية، وقالا بدخولهما فيقع في الأولى ثنتان وفي الثانية ثلاث استحساناً بالتعارف إلا أنهما أطلقا فيه وأبو حنيفة يقول : إنما تدخل الغايتان عرفاً فيما مرجعه الإباحة كخذ من مالي من عشرة إلى مائة وبع عبدي بمال من مائة إلى ألف، وكل من الملح إلى الحلو فله أخذ المائة والبيع بألف وأكل الحلواء، وأما ما أصله الخطر حتى يباح إلا لدفع الحاجة ،فلا والطلاق منه فكان قرينة على عدم إرادة الكل غير أن الغاية الأولى لا بد من وجودها ليرتب عليها الطلقة الثانية في صورة إيقاعها وهي صورة من واحدة إلى ثلاث إذ لا ثانية بلا أولى ووجود الطلاق عين وقوعه بخلاف الغاية الثانية وهي ثلاث في هذه الصورة فإنه يصح وقوع الثانية بلا ثالثة أما صورة من واحدة إلى ثنتين فلا حاجة إلى إدخالها لأنها إنما دخلت ضرورة إيقاع الثانية وهو منتف وإيقاع الواحدة ليس باعتبار إدخالها غاية بل بما ذكرنا من انتفاء العرف فيه فلا تدخل فيلغو قوله من واحدة إلى ثنتين ويقع بطالق واحدة ولا يرد أنت طالق ثانية حيث لا يقع إلا واحدة لأن ثانية لغو فيقع بأنت طالق. وقد ظهر بهذا التقرير أن الاختلاف إنما نشأ من اعتبار إثبات العرف وعدمه مع الاتفاق على اعتبار العرف فلا يرد دخول المرافق لأن العرف لما أدخل ما بعد إلى تارة وأخرجه أخرى وكان الاحتياط الدخول فإن قيل : ما بين هذا وهذا يستدعي وجود الأمرين ووجودهما وقوعهما فيقع الثلاث الجواب أن ذلك في المحسوسات، وأما ما نحن فيه من الأمور المعنوية فإنما يقتضي الأول احتمال وجود الثاني عرفاً، ففيما بين الستين إلى السبعين يصدق إذا لم يبلغ السبعين. كذا في فتح القدير. وفي جامع الفصولين : لو باع بالخيار إلى غد دخل الغد في الخيار ، ولو حلف ليقضين دينه إلى خمسة أيام لا يحنث ما لم تغرب الشمس من اليوم الخامس، وكذا لا يكلمه إلى عشرة أيام دخل العاشر وكذا في إن تزوجت إلى عشر سنين دخلت العاشرة، وأما في الإجازة ففي بعض الكتب : لو أجر إلى خمس سنين دخلت الخامسة، وفي عامة الكتب لا تدخل ا هـ . و تمام تقريره في شرحنا المسمى بتعليق الأنوار على أصول المنار. ولو نوى في الثانية واحدة دين ديانة لا قضاء لأنه يحتمله وهو خلاف الظاهر. وأشار بقوله إلى ثنتين إلى أنه لو قال من واحدة إلى واحدة تقع واحدة بالأولى اتفاقاً، وقيل لا يقع شيء عند زفر أي في قوله من واحدة إلى واحدة.
His saying: (And from one or between one to two is one, and to three is two) meaning according to Abu Hanifa: the first endpoint is included but not the second. The two disciples say both are included, so in the first case two occur and in the second three, by istihsan based on common custom, but they are absolute. Abu Hanifa says: Both endpoints are included in custom only in matters that are permissive (ibaha), like 'take from my money from ten to one hundred' or 'sell my slave for a price from one hundred to one thousand' or 'eat from salt to sweet', so he may take one hundred, sell for one thousand, eat sweet. But in matters that are originally risky (khatar) and only become permissible out of necessity, like divorce, it is not included; so the word 'to' indicates he did not intend all. However, the first endpoint must exist because the second divorce depends on it in the case of 'from one to three', since there is no second without the first, and the existence of the divorce is its very occurrence, unlike the second endpoint (three) because it is possible to have the second without the third. As for the case of 'from one to two', there is no need to include the endpoint, because inclusion would be necessary only for the occurrence of the second, which is absent; the occurrence of the one is not due to inclusion of the endpoint but due to the absence of custom, so it is not included, and his saying 'from one to two' becomes void, and one divorce occurs by 'you are divorced'. It cannot be said 'you are divorced a second' because only one occurs; 'second' is void and it falls by 'you are divorced'. It has become apparent from this explanation that the difference arises from affirming custom or not, despite agreement on considering custom. So one cannot object that the inclusion of what comes after 'to' sometimes enters and sometimes exits, and caution requires inclusion. If it is said: 'what is between this and that' requires the existence of both, so three occur, the answer is that this applies to physical things, but in our case of abstract matters, it only suggests the possibility of the second customarily. For example, 'between sixty and seventy' is true even if not reaching seventy. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if he sells with an option until tomorrow, tomorrow enters the option; if he swears to pay his debt within five days, he does not break the oath until sunset of the fifth; similarly, 'I will not speak to him for ten days' includes the tenth; similarly, 'if I marry within ten years' includes the tenth. As for ijarah, in some books: if he rents for five years, the fifth enters; in most books it does not. If he intends in the second case one divorce, he is truthful in private but not in court because it is possible but contrary to the apparent meaning. His saying 'to two' indicates that if he said 'from one to one', one divorce occurs by the first, by agreement. It is said that nothing occurs according to Zufar in his saying 'from one to one'.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the interpretation of the phrase 'from one to two' or 'from one to three' in divorce. The core issue is whether the word 'to' (ila) includes the endpoint number. Abu Hanifa holds that the first endpoint is included (so 'from one to two' yields one divorce, because 'to two' means up to but not including two, but the first endpoint one is included) but the second is not. Thus, in 'from one to three', only the first divorce occurs, but the second? Wait, the text says: 'and to three is two' meaning from one to three yields two divorces according to Abu Hanifa? Actually, the text says 'وإلى ثلاث ثنتان' meaning 'to three is two'. So Abu Hanifa: 'from one to two' yields one, 'from one to three' yields two. The disciples: both endpoints are included, so 'from one to two' yields two, 'from one to three' yields three. They use istihsan based on common custom (ta'aruf). Abu Hanifa distinguishes: in permissive matters (ibaha), custom includes both endpoints, but in matters of risk (khatar) like divorce, it does not. He also uses a special reasoning: in 'from one to three', the second divorce depends on the first, so the first must be included, but the third is not necessarily included because the second can occur without the third. In 'from one to two', there is no need to include the second endpoint because there is no third divorce to trigger. The passage also includes analogies from other areas (oaths, options, ijarah) to illustrate the principle. Finally, Zufar's opinion that 'from one to one' yields nothing is mentioned.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāya): The endpoint or limit in a numerical expression; here it refers to the number after 'to' (ila).
تعارف (ta‘āruf): Common custom or conventional usage in language.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; preferring a ruling based on custom or equity over strict analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the interpretation of 'to' in divorce depends on whether the subject is permissive or risky; for divorce, the endpoint is not included.
