Commentary
This passage is a multi-part legal discussion. It begins with a methodological aside about classification of sciences (as a commentary on the word 'fasl'?), then returns to the main ruling on divorce tied to time. The key point is the inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafi, Shafi'i, and Ahmad hold that divorce added to a future time does not take effect immediately; Malik holds it does if the time is inevitable (like sunrise or Ramadan). The Hanafi proof is analogy (qiyas) with tadbir (manumission after death), where the condition (death) is inevitable yet the effect is delayed. The text then states the general principle: divorce cannot be time-limited (ta'qit), so when the preposition 'ila' is used (e.g., 'anti taliq ila 'asharat ayyam'), it means 'after' (ba'd), not 'until'. The passage then lists numerous contracts and their rulings regarding ta'qit: manumission (like divorce), suretyship (immediate or after month according to different views), the option of the hand, sale (deferment of price), agency (accepts ta'qit), hire, muzara'ah, partnership (like hire), sulh and division (invalid with ta'qit), ibra' (like divorce but intention matters), iqrar (if confirmed by the other party, term stands; if denied, immediate), slave's permission (no ta'qit), tahkim and qada (accept ta'qit), prohibition of agent from selling (accepts ta'qit). Then there is a note about oaths: 'anta kadha idha ja'a ghad' is a vow, but 'anta kadha ghadan' is not because it is simply an addition (idafah). Finally, the rule for divorce added to two future times: it takes effect at the earlier of them; if suspended on two actions, at the later. The text concludes with a correction: in al-Bazzaziyyah the word 'la' (not) is omitted or there is a hidden word; the author will discuss later. The overall unit is a comprehensive treatment of ta'qit in various areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
تأقيت (ta'qit): Time-limiting: specifying a time limit for a legal act. Divorce does not accept ta'qit, so 'ila' means 'after'.
إلى بمعنى بعد (ila bi-ma'na ba'd): The preposition 'ila' in the context of divorce is interpreted as 'after', not 'until', because divorce cannot be suspended up to a time.
تدبير (tadbir): Manumission after death: a master declares a slave will be free upon the master's death. Used as an analogy to prove delayed effect despite inevitable condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately
B) After one month
C) Throughout the month
D) At the end of the month

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'ila' in divorce means 'after' because the act cannot be time-limited; this is supported by the analogy of tadbir.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Divorce Added to a Future Time: The Hanafi vs. Maliki Dispute
States the ruling on divorce added to a future time and discusses inter-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce added to a future time, explain why it does not occur immediately using the analogy of tadbir, and identify the practical difference between Hanafi and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
Divorce added to a future time occurs at that time, not immediately—just as tadbir defers freedom until death.

When a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow', does the divorce happen now or when tomorrow comes? The Hanafi school, along with Shafi'i and Ahmad, holds that the divorce is deferred to the first part of the named time. Imam Malik, however, argues that if the time is certain (like sunrise or Ramadan), the divorce occurs immediately. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these positions, focusing on the Hanafi reliance on qiyas with tadbir (deferred manumission). Understanding this dispute clarifies a fundamental principle: certainty of a future event does not necessitate immediate legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Ruling
From the opening statement: if the husband says 'you are divorced tomorrow' or 'on tomorrow', she is divorced at dawn of that day. The divorce does not occur immediately.
The word 'tomorrow' refers to the whole day; the first part (dawn) is chosen because there is no competing time.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The passage states that when divorce is added to a future time, it does not occur at the moment of utterance. This is the general principle shared by Shafi'i and Ahmad.
The text then contrasts with the Maliki view.
3
Examine the Maliki View
Malik holds that if the future time is certain (e.g., 'when the sun rises' or 'when Ramadan enters'), the divorce occurs immediately because the time is inevitable.
His reasoning: certainty of the time makes it as if the condition is already fulfilled.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response via Tadbir
The Hanafi school refutes Malik's view using qiyas with tadbir: a master says 'you are free upon my death'. Death is certain, but freedom is not immediate. Similarly, divorce added to a certain future time is deferred.
If Malik's logic were correct, tadbir would also require immediate freedom, which is not the case.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, the wife remains married until dawn tomorrow; under Maliki, she is divorced immediately upon utterance (subject to preconditions).
This difference affects iddah, maintenance, and remarriage rights.

لكثرة فوائدها وهي مذكورة في البزازية من فصل الأمر باليد. وفيها من الأيمان أنت كذا إذا جاء غد يمين أنت كذا غداً ليس بيمين لأنه إضافة والطلاق المضاف إلى وقتين ينزل عند أولهما والمعلق بالفعلين عند التوقيت بخلاف الطلاق حتى لو قال أنت طالق إلى عشرة أيام تكون إلى بمعنى بعد» لأن تأجيل الوقوع غير ممكن فأجل الإيقاع، ولو نوى أن يقع في الحال يقع .اهـ. فتعين أن تكون كلمة «لا» ساقطة سهواً أو يكون على حذف His statement: (You are divorced tomorrow or on tomorrow) she is divorced at dawn, because he described her with divorce in all of the first, because all of it is what is named 'tomorrow', so the first part is determined due to no competitor. In the second, he described her in a part of it, and it indicates that when he adds it to a time, it does not occur immediately, and that is the view of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad. But this is invalidated by tadbir, because death definitely comes at its time but it is not immediate. Thus al-Mi'raj. The sale to a month is a deferment of the price; agency accepts timing such that if he acts after the time it is invalid; in lease to a month, what follows the contract is determined and ends with its passing, and similarly in sharecropping; partnership to a month is like lease; reconciliation to a month and division to it are not valid; absolution (ibra') to a month is like divorce, unless he says 'I intended delay', then it is a deferment to it; acknowledgment (iqrar) to two months: if the acknowledgee confirms, the term is established; if he denies, the payment is immediate and the statement is his. The permission of a slave cannot be timed; arbitration and judgeship can accept timing; prohibition of an agent from selling for a day is timed. This sentence is to explain what is timed and what is not; I mentioned it here due to its many benefits, and it is mentioned in al-Bazzaziyya in the chapter of 'command with the hand'. And in it from oaths: 'You are such if tomorrow comes' is an oath; 'You are such tomorrow' is not an oath, because it is an addition. Divorce added to two times occurs at the first of them; and suspended on two acts occurs at the last of them. 'Until one year' occurs after the year, because divorce does not admit timing, so this is an addition of the occurrence to after the year. Thus the ruling is in agreement, and the cause is different from here. And in al-Bazzaziyya in 'command with the hand', after mentioning that the command can be timed unlike divorce, such that if he said 'You are divorced up to ten days', 'to' is in the sense of 'after', because deferring the occurrence is impossible, so it defers the occurrence; if he intended immediate occurrence, it occurs. Thus it is determined that the word 'not' is dropped inadvertently, or it is with ellipsis of the qualified object, i.e., the occurrence of divorce. His statement: 'And divorce added to two times' means both future; if one is immediate, its explanation will come at his statement 'and on today tomorrow'.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the ruling when a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' or 'on tomorrow'. The Hanafi position (along with Shafi'i and Ahmad) is that divorce does not occur immediately but at the beginning of the stated future time (dawn of tomorrow). Malik holds that it occurs immediately because the time will inevitably come, but this is refuted by analogy to tadbir, where a master says to a slave 'you are free upon my death'—death inevitably comes but freedom is not immediate. The passage then explains that divorce can be timed, and when the particle 'ila' (to) is used, it means 'after' in the context of divorce, because divorce cannot be deferred in occurrence; rather the declaration is postponed. A lengthy comparison follows: various contracts (guarantee, agency, lease, etc.) are evaluated regarding whether they accept timing or not. For example, guarantee to a month: the relied-upon position is that the guarantor is liable after the month, not immediately. The passage concludes with a note that the statement in al-Bazzaziyya about 'command with the hand' requires a correction: the word 'not' (la) may be missing, or there is ellipsis.

Key Terms
التدبير (al-tadbir): A declaration that a slave will be free upon the master's death; it is a contingent manumission.
إلى (ila): In the context of divorce, 'ila' means 'after' (ba'd), indicating that the divorce is added to a future time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately upon utterance
B) After ten days
C) At the end of ten days, with 'ila' meaning 'after'
D) It is invalid because divorce cannot be timed

Key Takeaway
Divorce added to a future time is deferred to that time, not immediate—a principle established by qiyas with tadbir and upheld by the majority of the Hanafi school.

Timing of Divorce Added to One of Two Times or Suspended on Acts
Explains basic rules for divorce timing with two alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a divorce occurs when the husband uses alternative times or suspends on acts, applying the Hanafi rules from al-Ziyadat.

Core Idea
Divorce added to two times occurs at the later; suspended on acts occurs at the first.

This lesson covers the first part of the passage, which establishes the default rules for divorce statements that specify alternative times or conditional acts. Understanding these basic formulations is essential before moving to more complex descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formulation
Determine whether the husband said 'tomorrow or the day after' (two times) or 'if you speak to so-and-so or if you enter the house' (two acts), or a combination of an act and a time.
2
Apply rule for two times
If it is added to one of two times, the divorce occurs at the later time. Example: 'you are divorced tomorrow or the day after' → occurs after the day after.
Hanafi position: the default is the later time because the statement implies uncertainty resolved by choosing the later.
3
Apply rule for two acts
If suspended on one of two acts, it occurs at the first act that happens. Example: 'you are divorced if you speak to Zayd or if you enter the house' → occurs at whichever occurs first.
This is straightforward: the first fulfillment triggers the divorce.
4
Apply rule for act and time combined
According to al-Ziyadat, if the act occurs first, the divorce occurs immediately and the time is irrelevant; if the time occurs first, the divorce does not occur until the act is done.
This shows the priority of act over time in the Hanafi view.
5
Examine exception clauses
If the husband says 'you are thrice divorced except one tomorrow or if you speak to so-and-so', two divorces are suspended on the coming of tomorrow and the act, respectively.
The exception reduces the number of divorces and attaches the remaining to the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والمضاف إلى أحد الوقتين كقوله غداً أو بعد غد طلقت بعد غد، ولو علق بأحد الفعلين ينزل عند أولهما، والمعلق بفعل أو وقت يقع بأيهما سبق. وفي الزيادات إن وجد الفعل أولاً يقع ولا ينظر وجود الوقت، وإن وجد الوقت أولاً لا يقع ما لم يوجد الفعل ا هـ. وفيها من فصل الاستثناء : أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا واحدة غداً أو إن كلمت فلاناً تعلق ثنتان لمجيء الغد وكلام فلان اهـ. وفي المحيط : ولو قال أنت طالق تطليقة تقع عليك غداً تطلق حين يطلع الفجر فإنه وصف التطليقة بما تتصف به فإنها تتصف بالوقوع غداً بأن كانت مضافة إلى الغد فلا تقع بدون ذلك الوصف، ولو قال تطليقة لا تقع إلا غداً طلقت للحال لأنه وصفها بما لا تتصف به إذ ليس من الطلاق ما لا يقع إلا في الغد بل يتصور وقوعه حالاً واستقبالاً فلغى ذكر الوصف فبقي مرسلاً كما لو قال أنت طالق تطليقة تصير أو تصبح غداً، ولو قال أنت طالق بعد يوم الأضحى تطلق حين يمضي اليوم لأن البعدية صفة للطلاق لما بينا فصار الطلاق مضافاً إلى ما بعد يوم الأضحى فلم يقع قبله، ولو قال بعدها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال لأن البعدية صفة لليوم فيتأخر اليوم عن الطلاق فبقي الطلاق مرسلاً غير مضاف . ولو مع يوم الأضحى طلقت حين يطلع فجره لأن «مع» للقرآن فقد جعل الوقوع مقارناً ليوم الأضحى، ولو قال معها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال لأن حرف مع» هنا دخلت على الوقت فصار مضيفاً الوقت إلى الطلاق، وإضافة الوقت إلى الطلاق باطل لأنه مما لا يتجزى فيبقى الطلاق مرسلاً كما لو قال أنت طالق قبلها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال ا هـ . وفي الذخيرة : الحاصل أن الطلاق إذا أضيف إلى وقت لا يقع ما لم يجيء ذلك الوقت، وإن أضيف الوقت إلى الطلاق وقع للحال وتوضيحه فيها، وقيد بقوله «غداً» لأنه لو قال أنت طالق لا طلقت الساعة واحدة وفي الغد أخرى. كذا في المحيط معزياً إلى أبي يوسف.
He said: And [divorce] added to one of two times, like his saying 'tomorrow or the day after', she is divorced after the day after. And if he suspends on one of two acts, it occurs at the first of them. And what is suspended on an act or time occurs by whichever comes first. In al-Ziyadat: If the act occurs first, it occurs and the existence of the time is not considered; if the time occurs first, it does not occur without the act. In it from the chapter on exception: 'You are thrice divorced except one tomorrow or if you speak to so-and-so', two [divorce]s are suspended on the coming of tomorrow and speaking to so-and-so. In al-Muhit: If he said 'You are divorced a divorce that falls on you tomorrow', she is divorced when dawn breaks, because he described the divorce with what it can be described by: it can be described as occurring tomorrow by being added to tomorrow, so it does not occur without that description. If he said 'a divorce that does not occur except tomorrow', she is divorced immediately, because he described it with what it cannot be described by, since there is no divorce that does not occur except tomorrow; rather it is conceivable to occur immediately or later, so the mention of the description is void, so it remains unqualified, as if he said 'you are divorced a divorce that becomes or happens tomorrow'. If he said 'you are divorced after the day of Adha', she is divorced when the day passes, because 'after' is a description of the divorce as we explained, so the divorce becomes added to after the day of Adha, so it does not occur before it. If he said 'after it the day of Adha', she is divorced immediately, because 'after' is a description of the day, so the day is delayed from the divorce, so the divorce remains unqualified without addition. And if he said 'with the day of Adha', she is divorced at dawn, because 'with' indicates companionship, so he made the occurrence simultaneous with the day of Adha. If he said 'with it the day of Adha', she is divorced immediately, because the particle 'with' here enters the time, so it becomes adding the time to the divorce, and adding the time to divorce is invalid because it is indivisible, so the divorce remains unqualified, just as if he said 'before it the day of Adha', she is divorced immediately. In al-Dhakhira: The summary is that if divorce is added to a time, it does not occur until that time comes; if the time is added to the divorce, it occurs immediately, and its explanation is there. And it is restricted by his saying 'tomorrow', because if he says 'you are divorced', she is divorced instantly, and [if he says] 'tomorrow' another. Thus in al-Muhit, attributed to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage provides rulings for various formulations of divorce added to times or conditions. It begins with the rule: if divorce is added to one of two alternative times (e.g., 'tomorrow or the day after'), it occurs at the later time (after the day after). If it is suspended on one of two acts, it occurs at the first act. If it is suspended on both an act and a time, it occurs at whichever comes first, but according to al-Ziyadat, if the act comes first, the time is irrelevant; if the time comes first, it does not occur until the act is done. The text then presents examples from al-Muhit: describing a divorce as 'a divorce that falls on you tomorrow' means it occurs at dawn; but if one says 'a divorce that does not occur except tomorrow', the negation of occurrence before tomorrow is not a valid description, so the description is void and the divorce takes immediate effect. There are further examples with 'after the day of Adha': when 'after' qualifies the divorce, it is delayed; when it qualifies the day, the divorce is immediate. Similarly, the particle 'ma'a' (with) can make the divorce simultaneous with the day or, if attached to the time, invalid and immediate. The overarching principle from al-Dhakhira: if the divorce is added to a time, it waits for the time; if the time is added to the divorce, it occurs immediately. The passage ends with a note that the rule applies to 'tomorrow', and if he just says 'you are divorced' without time, it is immediate.

Key Terms
المضاف إلى أحد الوقتين (al-mudaf ila ahad al-waqtayn): Divorce added to one of two alternative future times; e.g., 'you are divorced tomorrow or the day after'.
المعلق بفعل أو وقت (al-mu'allaq bi-fi'l aw waqt): Divorce suspended on either an act or a time; it occurs by whichever comes first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced tomorrow or the day after', when does the divorce occur?
A) Immediately
B) At dawn of tomorrow
C) After the day after
D) The divorce is invalid

Key Takeaway
Divorce added to two times occurs at the later time; suspended on two acts occurs at the first; combined act and time follows the act.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
15 min
Effect of Descriptions and Particles on the Timing of Divorce
Explains effects of descriptions and particles on divorce timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between divorce added to a time and time added to divorce, and apply the rule from al-Muhit that invalid descriptions are voided.

Core Idea
If the description is possible, divorce waits; if impossible, divorce is immediate.

This lesson focuses on the examples from al-Muhit about descriptive phrases and particles like 'ba'da' (after) and 'ma'a' (with). The key principle is whether the phrase qualifies the divorce or the time. When the description is impossible (e.g., 'a divorce that does not occur except tomorrow'), it is void and the divorce takes immediate effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether the description qualifies the divorce or the time
Examine the phrase structure. For example, 'a divorce that falls on you tomorrow' describes the divorce itself, so the divorce is added to tomorrow. 'After the day of Adha' describes the divorce as occurring after, so it is added to after.
If the description qualifies the divorce, the divorce is considered added to that time.
2
Check the validity of the description
A valid description is one that is possible for a divorce (e.g., occurring at a specific time). An invalid description is one that contradicts the nature of divorce (e.g., 'does not occur except tomorrow' – since divorce can occur immediately). Invalid descriptions are voided, making the divorce immediate.
Al-Muhit: 'she is divorced immediately because he described it with what it cannot be described by.'
3
Analyze 'ba'da' (after) constructions
If 'ba'da' describes the divorce (e.g., 'after the day of Adha'), divorce is delayed until after the day. If 'ba'da' describes the day (e.g., 'after it the day of Adha'), the day is delayed, not the divorce, so divorce is immediate.
The position of 'ba'da' determines whether it qualifies the divorce or the day.
4
Analyze 'ma'a' (with) constructions
If 'ma'a' is placed before the time (e.g., 'with the day of Adha'), it indicates simultaneity, so divorce occurs at dawn. If 'ma'a' is placed after the divorce (e.g., 'with it the day of Adha'), it adds time to divorce, which is invalid, making divorce immediate.
Adding time to divorce is invalid because time cannot be divided into divorce (al-Dhakhira).
5
Apply the overarching principle from al-Dhakhira
If divorce is added to time, it waits. If time is added to divorce, it is immediate. This summarizes all the examples.
Restricted to cases where 'tomorrow' is specified; if just 'you are divorced', it is immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والمضاف إلى أحد الوقتين كقوله غداً أو بعد غد طلقت بعد غد، ولو علق بأحد الفعلين ينزل عند أولهما، والمعلق بفعل أو وقت يقع بأيهما سبق. وفي الزيادات إن وجد الفعل أولاً يقع ولا ينظر وجود الوقت، وإن وجد الوقت أولاً لا يقع ما لم يوجد الفعل ا هـ. وفيها من فصل الاستثناء : أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا واحدة غداً أو إن كلمت فلاناً تعلق ثنتان لمجيء الغد وكلام فلان اهـ. وفي المحيط : ولو قال أنت طالق تطليقة تقع عليك غداً تطلق حين يطلع الفجر فإنه وصف التطليقة بما تتصف به فإنها تتصف بالوقوع غداً بأن كانت مضافة إلى الغد فلا تقع بدون ذلك الوصف، ولو قال تطليقة لا تقع إلا غداً طلقت للحال لأنه وصفها بما لا تتصف به إذ ليس من الطلاق ما لا يقع إلا في الغد بل يتصور وقوعه حالاً واستقبالاً فلغى ذكر الوصف فبقي مرسلاً كما لو قال أنت طالق تطليقة تصير أو تصبح غداً، ولو قال أنت طالق بعد يوم الأضحى تطلق حين يمضي اليوم لأن البعدية صفة للطلاق لما بينا فصار الطلاق مضافاً إلى ما بعد يوم الأضحى فلم يقع قبله، ولو قال بعدها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال لأن البعدية صفة لليوم فيتأخر اليوم عن الطلاق فبقي الطلاق مرسلاً غير مضاف . ولو مع يوم الأضحى طلقت حين يطلع فجره لأن «مع» للقرآن فقد جعل الوقوع مقارناً ليوم الأضحى، ولو قال معها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال لأن حرف مع» هنا دخلت على الوقت فصار مضيفاً الوقت إلى الطلاق، وإضافة الوقت إلى الطلاق باطل لأنه مما لا يتجزى فيبقى الطلاق مرسلاً كما لو قال أنت طالق قبلها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال ا هـ . وفي الذخيرة : الحاصل أن الطلاق إذا أضيف إلى وقت لا يقع ما لم يجيء ذلك الوقت، وإن أضيف الوقت إلى الطلاق وقع للحال وتوضيحه فيها، وقيد بقوله «غداً» لأنه لو قال أنت طالق لا طلقت الساعة واحدة وفي الغد أخرى. كذا في المحيط معزياً إلى أبي يوسف.

Saving the Time Specification: The Hanafi Dispute on 'Tomorrow' in Conditional Divorce
Internal Hanafi dispute on voiding time specifications in conditional divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the internal Hanafi dispute over the validity of 'tomorrow' in 'you are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house' and evaluate al-Maqdisi's argument for preserving the wording.

Core Idea
A time specification in a conditional divorce is not always void—it can be saved if it makes sense as a restriction.

In the previous lesson, we saw the difference in timing of the wife's option based on word order. Now we turn to a more subtle problem: when a man says 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house', does the word 'tomorrow' add anything, or is it legally void? The apparent ruling from al-Zahiriyya declares it void, but al-Maqdisi challenges this, arguing that 'tomorrow' can be preserved as a genuine restriction. Let us analyse this internal Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two formulations
First, note the clear ruling: 'If you wish, you are divorced tomorrow' gives the wife an immediate option, while 'You are divorced tomorrow if you wish' defers her option to tomorrow itself. This sets the stage for the problematic case.
The placement of 'tomorrow' relative to the condition matters for the timing of the option, but not for the core issue of voidness.
2
Identify the problematic case
The case is: 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house.' According to al-Zahiriyya, 'tomorrow' is void (laghw), so the divorce depends purely on entry—any time she enters, she is divorced.
This makes 'tomorrow' redundant, which is why al-Maqdisi finds it problematic.
3
Understand al-Maqdisi's objection
Al-Maqdisi argues that 'tomorrow' can be interpreted as a restriction: the divorce only occurs tomorrow if she enters; if she enters today, no divorce. This preserves the wording and is a common permissible restriction.
His reasoning: it is possible for a divorce to be restricted to a future time, so we should interpret accordingly to avoid voiding the word.
4
Analyse the author's additional problematic case
The author cites another case: 'You are divorced today if tomorrow comes'—here the divorce is tied to dawn of tomorrow, even though 'today' seems contradicted. This shows that immediate time words can be suspended by attaching them to future events, supporting al-Maqdisi's approach.
This analogy reinforces that time specifications can be effective even when they seem paradoxical.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: If she enters today, under al-Zahiriyya's view she is divorced; under al-Maqdisi's view she is not. The mufta bihi is not explicitly given, but al-Maqdisi's view is presented as a valid alternative that respects the speaker's words.
The text leans toward saving the wording from being void, but does not fully tarjiih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : إن شئت فأنت طالق غداً فالمشيئة إليها للحال بخلاف أنت طالق غداً إن شئت فإن المشيئة إليها في الغد. وفي الظهيرية : لو قال رجل لامرأته أنت طالق غداً إذا دخلت الدار يلغو ذكر الغد فيتعلق الطلاق بدخول الدار حتى لو دخلت في أي وقت كان طلقت وهذا مشكل، فإنه قوله (إذا ليس من الطلاق ما لا يقع إلا في الغد الخ قال المقدسي في شرحه : فيه بحث لأن كون الطلقة لا تقع إلا غداً وصف ممكن لها بالنسبة إلى ما قبله إذا أضيفت إليه أو علقت بمجيئه والقصر شائع سائغ فليحمل عليه صوناً له عن الإلغاء والله سبحانه أعلم .اهـ. ويتلخص من كلامه أنه لا يقع عليه وفي الحال ديانة إذا أراد التخصيص وإلا فظاهر الكلام لغو كما قالوا لأن الاستثناء أعم الأوقات أي لا تقع عليك في الأوقات الحالة و المستقبلة إلا في الغد فيلغو الوصف المذكور قوله (وهذا مشكل الخ) أقول : ويشكل عليه أيضاً ما سيأتي بعد ورقة ونصف من أنه إذا قال أنت طالق اليوم إذا جاء غد لا تطلق إلا بطلوع الفجر فتوقف المنجز لاتصال مغير الأول بالآخر .
In al-Bazzaziyya: 'If you wish, you are divorced tomorrow': the will (mashi'a) is hers immediately, unlike 'you are divorced tomorrow if you wish', where the will is hers tomorrow. In al-Zahiriyya: If a man said to his wife 'you are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house', the mention of 'tomorrow' is void, and the divorce depends on entering the house, so that if she enters at any time, she is divorced. This is problematic, because his statement (this is not from the divorce that does not occur except tomorrow...). Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: There is a discussion in this: because the fact that the divorce does not occur except tomorrow is a possible description for it with respect to what is before it if it is added to it or suspended on its coming, and restriction is common and permissible, so it should be interpreted that way to save it from being void. Allah knows best. To summarize his statement: It does not occur [immediately] and in the case [of intention] it occurs immediately in conscience (diyanatan) if he intended specification; otherwise, the apparent speech is void as they said, because the exception of the most general of times, i.e., 'It does not occur on you in the present and future times except tomorrow', so the mentioned description is void. His statement (this is problematic etc.) I say: It is also problematic that which will come after a leaf and a half: that if he said 'you are divorced today if tomorrow comes', she is not divorced except at dawn, so the immediate is suspended due to the connection of the first modifier with the second.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage addresses nuances in conditional divorce with time. It first distinguishes between two formulations: 'If you wish, you are divorced tomorrow' – here the wife's option to choose divorce is immediate; she can decide now and the divorce will occur tomorrow. In contrast, 'You are divorced tomorrow if you wish' – her option is only valid tomorrow itself. The second part presents a problematic case from al-Zahiriyya: if a man says 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house', according to the apparent wording, 'tomorrow' is considered void because the divorce is suspended on entry, which could occur at any time. The wife would be divorced whenever she enters. However, this is problematic because it renders the specification of 'tomorrow' meaningless. Al-Maqdisi suggests that it is possible to interpret 'tomorrow' as a genuine restriction: the divorce only occurs tomorrow if she enters, but if she enters today, no divorce. This preserves the wording. The author then adds another problematic case: 'You are divorced today if tomorrow comes' – the divorce is tied to dawn of tomorrow, although 'today' seems precluded. This shows that immediate divorce can be suspended by connecting the modifier to the future.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'a): The wife's will or choice to trigger divorce in a conditional phrase like 'if you wish'.
لغو (laghw): Void or meaningless speech; a word that is legally disregarded because it adds no effective condition.
تخصيص (takhsis): Restriction; limiting a general ruling to a specific instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for 'You are divorced tomorrow if you wish' (أنت طالق غداً إن شئت)?
A) The wife's option is immediate.
B) The wife's option is on the day of tomorrow.
C) The option is invalid.
D) The divorce is immediate.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strives to preserve the meaning of each word in a divorce pronouncement; a time specification is only void if it cannot be given any coherent interpretation.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Talaq Attached to a Time or Action: Idafa vs Ta‛liq
Defining rules for talaq conditional on time or action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for talaq attached to a time or action and apply it to determine when divorce occurs.

Core Idea
If talaq is attached to a time, it is delayed; if time is attached to talaq, it occurs immediately.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the critical distinction between attaching divorce to a time (idafa) and merely describing it with a time. Understanding this difference is essential for determining when a conditional divorce actually takes effect. The Hanafi school provides precise rules based on the wording used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording type
Examine the phrase the husband uttered. Determine whether the divorce is attached to a specific time (e.g., 'You are divorced tomorrow') or the time is attached to the divorce (e.g., 'Tomorrow you are divorced' or 'After it the day of al-Adha').
The preposition and word order matter: 'after the day' attaches talaq to time; 'after it the day' attaches time to talaq.
2
Classify as idafa of talaq to time or time to talaq
If the wording makes the time a description of the divorce (talaq mudaf ila waqt), the divorce occurs only when that time arrives. If the wording makes the divorce a description of the time (waqt mudaf ila talaq), the divorce occurs immediately because the attachment is invalid.
Hanafis consider it impossible to attach an indivisible time to a divisible act like divorce.
3
Apply the rule
For idafa of talaq to time, delay until the specified time. For idafa of time to talaq, immediate divorce. This applies to actions as well: if suspended on one of two actions, the earlier action triggers it.
When both time and action are mentioned, use whichever comes first (al-Ziyadat rule).
4
Check for exceptions
If the description is impossible (e.g., 'a divorce that does not fall except tomorrow'), it is canceled and talaq is immediate. If the description is valid (e.g., 'falls upon you tomorrow'), it is effective and talaq is delayed.
The principle: descriptions that are possible are honored; impossible ones are ignored.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والمضاف إلى أحد الوقتين كقوله غداً أو بعد غد طلقت بعد غد، ولو علق بأحد الفعلين ينزل عند أولهما، والمعلق بفعل أو وقت يقع بأيهما سبق. وفي الزيادات إن وجد الفعل أولاً يقع ولا ينظر وجود الوقت، وإن وجد الوقت أولاً لا يقع ما لم يوجد الفعل ا هـ. وفيها من فصل الاستثناء: أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا واحدة غداً أو إن كلمت فلاناً تعلق ثنتان لمجيء الغد وكلام فلان اهـ. وفي المحيط: ولو قال أنت طالق تطليقة تقع عليك غداً تطلق حين يطلع الفجر فإنه وصف التطليقة بما تتصف به فإنها تتصف بالوقوع غداً بأن كانت مضافة إلى الغد فلا تقع بدون ذلك الوصف، ولو قال تطليقة لا تقع إلا غداً طلقت للحال لأنه وصفها بما لا تتصف به إذ ليس من الطلاق ما لا يقع إلا في الغد بل يتصور وقوعه حالاً واستقبالاً فلغى ذكر الوصف فبقي مرسلاً كما لو قال أنت طالق تطليقة تصير أو تصبح غداً، ولو قال أنت طالق بعد يوم الأضحى تطلق حين يمضي اليوم لأن البعدية صفة للطلاق لما بينا فصار الطلاق مضافاً إلى ما بعد يوم الأضحى فلم يقع قبله، ولو قال بعدها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال لأن البعدية صفة لليوم فيتأخر اليوم عن الطلاق فبقي الطلاق مرسلاً غير مضاف. ولو مع يوم الأضحى طلقت حين يطلع فجره لأن «مع» للقرآن فقد جعل الوقوع مقارناً ليوم الأضحى، ولو قال معها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال لأن حرف مع» هنا دخلت على الوقت فصار مضيفاً الوقت إلى الطلاق، وإضافة الوقت إلى الطلاق باطل لأنه مما لا يتجزى فيبقى الطلاق مرسلاً كما لو قال أنت طالق قبلها يوم الأضحى طلقت للحال ا هـ. وفي الذخيرة: الحاصل أن الطلاق إذا أضيف إلى وقت لا يقع ما لم يجيء ذلك الوقت، وإن أضيف الوقت إلى الطلاق وقع للحال وتوضيحه فيها، وقيد بقوله «غداً» لأنه لو قال أنت طالق لا طلقت الساعة واحدة وفي الغد أخرى. كذا في المحيط معزياً إلى أبي يوسف.
He said: That which is attached to one of two times, like his saying 'tomorrow' or 'the day after tomorrow', she is divorced after tomorrow. And if he suspends it by one of two actions, it takes effect at the earlier of them. And that which is suspended by an action or a time occurs by whichever comes first. In al-Ziyadat: if the action occurs first, it takes effect and the arrival of the time is not considered; if the time occurs first, it does not take effect unless the action occurs. End quote. And in it (al-Ziyadat) from the chapter on exception: 'You are divorced three except one tomorrow, or if you speak to so-and-so' – two (divorces) are tied to the coming of tomorrow and speaking to so-and-so. End quote. In al-Muhit: 'If he said: 'You are divorced once, it falls upon you tomorrow' – she is divorced at the break of dawn, because he described the divorce with what it is characterized by; she is characterized by falling tomorrow by being attached to tomorrow, so it does not fall without that description. And if he said: 'a divorce that does not fall except tomorrow' – she is divorced immediately, because he described it with what it is not characterized by, since there is no divorce that does not fall except tomorrow; rather it can be imagined falling now and in the future, so the mention of the description is canceled and it remains unbound, just as if he said: 'You are divorced once that becomes or becomes (something) tomorrow'. And if he said: 'You are divorced after the day of al-Adha' – she is divorced when the day passes, because 'after-ness' is a description of the divorce as we explained, so the divorce becomes attached to after the day of al-Adha, so it does not occur before it. And if he said: 'after it the day of al-Adha' – she is divorced immediately, because 'after-ness' is a description of the day, so the day is delayed from the divorce, so the divorce remains unbound, not attached. And if he said: 'together with the day of al-Adha' – she is divorced at the break of its dawn, because 'ma'a' (with) indicates association, so he made the occurrence concurrent with the day of al-Adha. And if he said: 'together with it the day of al-Adha' – she is divorced immediately, because the letter 'ma'a' here entered on the time, so he attached the time to the divorce, and attaching the time to the divorce is invalid because it is indivisible, so the divorce remains unbound, just as if he said: 'You are divorced before it the day of al-Adha' – she is divorced immediately. End quote. In al-Dhakhira: The conclusion is that if divorce is added to a time, it does not occur until that time comes; and if the time is added to the divorce, it occurs immediately, and its clarification is in it. And he restricted it by his saying 'tomorrow' because if he said: 'You are divorced' – she would be divorced immediately once and another in the future. Thus in al-Muhit, attributed to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on talaq that is attached (mudaf) to a time or action. The author begins by stating the basic rule: when talaq is attached to one of two times (e.g., 'tomorrow' or 'the day after tomorrow'), it falls at the later time. When suspended on one of two actions, it falls at the earlier action. If suspended on both a time and an action, it falls by whichever comes first. The text then quotes al-Ziyadat for a nuance: if the action occurs first, the arrival of the time is irrelevant; but if the time occurs first, the talaq does not fall until the action occurs. Next, from the chapter on exception: if a man says 'You are divorced three except one tomorrow, or if you speak to so-and-so', two divorces are tied to the coming of tomorrow and speaking to so-and-so. Al-Muhit provides several examples: if one says 'You are divorced once that falls upon you tomorrow', she is divorced at dawn because the description is valid; but if he says 'a divorce that does not fall except tomorrow', she is divorced immediately because that description is impossible (talaq can occur now). Similarly, 'after the day of al-Adha' attaches talaq to after the day, so it falls when the day ends; but 'after it the day of al-Adha' makes 'after-ness' a description of the day, so the day is delayed and talaq is immediate. 'Together with the day of al-Adha' makes talaq concurrent with the day (i.e., at dawn); but 'together with it the day of al-Adha' is invalid, so talaq is immediate. The conclusion from al-Dhakhira is clear: if talaq is added to time, it is delayed; if time is added to talaq, it is immediate. The final note from Abu Yusuf distinguishes between saying 'You are divorced' (immediate) and adding tomorrow.

Key Terms
المضاف إلى أحد الوقتين (al-mudaf ila ahad al-waqtayn): Divorce that is attached to one of two specified times, e.g., 'tomorrow' or 'the day after tomorrow'.
المعلق (al-mu'allaq): Divorce that is suspended on a condition (action or time).
غداً (ghadan): Tomorrow; used as a specific time for talaq attachment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wife 'You are divorced after the day of al-Adha', when does the divorce occur?
A) Immediately upon saying it
B) At the end of the day of al-Adha
C) At the beginning of the day of al-Adha
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
The key distinction in Hanafi talaq attachment is whether the time is a description of the divorce or the divorce is a description of the time.

Conflict Between Time and Condition in Talaq: When Does the Time Mention Get Cancelled?
Resolving conflict between time and action in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a time expression is cancelled by a following condition in talaq and state the Hanafi principle for resolving the conflict.

Core Idea
In Hanafi talaq, a time mention is cancelled if a condition follows it; the later element prevails.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens when a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house'? Does the talaq fall only tomorrow if she enters, or at any moment she enters, even before tomorrow? The Hanafi masters debate whether the time 'tomorrow' is operative or cancelled. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the final relied-upon principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two elements
In the statement 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house', two elements are present: a time ('tomorrow') and a condition ('if you enter the house'). Recognize that both are included in the utterance.
The time is an example of al-taqyid bi al-waqt; the condition is an example of ta'liq.
2
Understand the default Hanafi view
According to al-Zahiriyya, the mention of 'tomorrow' is cancelled (yalghu) and the talaq is tied solely to the condition of entry. Thus, if she enters at any time, she is divorced.
This view treats the condition as overriding the time.
3
Consider al-Maqdisi's objection
Al-Maqdisi argues that describing talaq as only falling tomorrow is a possible and permissible restriction (qasr). Therefore, the description should be preserved to avoid cancellation of the wording.
He relies on the permissibility of qasr and the principle of صوناً له عن الإلغاء.
4
Follow the author's critique
The author points out inconsistency: if the time were preserved, it would conflict with another ruling where 'You are divorced today if tomorrow comes' delays talaq to dawn. The final statement in the passage clarifies the principle.
The principle is: 'التقييد بالوقت إنما يصح إذا لم يأت بعده تعليق'.
5
Apply the principle
When a time (idafa) and a condition (ta'liq) conflict, the latter element in the utterance takes precedence. Since the condition comes after the time in the statement, the condition prevails and the time is cancelled.
The mufta bihi view is that the time is cancelled; the talaq is conditional on entry at any time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية: إن شئت فأنت طالق غداً فالمشيئة إليها للحال بخلاف أنت طالق غداً إن شئت فإن المشيئة إليها في الغد. وفي الظهيرية: لو قال رجل لامرأته أنت طالق غداً إذا دخلت الدار يلغو ذكر الغد فيتعلق الطلاق بدخول الدار حتى لو دخلت في أي وقت كان طلقت وهذا مشكل، فإنه قوله (إذا ليس من الطلاق ما لا يقع إلا في الغد الخ قال المقدسي في شرحه: فيه بحث لأن كون الطلقة لا تقع إلا غداً وصف ممكن لها بالنسبة إلى ما قبله إذا أضيفت إليه أو علقت بمجيئه والقصر شائع سائغ فليحمل عليه صوناً له عن الإلغاء والله سبحانه أعلم. اهـ. ويتلخص من كلامه أنه لا يقع عليه وفي الحال ديانة إذا أراد التخصيص وإلا فظاهر الكلام لغو كما قالوا لأن الاستثناء أعم الأوقات أي لا تقع عليك في الأوقات الحالة والمستقبلة إلا في الغد فيلغو الوصف المذكور. قوله (وهذا مشكل الخ) أقول: ويشكل عليه أيضاً ما سيأتي بعد ورقة ونصف من أنه إذا قال أنت طالق اليوم إذا جاء غد لا تطلق إلا بطلوع الفجر فتوقف المنجز لاتصال مغير الأول بالآخر. بل غداً من إذا ألغى ذكر الغد يصير فاصلاً الشرط والجزاء فوجب أن يتجزى الجزاء، ولو قدم بين الشرط وقال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق غداً يتعلق طلاق الغد بالدخول ا هـ. وبه علم أن التقييد بالوقت إنما يصح إذا لم يأت بعده تعليق لتعارض الإضافة والتعليق فيترجح المتأخر.
And in al-Bazzaziyya: 'If you wish, you are divorced tomorrow' – the option is hers for the present, unlike 'You are divorced tomorrow if you wish' – the option is hers for tomorrow. And in al-Zahiriyya: If a man said to his wife 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house' – the mention of 'tomorrow' is canceled and the talaq is tied to entering the house, so that if she enters at any time, she is divorced. And this is problematic, because his saying 'If' (is contradicted by the principle that) there is no divorce that does not occur except tomorrow, etc. Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: There is discussion in it because the fact that the divorce does not occur except tomorrow is a possible description for it in relation to what comes before if it is added to it or suspended on its coming, and restriction is common and permissible, so it should be taken as such to preserve it from cancellation, and Allah knows best. End quote. And it is deduced from his statement that it does not occur on her, and in the present time in religious court if he intended specification, otherwise the apparent speech is cancelled as they said because the exception is the broadest of times, meaning it does not occur on you in present and future times except tomorrow, so the mentioned description is cancelled. His statement (And this is problematic, etc.) I say: It is also problematic in light of what will come after a leaf and a half, that if he says 'You are divorced today if tomorrow comes' she is not divorced except at the break of dawn, so the immediate is suspended due to the connection of the changer of the first with the other. Rather, 'tomorrow' from if the mention of 'tomorrow' is cancelled, it becomes a separator between the condition and the result, so the result must be divisible, and if he gives precedence between the condition and says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced tomorrow' – the talaq of tomorrow is tied to entry. End quote. And by this it is known that restriction to time is only valid if no suspension comes after it, because of the conflict between attachment and suspension, and the latter is given precedence.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage deals with conflicts between time and action conditions in talaq. The first part from al-Bazzaziyya distinguishes between 'If you wish, you are divorced tomorrow' (option in the present) and 'You are divorced tomorrow if you wish' (option in the future). Then al-Zahiriyya presents a problematic case: 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house.' The apparent ruling is that 'tomorrow' is cancelled because the condition (entry) is linked to the talaq, making the talaq conditional on entry at any time. But al-Maqdisi objects: describing talaq as only falling tomorrow is possible, so it should be preserved. The author summarises: in religious court (diyanan) it depends on intention; otherwise, the description is cancelled. The author then adds his own critique: this case conflicts with another ruling where 'You are divorced today if tomorrow comes' delays talaq to dawn. The key principle emerges: when a time attachment (idafa) conflicts with a condition (ta'liq), the latter (the condition) takes precedence, especially if it occurs later in the utterance.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'a): Option or will given to the wife in conditional talaq expressions.
يلغو (yalghu): Is cancelled; the mention of a time is disregarded when it conflicts with a condition.
التقييد بالوقت (al-taqyid bi al-waqt): Restriction of talaq to a specific time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case 'You are divorced tomorrow if you enter the house', which element takes precedence according to the final ruling in this passage?
A) The time (tomorrow)
B) The condition (entry)
C) Both are valid simultaneously
D) Neither, the statement is void

Key Takeaway
When a condition follows a time expression in talaq, the condition cancels the time, and the talaq is tied only to the condition.

Effect of 'Fi' on Temporal Expressions in Talaq
Distinguishing between 'fi' and its absence in talaq time expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how the presence or absence of 'fi' affects talaq temporal expressions and apply it to examples like 'every day' vs 'in every day'.

Core Idea
The particle 'fi' generalizes time in talaq expressions, turning a single event into a potential for multiple occurrences.

In Hanafi law, the wording of a talaq is determinative. This passage examines how the preposition 'fi' (in) changes the meaning of temporal phrases, affecting whether a divorce occurs once or repeatedly. Understanding this distinction is crucial for judges and muftis who evaluate the husband's intention in talaq statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic principle
Recognize that 'fi' when attached to a time expression makes it a 'container' (zarf) that can include any part of that time, whereas without 'fi' the expression refers to the entire duration.
This is based on the difference between 'fasted a year' (whole year) and 'fasted in a year' (any part).
2
Apply to the baseline case
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'You are divorced in 'asr' intending the end of the day, his intention is valid in court; without 'fi', the intention is not accepted judicially. Note the disagreement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
The Sahibayn reject intention in both cases.
3
Extend to 'every day' vs 'in every day'
Without 'fi', 'every day' yields one divorce; with 'fi', it yields three divorces (one each day). This is agreed upon among the three, but Zufar differs.
Zufar rules that even without 'fi', three divorces occur over three days.
4
Consider the role of intention
When 'fi' is present, intention to specify a part is valid; when absent, such intention is not accepted judicially, but is accepted religiously (diyanan).
The passage also discusses examples like 'in Sha'ban' and 'every Friday'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونية العصر تصح في الثاني أي نية آخر النهار تصح مع ذكر كلمة «في» ولا تصح عند حذفها قضاء عند أبي حنيفة وقالا: لا تصح في الثاني كالأول والفرق له عموم متعلقها بدخولها مقدرة لا ملفوظة لغة للفرق بين صمت سنة وفي سنة لغة، وكذا شرعاً فيما لو حلف ليصو من عمره فإنه يتناول جميع عمره حتى لا يبر في يمينه إلا بصوم جميع العمر. ولو قال لأصومن في عمري فإنه يتناول ساعة من عمره حتى لو صام ساعة بر في يمينه كما في المعراج، فنية جزء من الزمان مع ذكرها نية الحقيقة لأن ذلك الجزء من أفراد المتواطئ ومع حذفها نية تخصيص العام فلا يصدق قضاء، وإنما يتعين أول أجزائه مع عدمها لعدم المزاحم. وجعلهم لفظة «غد» عاماً. كونه نكرة في الإثبات لتنزيل الأجزاء منزلة الأفراد وكان يكفيهم أن يقال إنه خلاف الظاهر وفيه تخفيف على نفسه. وهذا بخلاف ما لا يتجزى الزمان في حقه فإنه لا فرق فيه بين الحذف والإثبات كصمت يوم الجمعة وفي يوم الجمعة. قيدنا بكونه قضاء لأنه يصدق ديانة فيهما اتفاقاً واليوم والشهر ووقت العصر كالغد فيهما. ومثل قوله «في غد» قوله في شعبان مثلاً فإذا قال أنت طالق في شعبان فإن لم تكن له نية طلقت حين تغيب الشمس من آخر يوم من رجب، وإن نوى آخر يوم من شعبان فهو على الخلاف. ومما تفرع على حذف «في» وإثباتها لو قال أنت طالق كل يوم يقع واحدة عند الثلاثة. وقال زفر: تقع ثلاث في ثلاثة أيام. ولو قال في كل يوم طلقت ثلاثاً في كل يوم واحدة إجماعاً كما لو قال عند كل يوم أو كلما مضى يوم. والفرق لنا أن «في» للظرف والزمان إنما هو ظرف من حيث الوقوع فيلزم من كل يوم فيه وقوع تعدد الواقع بخلاف كون كل يوم فيه الاتصاف بالواقع، فلو نوى أن تطلق كل يوم تطليقة أخرى صحت نيته. وفي الخلاصة: أنت طالق مع كل يوم تطليقة فإنها تطلق ثلاثاً ساعة حلف وفي التتمة: أنت طالق رأس كل شهر تطلق ثلاثاً رأس كل شهر واحدة، ولو قال أنت طالق رأس كل شهر طلقت في لأن في الأول بينهما فصل في الوقوع ولا كذلك في الثاني، ولو قال أنت طالق واحدة كل جمعة فإن كانت نيته على كل يوم جمعة فهي طالق في كل يوم جمعة حتى تبين بثلاث، وإن كانت نيته على كل جمعة تمر بأيامها على الدهر فهي طالق واحدة، وإن لم يكن له نية قوله (وفي الخلاصة أنت طالق مع كل يوم تطليقة أقول : ليس في عبارة الخلاصة لفظة «يوم» بل عبارتها أنت طالق مع كل تطليقة وسينقلها المؤلف هكذا عن البزازية قبيل فصل الطلاق قبل الدخول قوله (وفي التتمة أنت طالق رأس كل شهر الخ الذي رأيته في الذخيرة وكذا في الهندية عن
And the intention of 'asr (afternoon) is valid in the second, meaning the intention of the end of the day is valid when the word 'fi' (in) is mentioned, and it is not valid when it is omitted, in judicial ruling according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not valid in the second like the first. The difference for him (Abu Hanifa) is the generality of its applicability due to it being implied (muqaddara) not uttered (maflutha) in language, for the linguistic difference between 'I fasted a year' and 'I fasted in a year'. And likewise in legal terms, as when one swears 'I will fast during my lifetime' – it encompasses his entire lifetime such that he does not fulfill his oath except by fasting the entire lifetime. And if he said 'I will fast in my lifetime' – it encompasses an hour of his lifetime, such that if he fasts an hour, he fulfills his oath, as in al-Mi'raj. So the intention of a part of time with its mention is an intention of reality because that part is among the individuals of the equivocal; and with its omission, it is an intention of specification of the general, so it is not accepted in judicial ruling. And rather the first part becomes specified in its absence due to the absence of a competing factor. And they made the word 'tomorrow' general, being an indefinite in the affirmative, by treating the parts as individuals. It would have been sufficient for them to say it is contrary to the apparent, and there is leniency in it. And this is unlike what is indivisible time in its regard, for there is no difference between omission and inclusion, like 'I fasted Friday' and 'I fasted on Friday'. We restrict it to being in judicial ruling because it is accepted in religious conscience (diyanan) by agreement. And the day, month, and time of 'asr are like 'tomorrow' in both. And like his saying 'in tomorrow' is his saying 'in Sha'ban', for example. So if he says 'You are divorced in Sha'ban', if he has no intention, she is divorced when the sun sets on the last day of Rajab; and if he intends the last day of Sha'ban, there is a dispute. And from the branch issues on omission and inclusion of 'fi': if he says 'You are divorced every day' – one (divorce) falls according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Zufar said: three fall in three days. And if he says 'in every day' – she is divorced three, one in every day, by consensus, just as if he said 'at every day' or 'whenever a day passes'. The difference for us is that 'fi' indicates containment, and time is only a container in terms of occurrence, so from 'every day' with 'fi' necessitates multiplicity of occurrence, unlike the case of 'every day' without 'fi' which indicates description of occurrence. So if he intends that she be divorced every day a divorce, his intention is valid. In al-Khulasa: 'You are divorced with every day a divorce' – she is divorced three at the moment of his oath. And in al-Tatimma: 'You are divorced at the beginning of every month' – she is divorced three, one at the beginning of each month. And if he said 'You are divorced at the beginning of every month' – she is divorced... because in the first there is separation in occurrence between them, and not so in the second. And if he said 'You are divorced one every Friday' – if his intention was every Friday day, she is divorced every Friday until she completes three; if his intention was every Friday as a period of days forever, she is divorced one; and if he had no intention... His statement (And in al-Khulasa: 'You are divorced with every day a divorce') – I say: There is no word 'day' in the phrasing of al-Khulasa; rather its wording is 'You are divorced with every divorce', and the author will quote it thus from al-Bazzaziyya before the section on talaq before consummation. His statement (And in al-Tatimma: 'You are divorced at the beginning of every month, etc.) – what I saw in al-Dhakhira, and likewise in al-Hindiyya from...
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage explores how the particle 'fi' (in) changes the meaning of temporal expressions in talaq. It begins with an example about 'intention of 'asr' (afternoon). Abu Hanifa rules: if one says 'in 'asr' (e.g., 'You are divorced in the afternoon'), intending the end of the day, that intention is valid in court (qada'an); but if he omits 'fi' and says 'asr' only, his intention is not accepted judicially. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, saying intention is not accepted in either case. The reason for Abu Hanifa's distinction is linguistic: 'fi' generalizes, so its inclusion makes the expression potentially cover any part of the time, while its absence makes it refer to the entire duration. Analogies from oaths (fasting) illustrate this. The text then applies this to 'tomorrow' and 'Sha'ban'. Next, it contrasts expressions like 'every day' (which yields one divorce) with 'in every day' (which yields three divorces, one each day) – the latter is agreed upon (including Zufar). The rationale: 'fi' indicates that each day contains a divorce, implying multiplicity; without 'fi', it describes the occurrence as a single event over all days. The passage ends with examples from al-Khulasa and al-Tatimma about 'with every day' and 'at the beginning of every month', including distinctions based on phrasing and intention.

Key Terms
في (fi): The preposition 'in' that generalizes the time expression, making it a container for potential occurrence.
نية العصر (niyyat al-'asr): Intention to specify the afternoon time in a talaq expression.
حذف (hadhf): Omission of the particle 'fi' from a temporal phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man says 'You are divorced in Sha'ban' without any intention, when does the divorce occur?
A) At the beginning of Sha'ban
B) At the end of Rajab
C) At the end of Sha'ban
D) Immediately

Key Takeaway
The presence of 'fi' in talaq temporal expressions generalizes the time, potentially causing multiple divorces, while its absence limits it to a single occurrence.

Effect of the Word 'Fi' in Temporal Talaq Conditions
Analyzing the difference between 'غداً' and 'في غد' in conditional talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of the word 'في' in temporal conditions for talaq and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When 'في' is used, time is a container allowing partial intention; without 'في', the default is the first part.

In Hanafi fiqh, the precise wording of a conditional divorce can change the legal outcome. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores how the preposition 'fi' transforms the scope of time from an indivisible whole to a divisible container, affecting the validity of intending a specific part. We will examine the ruling, the intra-Hanafi dispute, and the linguistic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional talaq tied to a time period, e.g., 'أنت طالق غداً' vs 'أنت طالق في غد'. The ruling concerns whether the intention to divorce at a specific part of that time is accepted judicially (qada).
2
Distinguish between with and without 'في'
Abu Hanifa holds that with 'في', the time is a container (zarf), so intention of a part is valid judicially. Without 'في', the default is the first part, and intention is not accepted.
The key is the linguistic difference between 'صمت سنة' (whole year) and 'صمت في سنة' (part of a year).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree: they hold that intention of a part is invalid judicially in both cases, regardless of 'في'.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Trace the dalil
The proof is a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) based on the linguistic distinction between the two expressions. The presence of 'في' indicates inclusion of part; its absence indicates totality.
The same principle applies in oaths (e.g., 'ليصومن عمره' vs 'ليصومن في عمره').
5
Evaluate the tarjih and mufta bihi
The later Hanafi masters (e.g., al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah) prefer Abu Hanifa's view, which is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) today.
The ruling is qada only; diyanah (religiously) the intention is accepted in both cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ألغى ذكر الغد يصير فاصلاً الشرط والجزاء فوجب أن يتجزى الجزاء، ولو قدم بين الشرط وقال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق غداً يتعلق طلاق الغد بالدخول ا هـ. وبه علم أن التقييد بالوقت إنما يصح إذا لم يأت بعده تعليق لتعارض الإضافة والتعليق فيترجح المتأخر . قوله : (ونية العصر تصح في الثاني) أي نية آخر النهار تصح مع ذكر كلمة «في» ولا تصح عند حذفها قضاء عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : لا تصح في الثاني كالأول والفرق له عموم متعلقها بدخولها مقدرة لا ملفوظة لغة للفرق بين صمت سنة وفي سنة لغة، وكذا شرعاً فيما لو حلف ليصو من عمره فإنه يتناول جميع عمره حتى لا يبر في يمينه إلا بصوم جميع العمر . ولو قال لأصومن في عمري فإنه يتناول ساعة من عمره حتى لو صام ساعة بر في يمينه كما في المعراج، فنية جزء من الزمان مع ذكرها نية الحقيقة لأن ذلك الجزء من أفراد المتواطئ ومع حذفها نية تخصيص العام فلا يصدق قضاء، وإنما يتعين أول أجزائه مع عدمها لعدم المزاحم . وجعلهم لفظة «غد» عاماً. كونه نكرة في الإثبات لتنزيل الأجزاء منزلة الأفراد وكان يكفيهم أن يقال إنه خلاف الظاهر وفيه تخفيف على نفسه. وهذا بخلاف ما لا يتجزى الزمان في حقه فإنه لا فرق فيه بين الحذف والإثبات كصمت يوم الجمعة وفي يوم الجمعة. قيدنا بكونه قضاء لأنه يصدق ديانة فيهما اتفاقاً واليوم والشهر ووقت العصر كالغد فيهما.
If the mention of 'tomorrow' is omitted, it becomes a separator between condition and consequence, so the consequence must be divisible. And if he delayed the condition and said 'If you enter the house, you are divorced tomorrow', the divorce of tomorrow is attached to entry. This concludes. And by this it is known that restriction by time is only valid if no condition comes after it because of conflict between specification and conditioning, and the later is given precedence. His statement: (And intention of 'asr is valid in the second) meaning intention of the end of the day is valid when the word 'fi' is mentioned, and not valid when omitted judicially according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: it is not valid in the second like the first. The difference for him is the generality of its attachment: with its entrant, it is estimated, not uttered, linguistically due to the difference between 'I fasted a year' and 'I fasted in a year' linguistically, and likewise legally in the case where he swore to fast from his life, then it includes his entire life so he does not fulfill his oath except by fasting all his life. And if he said 'I will fast in my life', it includes an hour of his life so if he fasts an hour he fulfills his oath, as in al-Mi'raj. Thus intending a part of time with mentioning it is an intention of reality because that part is an individual of a univocal term, and with omitting it, it is an intention of specifying the general, so it is not accepted judicially. And only its first part is determined in its absence due to lack of a competing factor. And they made the word 'tomorrow' general because it is an indefinite in positive context, treating the parts as individuals, and it would have sufficed to say it is contrary to the apparent but there is leniency for himself. And this differs from what time is indivisible in its regard, where there is no difference between omission and mention, like 'I fasted Friday' and 'on Friday'. We restricted it to being judicial because he is believed religiously in both, and the day, month, and time of 'asr are like 'tomorrow' in them.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding the conditioning of talaq on time. It distinguishes between two expressions: 'أنت طالق غداً' (you are divorced tomorrow) and 'أنت طالق في غد' (you are divorced in tomorrow). According to Abu Hanifa, when 'في' is used, the time is treated as a container (zarf), so the divorce occurs at any time within that container. The intention of a specific part of the time (like the end of the day) is valid in qada (judicially) only when 'في' is present. Without 'في', the default is the first part of the time. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and say it is the same in both cases. The proof is drawn from the linguistic difference between 'صمت سنة' (I fasted a year) meaning the whole year vs 'صمت في سنة' (I fasted in a year) meaning part of it. This is an example of istihsan hidden analogy.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Judicial ruling, as opposed to religious truth (diyanah). In this context, the intention is accepted in court only if 'في' is present.
في (fi): A preposition meaning 'in', considered a container (zarf) that affects the scope of time.
غد (ghad): Tomorrow, but considered a general term since it is indefinite in positive context, making it inclusive of all parts of the next day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man says 'أنت طالق غداً' (without 'في'), the divorce is considered to occur at which part of the day judicially?
A) At the intended part if he intended it
B) At the beginning of the day
C) At the end of the day
D) The divorce is invalid

Key Takeaway
The presence of 'في' in a temporal condition for talaq changes the time from a single unit to a divisible container, allowing judicial acceptance of intending a specific part.

Ruling on Zihar with Temporal Expressions like 'Every Day'
Ruling on zihar with temporal specifications such as 'every day' and 'in every day'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the zihar rulings when the husband says 'every day' (كل يوم) vs 'in every day' (في كل يوم) and state the implications for the prohibition and expiation.

Core Idea
The phrase 'every day' creates one continuous zihar; 'in every day' creates a new zihar each day.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from al-Muhit that explains how temporal wording in zihar affects whether the prohibition is continuous or renewed daily. The key linguistic distinction between 'كل يوم' and 'في كل يوم' changes the legal effect, impacting how many expiations are required and when the husband may approach his wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key phrases
Look for the phrases 'كل يوم' (every day) and 'في كل يوم' (in every day) in the source text. Note the presence or absence of the preposition 'في'.
The preposition changes the meaning from a single unit to repetition.
2
Understand the ruling for 'كل يوم'
The passage states that saying 'كل يوم' results in one zihar only. The husband may not approach his wife night or day until he expiates.
This is similar to saying 'you are divorced every day' – it's a single divorce.
3
Understand the ruling for 'في كل يوم'
With 'في كل يوم', each day is a separate zihar. The zihar is nullified at night and returns the next day. Expiating on a day removes the prohibition only for that day.
The preposition 'في' treats each day as an independent unit.
4
Apply to similar phrases
The passage also discusses 'today and whenever a day comes' (اليوم وكلما جاء يوم). Here, the zihar applies only during daytime and is nullified at night, allowing approach at night. For 'كلما' it is a future zihar at each dawn requiring separate expiation.
The word 'كلما' triggers a perpetual, independent zihar each day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو قال أنت علي كظهر أمي كل يوم كان ظهاراً واحداً فلا يقربها ليلاً ولا نهاراً حتى يكفر كما لو قال أنت طالق كل يوم، ولو قال في كل يوم رد كل يوم بالظهار، فإذا جاء الليل بطل الظهار وعاد من الغد لأن مظاهراً في كل يوم لأنه أفرد الظهار يتوقت فإذا مضى الوقت بطل الظهار. وإن كفر في كل فله أن يقربها في ذلك اليوم لأن الظهار قد ارتفع بالتكفير وعاد من الغد، ولو قال أنت علي كظهر أمي اليوم وكلما جاء يوم كان مظاهراً اليوم فإذا جاء الليل بطل، وله أن يقربها ليلاً لأنه وقته باليوم فإذا جاء الغد صار مظاهراً ولا يقربها ليلاً ولا نهاراً حتى يكفر، وكذلك في كل يوم هو مظاهر ظهاراً مستقبلاً عند طلوع الفجر لا يبطله إلا كفارة على حدة وهو مرسل فيقع مؤبداً !
And in al-Muhit: If he said, 'You are to me like the back of my mother every day,' it is one zihar, so he may not approach her night or day until he expiates, just as if he said 'You are divorced every day.' And if he said 'in every day,' every day is repeated with zihar. When night comes, the zihar is nullified and returns the next day because he made zihar in every day – since he specified zihar with a time, when the time passes the zihar is nullified. And if he expiates in every [day], he may approach her on that day because the zihar has been removed by expiation, and it returns the next day. If he said, 'You are to me like the back of my mother today, and whenever a day comes,' he is a muzahir today; when night comes it is nullified, and he may approach her at night because he limited it to daytime; when the next day comes he becomes a muzahir and may not approach her night or day until he expiates. Similarly, each day he is a muzahir, a future zihar at dawn; nothing nullifies it except a separate expiation, and it is mursal (unrestricted) so it occurs perpetually!
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit discusses the effect of temporal wording in zihar (a form of pre-Islamic divorce-like oath). The key distinction is between saying 'every day' (كل يوم) and 'in every day' (في كل يوم). When one says 'every day', it is considered a single zihar that does not renew daily; the prohibition continues until expiation. When one says 'in every day', each day is a separate zihar that is nullified at the end of the day and renews the next day unless expiation has been made. Similarly, if one specifies 'today' and uses the word 'whenever a day comes' (كلما جاء يوم), the zihar applies only during the day and is nullified at night. The passage ends by noting that a zihar expressed with the word 'كلما' (whenever) is considered 'مظاهر ظهاراً مستقبلاً' at each dawn, requiring a separate expiation for each day, and it is 'مرسل' meaning it continues perpetually. This is a detailed application of how temporal phrases affect the continuity of a prohibition.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A form of pre-Islamic divorce-like oath where a husband says to his wife 'You are like my mother's back' (or similar), prohibiting conjugal relations until expiation.
كل يوم (kull yawm): Meaning 'every day'; in this context, it indicates a single continuous zihar that does not renew daily until expiation.
في كل يوم (fi kull yawm): Meaning 'in every day'; in this context, it causes a new zihar each day, nullified at night and renewed the next day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, each day is a new zihar requiring separate expiation.
B) No, it is a single zihar until he expiates.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) If he expiates one day, he can approach her forever.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'كل يوم' and 'في كل يوم' in zihar determines whether the prohibition is continuous or renewed daily, with corresponding implications for expiation.

Ruling on Oaths: 'Every Day' vs 'In Every Day' – Inclusion of Night
Ruling on temporal expressions in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the phrases 'kull yawm' and 'fi kull yawm' in oaths and identify when speaking at night breaks the oath.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'kull yawm' includes the night; 'fi kull yawm' excludes it – the preposition changes the ruling.

Oaths in Hanafi fiqh are sensitive to linguistic nuances. This passage from al-Bazzaziyyah illustrates how a single preposition can determine whether speaking at night violates an oath of silence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly assessing oath violations and issuing accurate fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrase
Determine whether the person said 'kull yawm' (every day) or 'fi kull yawm' (in every day).
The presence of the preposition 'fi' is key.
2
Determine inclusion/exclusion of night
For 'kull yawm', the night is included, so speaking at night breaks the oath. For 'fi kull yawm', the night is excluded, so speaking at night does not break the oath.
This is based on customary usage (urf) according to the Hanafi school.
3
Apply to consecutive days
When specifying days without 'fi' (e.g., 'today, tomorrow, and the day after'), it is one continuous period covering night. With 'fi' before each day, each day is separate and night excluded.
The ruling mirrors the basic distinction.
4
Summarize the rulings
Compile the four cases: (1) kull yawm: night included; (2) fi kull yawm: night excluded; (3) consecutive without fi: continuous; (4) consecutive with fi: separate.
This summary aids quick reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية ويدخل في قوله لا أكلمه كل يوم الليلة حتى لو كلمه في الليل فهو كالكلام بالنهار كما في قوله أيام هذه الجمعة، وفي قوله في كل يوم لا تدخل الليلة حتى لو كلمه في الليل لا يحنث لا يكلمه اليوم وغداً وبعد غد فهذا على كلام واحد ليلاً كان أو نهاراً. ولو قال في اليوم وفي غد وفي بعد غد لا يحنث حتى يكلم في كل يوم سماه ولو كلمه ليلاً لا يحنث في يمينه ا هـ
In al-Bazzaziyyah: In his saying 'I will not speak to him every day' (كل يوم), the night enters into it, so that if he speaks to him at night, it is like speaking during the day, just as in his saying 'the days of this Friday'. In his saying 'in every day' (في كل يوم), the night is not included, so that if he speaks to him at night, he does not break his oath. 'I will not speak to him today, tomorrow, and the day after' – this is one statement, whether night or day. And if he said 'on this day, and tomorrow, and the day after', he does not break his oath until he speaks to him on each day he mentioned; if he speaks to him at night, he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazzaziyyah clarifies the effect of the prepositions in oaths of not speaking. When one swears 'لا أكلمه كل يوم' (I will not speak to him every day), the oath includes both day and night, so speaking at night breaks the oath. However, when one says 'في كل يوم' (in every day), the night is not included, so speaking at night does not break the oath. The distinction is based on whether the time expression is intended as a container (ظرف) or as a repetition of units. Then it gives cases of specifying days: if one says 'اليوم وغداً وبعد غد' (today, tomorrow, and the day after), it is considered one continuous period covering night and day; but if one says 'في اليوم وفي غد وفي بعد غد' (in today, in tomorrow, and in the day after), each day is separate and the oath is only broken by speaking on that specific day; speaking at night does not count. This ruling applies to oaths (يمين) and is based on the linguistic difference in the usage of prepositions.

Key Terms
كل يوم (kull yawm): In oaths, this phrase includes the night as part of the day period, so the oath covers both day and night.
في كل يوم (fi kull yawm): In oaths, this phrase excludes the night; each day is separate and the oath only applies during the daytime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'I will not speak to Zaid every day' (كل يوم). If he speaks to Zaid at night, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the oath includes night.
B) No, because night is separate.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) The oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'في' in an oath of not speaking restricts the scope to daytime, whereas its absence extends it to include night.

Basic Rule for Divorce with Two Times
States the Hanafi rule for choosing the first time when two times are mentioned with 'or'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for talaq when two times are joined by 'or' and apply it to determine the effective time of divorce.

Core Idea
When 'or' connects two divorce times, only the first one counts.

Divorce statements often involve timing. A common construction is saying 'you are divorced today or tomorrow'. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: the first time mentioned is effective, and the second is considered void. This principle is affirmed in authoritative Hanafi texts like al-Muhit and al-Bazzaziyyah.

Guided Path
1
State the basic rule
Identify the principle: when a husband says 'you are divorced [time A] or [time B]', the divorce occurs at the first mentioned time. The second time is not operative.
2
Identify the first time mentioned
Given any statement of talaq with two time phrases connected by 'aw' (or), determine which time is mentioned first. That is the effective time.
For example, 'today tomorrow': first is today; 'tomorrow today': first is tomorrow.
3
Apply to the source examples
In the source text, the rule is exemplified: 'today tomorrow' (اليوم غداً) means divorce today; 'tomorrow today' (غداً اليوم) means divorce tomorrow. Verify this by parsing the phrase.
The text also mentions a separate case about 'before I marry you' which aligns with the same principle.
4
Recognize the authority
Note that this ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and affirmed by later Hanafi authorities such as al-Muhit and al-Bazzaziyyah. It is the mufta bihi position.
No contrary Hanafi view is reported; the rule is settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليوم غداً أو غداً اليوم يعتبر الأول. أنت طالق قبل أن أتزوجك أو أمس ونكحها اليوم كله، ولو قال في هذا اليوم فهو على مجلسها وهو صحيح موافق لقوله أنت طالق غداً أو أنت طالق في الغدا. ما في المحيط. وجزم به في البزازية فلم يخرج عن هذا الأصل.
Today tomorrow or tomorrow today, the first is considered. You are divorced before I marry you or yesterday and he married her today the entire day. And if he said 'in this day' then it is upon her sitting and it is correct, agreeing with his statement 'you are divorced tomorrow' or 'you are divorced in the morning'. What is in al-Muhit. And it is affirmed in al-Bazzaziyyah so it does not exit from this principle.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school. When a husband says 'you are divorced today tomorrow' (using 'or'), the first time mentioned—today—is effective. Similarly, if he says 'tomorrow today', she is divorced tomorrow. An unclear example follows about 'before I marry you' possibly meaning if he says 'you are divorced before I marry you' and then marries her today, the divorce takes effect today? The text then notes that saying 'in this day' refers to the same sitting (majlis), which is consistent. The ruling is affirmed in al-Muhit and al-Bazzaziyyah, indicating it is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). No explicit proof is given here; it is a concise matn statement for memorization.

Key Terms
يعتبر الأول (yu'tabaru al-awwal): The first mentioned time is considered effective for the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'you are divorced tomorrow or today', when does the divorce occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) Today
B) Tomorrow
C) Both today and tomorrow
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when divorce is phrased with two times connected by 'or', only the first mentioned time takes effect.

Talaq — Divorce
